Qada — Judiciary
beginner
10 min
Defining Plaintiff and Defendant in Hanafi Qada
Defines litigants and evidence rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of plaintiff (mudda'i) and defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) and apply the rule that the plaintiff bears the burden of proof while the defendant bears the oath.

Core Idea
The plaintiff brings evidence; the defendant swears an oath.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, identifying the plaintiff and defendant is essential because it determines who must present evidence and who may be required to take an oath. This lesson presents the foundational definitions and the primary proof (hadith) that establishes these rules. The passage also outlines the four key topics of the Book of Claims and the three criteria for judgment.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the definitions
Read the Arabic definitions: al-mudda'i is the one not compelled to litigate if he abandons the claim; al-mudda'a 'alayh is the one compelled to litigate. Understand the rationale: the plaintiff seeks to take property from the defendant, so he can drop the case; the defendant is forced to respond because he is in possession.
Hanafis define by procedural compulsion, not by who asserts a right.
2
Identify the four topics of the Book of Claims
The text lists: how to make a claim, who must bring witnesses, who must take an oath, and how judgment is made. These structure the entire book.
3
Locate the hadith of Ash'ath ibn Qays
The hadith narrates a dispute over a well. The Prophet ruled: 'Shahidaka aw yaminuh' (your witness or his oath). This is the core proof for the rule.
The hadith is in Bukhari and Abu Dawood.
4
Derive the rule from the hadith
The Prophet's statement places the burden of proof on the plaintiff (bring witnesses) and the oath on the defendant (if no witnesses). This is wajib for both.
The Quranic verse (3:77) about false oaths was revealed concerning this incident.
5
Understand the three criteria for judgment
The passage states: (1) witnesses, (2) if none, oath, (3) if still not resolved, physical signs. This is the hierarchy of evidence in Hanafi qada.
When explicit texts are missing, principles and custom are applied.

Source Text

كتاب الدعوى ۱۲۷۳۰) المدعى من لايجبر على الخصومة اذا تركها والمدعى عليه من يجبر على كتاب الدعوى ) ضروری نوے کتاب الدعوی میں چار باتیں ہیں۔ دعوی کس طرح کرے اور کس دعوی کا کیا مطلب ہے۔ دوسری بات ہے کہ کن لوگوں پر گواہ پیش کرنا لازم ہے اور کس طرح گواہ پیش کرے۔ تیسری بات ہے کہ اگر گواہ نہ ہوں تو کن لوگوں پر قسم لازم ہے اور کس طرح قتسم کھلائے۔ اور چوتھی بات یہ ہے کہ کس طرح فیصلہ کرے۔ یوں فیصلے کے معیار تین ہیں۔ گواہ ، وہ نہ ہوں تو قسم کھلانا۔ اور اس سے بھی معاملہ حل نہ ہوتا ہو تو علامات دیکھیں گے اور ان کے ذریعہ پتا چلا ئیں گے کہ یہ چیز کس کی ہونی چاہئے ۔ ان سکھوں کے ثبوت کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ ثم ان الاشعث ابن قيس خرج الينا فقال ما يحدثكم ابو عبد الرحمن ؟ قال محدثناه قال فقال صدق لفى نزلت كانت بيني و بين رجل خصومة في بئر فاختصمنا الى رسول الله الا الله فقال رسول الله الا الله شاهداک او یمینه قلت انه اذا يحلف ولا يبالي فقال رسول الله الا الله من حلف على يمين يستحق بها مالا وهو فيها فاجر لقى الله وهو عليه غضبان ثم انزل الله تصديق ذلك ثم اقتراء هذه الآية ان الذين يشترون بعهد الله وايمانهم ثمنا قليلا اولئك لا خلاق لهم في الآخرة ولا يكلمهم الله ( آیت ۷۷ سورة آل عمران ۳) (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب اذا اختلف الراهن والمرتهن ونحوه فالبينة علی المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیہ ص ۳۲۲ نمبر ۲۵۱۶/۲۵۱۵) ابوداؤد شریف ، باب الرجل حلف علی علمہ فیما غاب عنه ص ۱۵۴ نمبر ۳۶۲۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کس طرح دعوی کرے۔ یہ بھی ہے کہ مدعی پر گواہ لازم ہے اور مدعی علیہ پر قسم ہے۔ اور یہ بھی ہے کہ فیصلہ کس طرح کرے۔ اوت کتاب الدعوی میں بھی مدعی اور مدعی علیہ کا متعین کرنا بعض مرتبہ قرائن پر ہے یا محاورات پر ہے۔ اس لئے ایسے مسئلے کے لئے حدیث یا میں بھی دی اور کا پر یا آثار نہ مل سکے، وہاں اصول اور محاورہ پیش کر دیا گیا ہے۔ (۲۷۳۰) (۱) مدعی وہ ہے جو جھگڑے پر مجبور نہ کیا جاسکے اگر وہ اس کو چھوڑ دے، اور مدعی علیہ وہ ہے جو جھگڑے پر مجبور کیا جائے۔ مدعی اور مدعی علیہ کی بہت سی تعریف کی ہیں ان میں سے ایک تعریف یہ ہے کہ مدعی اس کو کہتے ہیں جو کسی سے مال لینا چاہتا ہو۔ اسی لئے وہ جھگڑا کرنے پر مجبور نہیں کیا جاسکے چاہے تو جھگڑا کر کے مال لے اور چاہے تو جھگڑا چھوڑ دے۔ اور مدعی علیہ اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کے قبضے میں
Book of Claims (Kitaab al-Da'waa). (2730) The plaintiff (al-mudda'i) is the one who is not compelled to litigate if he abandons it, and the defendant (al-mudda'a 'alayh) is the one who is compelled to litigate. (Book of Claims) Necessary matters: In the Book of Claims there are four things: how to make a claim and what each claim means; second: upon whom it is obligatory to bring witnesses and how to bring them; third: if there are no witnesses, upon whom is the oath obligatory and how to administer the oath; and fourth: how to judge. So the criteria for judgment are three: witnesses; if not, then oath; and if the matter is not resolved by that, then signs will be looked at and by them it will be known whose it should be. For proof of these principles, there is this hadith: Then al-Ash'ath ibn Qays came out to us and said, 'What is Abu Abd al-Rahman narrating to you?' He said, 'We narrated such and such.' He said, 'He told the truth. It was revealed concerning me. There was a dispute between me and a man over a well. We referred it to the Messenger of Allah. The Messenger of Allah said, 'Either you have two witnesses or his oath.' I said, 'He would swear and not care.' The Messenger of Allah said, 'Whoever swears an oath to acquire property thereby, and he is false in it, will meet Allah while He is angry with him.' Then Allah revealed the confirmation of that, and he recited this verse: 'Indeed, those who exchange the covenant of Allah and their [own] oaths for a small price, they will have no share in the Hereafter, and Allah will not speak to them...' (Surah Aal-e-Imran 3:77) (Bukhari, Bab idha ikhtalafa al-rahin... hadith 2516/2515; Abu Dawood, hadith 3623). This hadith contains how to make a claim, that the plaintiff must bring witnesses and the defendant must take an oath, and also how to judge. And in the Book of Claims, the identification of plaintiff and defendant is sometimes based on circumstantial evidence or conventions. Therefore, for such issues where no hadith, verse, or athar is found, principles and conventions are presented. (2730) (1) The plaintiff is the one who cannot be compelled to argue if he abandons it, and the defendant is the one who is compelled to argue. There are many definitions of plaintiff and defendant; one of them is that the plaintiff is the one who wants to take property from someone. Therefore, he cannot be compelled to argue; he may either litigate to take the property or abandon the litigation. The defendant is the one in whose possession (the property is).
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a definition and introduction to the Book of Claims (Kitaab al-Da'waa) from a Hanafi fiqh text. It opens with the definition of the litigants: the plaintiff (mudda'i) is one who is not forced to pursue the case if he drops it; the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) is one who is forced to respond. The commentator outlines four main topics of the book: how to claim, who must present evidence, when oaths are required, and how judgment is made. The three standards of proof are: witnesses, oath (when witnesses are absent), and if neither resolves, physical signs. The hadith of Ash'ath ibn Qays is cited as proof: a dispute over a well was brought to the Prophet, who ruled that the plaintiff must produce two witnesses or the defendant takes an oath. The Quranic verse (3:77) about false oaths was revealed concerning this incident. The commentator then notes that determining who is plaintiff and who is defendant sometimes depends on circumstantial evidence or conventional practice; when explicit texts are lacking, principles and custom are applied.

Key Terms
مدعي (mudda'i): The plaintiff: one who makes a claim and is not forced to pursue litigation if he drops it
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant: one against whom a claim is made and who is compelled to respond
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually two witnesses, required from the plaintiff

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is the plaintiff (mudda'i)?
A) The one who is compelled to respond to the claim
B) The one who wants to take property from another and cannot be forced to litigate if he drops it
C) The judge who hears the case
D) The witness who testifies

Key Takeaway
The plaintiff must bring evidence; the defendant, if no evidence, must take an oath.

Conditions for a Valid Claim: Specification of Genus and Quantity
Stating the rule with a hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a claim must specify the genus and quantity of the object, derive it from the hadith, and apply it to identify valid and invalid claims.

Core Idea
A claim is only accepted if the object is specified in genus and quantity.

A legal claim before a judge must be clear and determinate; otherwise, the court cannot proceed. The Hanafi school, based on a prophetic hadith, requires the plaintiff to mention both the genus (e.g., land, cow) and the quantity (e.g., kilograms, number) of the claimed object. This lesson examines the rule and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
Read the text: 'The claim is not accepted until he mentions something known in its genus and quantity.' This is the core Hanafi rule.
The ruling is a condition for admissibility.
2
Understand the required specification
The commentator clarifies: you must state the type of property (e.g., cow vs. buffalo) and its measurable quantity (e.g., weight, count).
Vague claims like 'you owe me something' are invalid.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith narrates a dispute over land. The plaintiff specified the land's genus (land) and its provenance (my father's land), making the claim clear.
The Prophet did not reject the claim for lack of specification; rather, he asked for proof.
4
Apply the rule to the hadith
The plaintiff's mention of 'land that belonged to my father' fulfills the requirement of genus and implied boundaries (quantity).
This shows the rule was practiced in front of the Prophet.
5
Note the legal consequence
Without such specification, the claim is inadmissible. The defendant is compellable to respond only if the claim is properly specified.
This protects the defendant from vague accusations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرح الثميرى الجزء الرابع) كتاب الدعوى الخصومة [2741] (۲) ولا تقبل الدعوى حتى يذكر شيئا معلوما في جنسه وقدره [2732] (۳) فان كان عينا في يد المدعى عليه كلف احضارها ليشير اليها بالدعوى وان مال ہو اس لئے جھگڑا اور خصومت پر مجبور کیا جا سکے۔ وہ خصومت نہ بھی کرنا چاہے تو اس کو خصومت کرنے پر مجبور کیا جائے کیونکہ مال اسی کے قبضے میں ہے۔ اس حدیث میں اس کی تفصیل ہے۔ عن علقمة بن وائل بن حجر الحضرمي عن ابيه قال جاء رجل من حضر موت ورجل من كنده الى رسول الله فقال الحضر مى يا رسول الله ان هذا غلبني على ارض كانت لابي فقال الكندي هي ارضي في يدى ازرعها ليس له فيها حق فقال النبي الا الله للحضرمي الك بينة؟ قال لا! قال فلك يمينه . قال يا رسول الله انه فاجر ليس يبالى ما حلف ليس يتورع من شيء فقال ليس لك منه الا ذلك (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الرجل خلف على علمه فیما غاب عنه ص ۱۵۴ نمبر ۳۶۲۳ / ترمذی شریف ، باب ، جاء فى ان البيئة على المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیه ص ۲۴۹ نمبر ۱۳۴۰ ) اس حدیث میں حضرت حضری مدعی ہیں وہ جھگڑا کر رہے ہیں اور حضور کے سامنے دعوی پیش کر رہے ہیں اور چاہے تو دعوی چھوڑ بھی سکتے ہیں۔ اور کندی مدعی علیہ ہیں ان کے قبضے میں زمین ہے وہ خصومت چھوڑ نا چاہے تو نہیں چھوڑ سکتے ۔ الخصومة : مقدمے میں جو دونوں طرف سے جھگڑا کرتے ہیں اس کو خصومت کہتے ہیں۔ [2731) (۲) دعوی مقبول نہیں ہو گا یہاں تک کہ ذکر کرے معلوم چیز جنس کے اعتبار سے اور مقدار کے اعتبار سے۔ مقدمے میں دعوی اس وقت تک مقبول نہیں ہو گا جب تک کہ چیز کی جنس نہ بیان کرے مثلا وہ گائے ہے یا بھینس ہے اور عددی یا کیلی چیز ہے تو اس کی مقدار بیان کرے کہ کتنا کیلو ہے۔ تا کہ دعوی کو واضح کیا جاسکے اور چیز متعین ہو جائے۔ اوپر کی حدیث میں قال الحضرمی یا رسول الله ان هذا غلبني على ارض كانت لابی (ب) (ابواؤ د شریف نمبر ۳۳۶۲۳ ترمذی شریف نمبر ۱۳۴۰) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ میرے باپ کی زمین تھی جس پر کندی نے قبضہ کیا ہے۔ زمین کی چوہدی بیان کی ورز مین کا تعارف کروایا اور جنس بھی بیان کی کہ وہ زمین ہے جس کا مجھے دعوی ہے۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ چیز کی جنس اور مقدار بیان کرنا ضروری ہے۔
Al-Sharh al-Thamiri, Part 4; Book of Claims. Al-Khusuma [2741] (2) The claim is not accepted until he mentions something known in its genus and quantity. [2732] (3) If it is a specific thing in the possession of the defendant, he is compelled to bring it to point at it in the claim, and ... This hadith details it. From Alqamah ibn Wail ibn Hajar al-Hadrami from his father: A man from Hadramawt and a man from Kinda came to the Messenger of Allah. The Hadrami said: O Messenger of Allah, this man has overpowered me regarding land that belonged to my father. The Kindi said: It is my land, in my hand, I cultivate it, he has no right in it. The Prophet said: O Hadrami, do you have proof? He said: No. He said: Then you have his oath. He said: O Messenger of Allah, he is a wicked man, he does not care about an oath, he does not refrain from anything. He said: You have nothing else except that. (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi). In this hadith, the Hadrami is the plaintiff, he is disputing and presenting the claim before the Prophet, and if he wishes he can abandon the claim. The Kindi is the defendant, the land is in his possession, he cannot abandon the dispute if he wants. Al-Khusuma: It is called the disputation between the two parties in a case. [2731] (2) The claim will not be accepted until he mentions the thing known in terms of genus and quantity. The claim is not accepted in a case until he states the genus of the thing—for example, is it a cow or a buffalo—and if it is a countable or measurable thing, he states its quantity—e.g., how many kilograms—so that the claim can be clarified and the thing specified. In the hadith above: 'O Messenger of Allah, this man has overpowered me regarding land that was my father's.' In this hadith, the boundaries of the land were stated, and its genus was also stated—that it is land which I claim. From this it is understood that it is necessary to state the genus and quantity of the thing.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri is a ruling with proof. It begins by stating the rule: a claim is only accepted if the object is specified in genus and quantity. The commentator then cites a famous hadith where two men came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) disputing land. The Hadrami claimed the land was his father's, and the Kindi claimed it was his. The Prophet asked the Hadrami for proof, and when he had none, told him to accept the defendant's oath. The commentator explains that the Hadrami specified the land's genus (it was land) and its boundaries (implied by mentioning it was his father's land), thus meeting the requirement. The Urdu commentary elaborates: one must state whether the item is a cow or buffalo, and if countable or measurable, its quantity. This ensures the claim is clear and the object is identified. The hadith serves as the proof for this rule, as the plaintiff mentioned both genus and boundaries.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusūmah): The disputation between the two parties in a case; the litigation.
الدعوى (al-daʿwā): A legal claim made by the plaintiff against the defendant before the judge.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or proof, typically witnesses, required to support the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a plaintiff specify in his claim for it to be valid?
A) Only the value of the object
B) The genus and quantity of the object
C) The name of the defendant
D) The date of the transaction

Key Takeaway
A claim must specify the genus and quantity of the object; otherwise, it is not admissible in court.

Requirements for a Valid Claim: Presenting the Object and Describing Real Estate
Explaining the requirement to present the object if in defendant's possession, and describe boundaries for real estate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to: state the three categories of claims (specific object in possession, real estate, right in dhimma); identify the required specification for each; and apply these rules to draft a valid claim statement.

Core Idea
A claim must eliminate ambiguity: bring the object, describe boundaries, or state the demand.

In a Hanafi courtroom, a judge cannot accept a vague claim. This passage sets the rules: if the claimed object is in the defendant's possession, the defendant must produce it so the plaintiff can point to it. If it cannot be brought, its value must be stated. For immovable property, the plaintiff must describe its four boundaries (and modern identifiers). For a debt, the plaintiff must explicitly state the demand. These formalities ensure the claim is specific and the defendant can respond properly.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three categories of claims
Identify three types: (1) a specific object in defendant's possession, (2) real estate, (3) a right in the dhimma (e.g., debt). Each has a different requirement.
These are mutually exclusive categories.
2
Determine the required specification for each
For a specific object: it must be brought to court if possible; if not, state its value. For real estate: describe its four boundaries (and modern plot/survey numbers). For a right in the dhimma: explicitly state the demand.
The common goal is removing ignorance (jahalah).
3
Note the additional assertions required
In every claim, the plaintiff must assert that the object is in the defendant's possession and that he demands its return (or payment).
These derive from the hadith of the Hadrami man.
4
Understand the consequence of non-compliance
If any of these requirements are missing, the claim is invalid (ghayr sahiha) and the judge should dismiss it or ask for correction.
The text says: 'When the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant...' implying validity depends on these conditions.
5
Apply to modern contexts
Modern courts use similar rules: unique identification of assets (e.g., serial numbers, CAD drawings, plot numbers) to avoid ambiguity.
The commentary explicitly adds plot and survey numbers.

Source Text

[2723] (۳) پس اگر وہ چیز بعینہ مدعی علیہ کے قبضے میں ہے تو اس کو مجبور کیا جائے گا اس کو حاضر کرنے کا تاکہ دعوی کے وقت اس کی طرف اشارہ کر سکے اور اگر حاضر نہ ہو تو اس کی قیمت بیان کرے۔ اگر وہ چیز بعینہ موجود ہو تو کہا جائے گا کہ اس کو مجلس قضا میں حاضر کرے تا کہ دعوی کے وقت اس کی طرف اشارہ کر سکے اور اگر حاضر نہ حاشیہ : (الف) حضر موت کے آدمی اور کندہ کے ایک آدمی حضور کے پاس آئے۔ پس حضرمی نے کہا یا رسول اللہ اس نے میری زمین پر قبضہ کر لیا جو میرے باپ کی تھی۔ پس کندی نے کہا یہ میری زمین میرے قبضے میں ہے۔ میں اس میں ہوتا ہوں اس میں کسی کا حق نہیں ہے۔ تو آپ نے حضرمی سے پو چھا کیا تمہارے پاس گواہ ہے؟ کہا نہیں ! [2732] (3) So if that thing is specifically in the possession of the defendant, he will be compelled to bring it so that he can point to it at the time of the claim, and if he cannot bring it, he will state its value. If the thing is present, he will be told to bring it to the courtroom so that he can point to it at the time of the claim, and if he cannot bring it... Footnote: (a) The man from Hadramawt and the man from Kinda came to the Prophet. The Hadrami said: O Messenger of Allah, he has seized my land that was my father's. The Kindi said: This is my land in my possession, I cultivate it, no one has a right to it. The Prophet asked the Hadrami: Do you have evidence? He said: No. He said: Then you have his oath. He said: O Messenger of Allah, he is a wicked man, he does not care about the oath, he does not refrain from anything. The Prophet said: You have nothing except that. (b) O Messenger of Allah, he has seized my land that was my father's. [2733] (4) If he claims real estate, he defines its boundaries, mentions that it is in the possession of the defendant, and that he demands it from him. [2734] (5) If it is a right in the liability (dhimma), he mentions that he demands it. [2735] (6) So when the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant about it; if he admits, the judge passes judgment against him. If he cannot bring it, he states its value. Pointing to the object at the time of claim or testimony specifies it, so he will be told to bring it. If he cannot bring it, he states its value because value also provides some specification. [2733] (4) If land is claimed, he must state its boundaries, and also mention that it is in the defendant's possession and that he demands it. Since land cannot be brought to the court, he must state its four boundaries: who are the people to the east, west, south, and north. In this era, he should also state the plot number and survey number so the land is specified. Whether movable or immovable property, he must also state that it is in the defendant's possession, because if it is not in his possession but in the plaintiff's own possession, what is the point of claiming? Also, he must say that he wants to take back that land or movable property, because if he does not want to take it back, what benefit is there in filing a claim and going to court? In the aforementioned hadith, it was that this land was my father's, from which its four boundaries were known. And from 'the Hadrami said: O Messenger of Allah, this man has overpowered me regarding land that was my father's', the demand is also understood.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on da'wa, presenting three distinct rulings. First (point 3): If the claimed object is a specific item in the defendant's possession, the defendant is compelled to bring it to the courtroom so the plaintiff can point to it. If the item cannot be brought, the plaintiff must state its value. This is because pointing or specifying value removes ambiguity. Second (point 4): For immovable property like land, the plaintiff cannot bring it to court, so he must describe it by mentioning its four boundaries (east, west, south, north) and also, according to the commentator, modern identifiers like plot and survey numbers. He must also assert that the property is in the defendant's possession and that he demands its return. The hadith of the man from Hadramawt is used as evidence: he specified the land by saying 'my father's land,' which indicated its boundaries. Third (point 5): If the claim is for a right in the defendant's liability (e.g., a debt), the plaintiff must explicitly state that he demands payment. The commentary emphasizes that the claim must include the demand element, not just state the existence of the right. The footnote (a) recounts the full hadith to clarify the roles of plaintiff and defendant.

Key Terms
الدعوى (al-da'wa): A formal legal claim in court.
عقار (ʿaqār): Immovable property such as land or real estate.
حق في الذمة (ḥaqq fī al-dhimmah): A right or debt that is owed by the defendant, not tied to a specific object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it is a requirement of the judge
B) Because land cannot be brought to court, and boundaries specify it
C) Because it increases the value of the claim
D) Because the defendant demands it

Key Takeaway
A valid claim in Hanafi law must eliminate ambiguity: present the object, define boundaries for land, or state the demand for a debt.

Procedure After Valid Claim: Judge's Inquiry and Confession
Describing the judge's role to ask the defendant and the effect of confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's procedural duty after a valid claim and explain the effect of the defendant's confession in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Confession is conclusive: once the defendant admits the claim, the judge must rule without further evidence.

In this passage, we learn the two key steps after a valid claim is filed. First, the plaintiff must explicitly demand his right if it is in the liability. Second, the judge must ask the defendant, and if he confesses, judgment is immediate. This reflects the Hanafi principle that confession (iqrar) is the strongest proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of claims
The passage distinguishes between claims for a specific item and claims for a right in the liability (debt). For a debt, the plaintiff must explicitly state he demands it.
2
Examine the judge's role after valid claim
Once the claim is valid, the judge must ask the defendant about the claim. This is a mandatory procedural step.
3
Analyze the effect of confession
If the defendant confesses the truth of the claim, the judge passes judgment immediately. No witnesses or oath are needed.
Confession (iqrar) is a complete proof in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[12۷۳۴ (۵) اور اگر اس کے ذمے حق ہو تو ذکر کرے کہ وہ اس کا مطالبہ کرتا ہے۔ مثلا دعوی یہ تھا کہ زید کے ذمے ہیں درہم میرا قرض ہے تو قرض ہونا ذمے میں حق ہوا۔ تو اس صورت میں بھی دعوی کے ساتھ یہ ذکر کرے کہ میں اس قرض کا مطالبہ کرتا ہوں تا کہ معلوم ہوا کہ صرف حق کا اقرار نہیں کروانا چاہتا بلکہ اس کا مطالبہ بھی کر رہا ہے۔ نہ یہ مسئلے اس اصول پر ہیں کہ متعرف اور متعین چیز کا دعوی ہوتا ہے مجہول کا دعوی نہیں ہوتا۔ [2735] (۲) پس جب دعوی صحیح ہو جائے تو قاضی مدعی علیہ سے اس کے بارے میں پوچھے۔ پس اگر اس نے اعتراف کر لیا تو اس پر اس کا فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا۔ ر مدعی کے دعوی دائر کرنے کے بعد قاضی مدعی علیہ سے پوچھے گا کہ کیا واقعی مدعی کا دعوی صحیح ہے؟ اگر وہ اس کا اعتراف کر لے کہ واقعی مدعی کا میرے ذمے حق ہے تو قاضی اس کا فیصلہ کر دے گا۔ اب گواہ یا ستم کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ جب مدعی علیہ نے اعتراف کر لیا تو اب گواہ کی یا مدعی علیہ کی قسم کی کیا ضرورت رہی (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عـن ابـن سـیـریـن قال اعترف
[2734] (5) And if it is a right in the liability, he mentions that he demands it. For example, the claim was that Zaid owes me dirhams as a debt; so the debt becomes a right in the liability. In this case, along with the claim, he should mention: I demand this debt, so that it is known that he does not only want to have the right acknowledged, but he is also demanding it. Yes, these issues are based on the principle that a claim is for a known and specified thing, not for an unknown thing. [2735] (6) So when the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant about it. If he confesses, judgment is passed against him. After the plaintiff files the claim, the judge will ask the defendant: Is the plaintiff's claim true? If he confesses that indeed the plaintiff has a right against him, the judge will decide. Now there is no need for witnesses or oath. When the defendant has confessed, what need is there for witnesses or the plaintiff's oath? (2) There is an athar: From Ibn Sirin, he said: 'Confession...'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on da'wa with two further rulings. First (point 5): If the claim is for a right in the defendant's liability (like a debt), the plaintiff must explicitly state that he demands its fulfillment. This distinguishes a mere acknowledgment of a right from an actual demand. The principle is that a claim must be for a known and specified thing; unknown claims are not valid. Second (point 6): Once the claim is properly made, the judge asks the defendant about it. If the defendant confesses that the claim is true, the judge immediately passes judgment against him. No further evidence (witnesses or oath) is required. The narrator cites an athar from Ibn Sirin emphasizing confession, though the quotation is cut off. The Urdu commentary reinforces that after confession, evidence is superfluous. This reflects the Hanafi principle that confession (iqrar) is conclusive proof.

Key Terms
الذمة (al-dhimmah): The legal liability or capacity of a person that bears rights and obligations.
اعتراف (iʿtirāf): Confession by the defendant that the claim is true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defendant's confession before a judge is conclusive and immediately leads to judgment without additional evidence.

Conditions for a Valid Claim and Judge's Procedure
Explains how to frame a claim and the judge's subsequent actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three essential elements of a valid claim (specification, possession, demand) and explain the judge's subsequent procedure (confession → judgment, denial → evidence, no evidence → oath).

Core Idea
A valid claim must specify the subject, assert defendant's possession, and demand return; the judge then proceeds by asking the defendant.

In Hanafi court procedure, not every claim is automatically heard. The plaintiff must frame the claim with sufficient detail and a clear demand. This lesson from Kitab al-Da'wa explains both the conditions that make a claim valid and the judge's subsequent steps depending on the defendant's response. Understanding this procedural framework is essential for anyone studying Islamic judicial process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements of a valid claim
The text requires: (a) specification of the subject (ta'rif) by value for movables or boundaries for land, (b) assertion that it is in the defendant's possession (yad), and (c) demand for its return or payment (talab).
Without any of these, the claim is defective.
2
Apply to movable and immovable property
For movables not present in court, state their value. For land, state its four boundaries (east, west, south, north) and modern identifiers like plot number. Also explicitly say that the property is in defendant's possession and you demand it back.
The Hadith of al-Hadrami demonstrates specifying boundaries and demand.
3
Understand the underlying principle
A claim must be for a specified (ma'ruf) thing, not unknown (majhul). The text states: 'These issues are based on the principle that a claim is made for a defined and specified thing, not for an unknown thing.'
4
Learn the judge's procedure upon confession (iqrar)
If the defendant confesses, the judge passes judgment immediately without need for evidence or oath. This is supported by the athar of Shuraih: 'Shahida 'alayka ibnu ukhti khalk' meaning the confession itself is proof.
Confession is the strongest proof (iqrar hujja qati'a).
5
Learn the procedure upon denial (inkar)
If the defendant denies, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence (bayyinah): two male witnesses or one male and two females (Quran 2:282). If plaintiff cannot produce evidence, he may request the defendant's oath. If the defendant swears, judgment goes in his favor.
This is based on the hadith: 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayh'.

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الرابع
۸۸
كتاب الدعوى .
بها [۲۳] (۷) وان انكر سأل المدعى البيئة فان احضرها قضى بها (۲۷۳۷](۸) وان عجز عن ذلك وطلب يمين خصمه استحلفه عليها.
رجل عند شریح با مرثم انكره فقضى عليه باعترافه فقال اتقضى على بغير بينة؟ فقال شهد علیک ابن اخت خالک (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الاعتراف عند القاضی ج ثامن ص ۳۰۳ نمبر ۱۵۳۰۱) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مدعی علیہ کے اعتراف سے فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا۔
۲۷۳۶] (۷) اور اگر انکار کرے تو طلب کرے مدعی سے البینہ، پس اگر اس کو حاضر کر دے تو فیصلہ کر دے اس کے مطابق۔ قاضی نے مدعی علیہ سے مدعی کے دعوی کے بارے میں پوچھا تو مدعی علیہ نے انکار کر دیا تو مدعی سے اپنے دعوی پر گواہ مانگا جائے گا، پس اگر دعوی کے ثبوت کے لئے دو گواہ پیش کر دیئے تو مدعی کے دعوی کے مطابق فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا۔
آیت میں ہے۔ واستشهدوا شهيدين من رجالكم فان لم يكونا رجلين فرجل وامرأتان ممن ترضون من الشهداء ان تضل احداهما فتذكر احداهما الاخرى (ب) ( آیت ۲۸۲ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت میں ہے کہ دو مرد گواہ بنائے یا ایک مرد اور دو عورتیں گواہ بنائے (۲) حدیث میں گزرا ۔ فقال النبي الله للحضر مى الك بينة؟ قال لا (ج) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی ان البيته على المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیه ص ۲۲۹ نمبر ۱۳۴۰ ابوداؤد شریف، باب الرجل يحلف علی عظیمیہ فیما غاب عنه ص ۱۵۴ نمبر ۳۶۲۳) اس حدیث میں حضور نے حضرت حضرمی سے گواہ مانگا۔ جس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ گواہ پیش کر دیتے تو آپ فیصلہ کر دیتے (۳) ایک حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس ان رسول الله قضی بیمین و شاهد (د) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب القضاء بالسیمین و الشاهد ص ۵۲ نمبر ۳۶۰۸ ر ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی الیمین مع الشاهد ص ۲۴۹ نمبر ۱۳۴۳) اس حدیث میں ایک گواہ اور خود مدعی کی گواہی سے فیصلہ فرمایا تو اگر دو گواہ ہوں تو بدرجہ اولی قاضی گواہی کے بعد فیصلہ فرمائیں گے۔
[۲۷۳۷ (۸) اگر بینہ حاضر کرنے سے عاجز ہو گیا اور طلب کرے اپنے مد مقابل کی قسم تو اس پر قسم لے گا۔ ان مدعی کو گواہ پیش کرنے کو کہا اس پر وہ گواہ پیش کرنے سے عاجز ہو گیا اور گواہ نہیں پیش کر سکا تو مدعی سے کہا جائے گا کہ اب آپ مدعی علیہ سے اس پر قسم لے سکتے ہیں۔ پس اگر مدعی مدعی علیہ سے قسم لینا چاہے تو مدعی علیہ سے قسم لی جائے گی ۔ پس اگر وہ قسم کھالے تو مدعی علیہ کے کہنے کے مطابق فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابن سیرین فرماتے ہیں کہ ایک آدمی نے حضرت شریح کے پاس اقرار کیا پھر مکر گیا تو اس پر اس کے اعتراف کرنے کی وجہ سے فیصلہ کیا تو وہ آدمی کہنے لگا کہ آپ مجھ پر بغیر گواہ کے فیصلہ کر رہے ہیں؟ حضرت شریح نے فرمایا تمہارے ماموں کی بہن کے بیٹے نے تمہارے خلاف گواہی دی۔ یعنی پہلے خود تو نے اعتراف کیا تھا (ب) تمہارے مردوں میں سے دو آدمیوں کو گواہ بناؤ، پس اگر دو مرد نہ ہوں تو ایک مرد اور دو عورتیں ، جن گواہوں سے تم راضی ہو۔ دو عورتیں اس لئے ہیں کہ ایک کو یاد نہ رہے تو دوسری اس کو یاد دلا دے (ج) آپ نے حضرت حضرمی سے پوچھا کیا تمہارے پاس گواہ ہے؟ فرمایا نہیں (1) حضور نے ایک قسم اور ایک گواہی کے ذریعہ فیصلہ فرمایا۔
Book of Claims. If (the object) is not present, mention its value [2733] (4) And if he claims real estate, he should specify it and mention that it is in the possession of the defendant and that he demands it from him. [2734] (5) And if it is a right in the liability (dhimma), he should mention that he demands it from him. [2735] (6) So when the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant about it; if he admits, he passes judgment against him... If silent, then state its value. At the time of claim or testimony, pointing will make the thing specified; therefore he will be asked to produce it. If he cannot produce it, he should state its value, because value also gives some specification. [2733] (4) If he claims land, he should state its boundaries and also mention that it is in the possession of the defendant and that he demands it from him. Since the land cannot be brought to the court, he should state its four boundaries: who are the people to its east, west, south, and north. In this era, he should also state what is the account number of the land and what is the plot number so that the land is specified. Whether it is movable or immovable property, he should also state that it is in the possession of the defendant. Because if it is not in the defendant's possession but in the plaintiff's own possession, what is the point of claiming? And he should also say: I want to take back this land or movable property. Because if he does not want to take it back, what benefit is there in filing a claim and a lawsuit? In the mentioned hadith, he said 'This land is my father's' from which its four boundaries were known. And from his saying 'O Messenger of Allah, this person has overcome me on the land that belonged to my father' (a) it is also known that he is demanding it. [2734] (5) And if it is a right in his liability, he should mention that he demands it from him. For example, the claim was: Zaid owes me dirhams as a debt, so the debt is a right in the liability. In this case too, along with the claim, he should mention: I demand this debt, so that it is known that he does not only want to get an acknowledgment of the right but is also demanding it. These issues are based on the principle that a claim is made for a defined and specified thing, not for an unknown thing. [2735] (2) Then, when the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant about it. So if he confesses, judgment is passed against him accordingly. After the plaintiff files the claim, the judge will ask the defendant: Is the plaintiff's claim really true? If he confesses that the plaintiff indeed has a right against him, the judge will pass judgment. Now there is no need for witnesses or oath. Since the defendant has confessed, what need remains for witnesses or the defendant's oath? (2) There is an athar: 'From Ibn Sirin, he said: A man confessed before Shuraih'... Footnote (a): Then Hadrami said: O Messenger of Allah, he has seized my land that belonged to my father. [Page 95] Al-Sharh al-Thumayri Vol. 4, p. 88, Book of Claims. [2736] (7) And if he denies, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence; if he brings it, he passes judgment accordingly. [2737] (8) And if he is unable to do that and requests the oath of his opponent, he administers the oath to him. A man confessed before Shuraih then denied it, so he passed judgment against him based on his confession. He said: Do you pass judgment against me without evidence? He replied: The son of your maternal aunt's daughter testified against you. (a) (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq, Bab al-I'tiraf 'inda al-Qadi, Vol. 8, p. 303 no. 15301) From this athar it is known that judgment is passed based on the defendant's confession. [2736] (7) And if he denies, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence; if he brings it, he passes judgment accordingly. The judge asked the defendant about the plaintiff's claim, and the defendant denied it, so the plaintiff will be asked to bring witnesses for his claim. If he presents two witnesses to prove his claim, judgment will be passed according to the plaintiff's claim. There is an ayah: 'And call to witness two witnesses from among your men; but if there are not two men, then one man and two women from among those you approve of the witnesses, so that if one of them errs, the other can remind her.' (b) (Surah al-Baqarah 282) This ayah states that two men or one man and two women should be witnesses. (2) A hadith has passed: 'The Prophet said to Hadrami: Do you have evidence? He said: No.' (c) (Tirmidhi, Bab al-Bayyinah 'ala al-Mudda'i wa al-Yamin 'ala al-Mudda'a 'alayh, p. 229 no. 1340; Abu Dawud, Bab al-Rajul yahlifu 'ala 'azimihi fima ghaba 'anhu, p. 154 no. 3623) In this hadith, the Prophet asked Hadrami for witnesses, meaning if he had brought witnesses, he would have passed judgment. (3) Another hadith: 'From Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah passed judgment based on an oath and one witness.' (d) (Abu Dawud, Bab al-Qada' bi al-Yamin wa al-Shahid, p. 52 no. 3608; Tirmidhi, Bab al-Yamin ma'a al-Shahid, p. 249 no. 1343) In this hadith, he passed judgment with one witness and the plaintiff's own testimony; so if there are two witnesses, the judge will certainly pass judgment after testimony. [2737] (8) If he is unable to produce evidence and requests the oath of his opponent, he will administer the oath to him. The plaintiff was asked to bring witnesses, and he was unable to bring them. So he is told: Now you can request an oath from the defendant. If the plaintiff wants to take an oath from the defendant, the oath will be administered to the defendant. If he takes the oath, judgment will be passed according to the defendant's statement. Footnote (a): Ibn Sirin says: A man confessed before Shuraih then withdrew, so he passed judgment against him due to his confession. The man said: Are you passing judgment against me without witnesses? Shuraih said: The son of your maternal aunt's daughter testified against you. Meaning you confessed yourself first. (b) Your men, make two men witnesses; if not two men, then one man and two women, from the witnesses you approve; two women so that if one forgets, the other reminds her. (c) He asked Hadrami: Do you have witnesses? He said: No. (d) He passed judgment by an oath and one witness.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa (Book of Claims) is a ruling-with-proof exposition on the requirements for a valid claim (da'wa) in a Hanafi court and the judge's subsequent procedure. The author first explains how the claim must be framed depending on the subject matter. If the claimed object is absent from court, the plaintiff must describe it sufficiently: for movable items, by stating their value; for immovable property (land), by stating its four boundaries (sharq, gharb, junub, shamal) and modern identifiers like account and plot numbers. The plaintiff must also assert that the defendant is in possession and that he demands its return. This requirement is derived from the Hadith of al-Hadrami, where the plaintiff said 'This person has overcome me on the land that belonged to my father,' showing both specification of boundaries and demand. For debts or rights in the dhimma (personal liability), the plaintiff must mention that he is demanding payment, not just establishing acknowledgment. The underlying principle is that a claim must be for a specified (mu'ayyan/muta'arrif) thing, not an unknown (majhul). Once a valid claim is made, the judge asks the defendant. If the defendant confesses (iqrar), judgment is given immediately without need for evidence or oath; the author supports this with the reported practice of Shuraih. If the defendant denies (inkar), the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence (bayyinah) — a minimum of two male witnesses or one male and two females (citing Quran 2:282), or according to another hadith, a single witness and an oath. If the plaintiff cannot bring evidence, he may request the defendant's oath (yamin). The judge administers the oath; if the defendant swears, judgment goes in his favor. This procedure is standard Hanafi law based on the famous principle 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayh' (evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier).

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A claim or lawsuit initiated by a plaintiff (mudda'i) against a defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) seeking a legal remedy.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant against whom a claim is made; the person alleged to be in possession or liable.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically the testimony of witnesses, that proves a claim in court.
يمين (yamin): An oath taken by a party, usually the defendant when plaintiff lacks evidence, to deny a claim.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment by a party (usually the defendant) of the claim against him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rules of da'wa, what must the plaintiff do if the claimed object is absent from court?
A) Bring the object to court at all costs.
B) Describe the object sufficiently (e.g., state its value for movables, boundaries for land) and demand its return from the defendant.
C) Immediately request the defendant's oath.
D) Provide two witnesses even before the judge questions the defendant.

Key Takeaway
A valid claim in Hanafi law requires specification, possession by defendant, and demand; the judge's procedure depends on the defendant's response (confession, denial with evidence, or denial leading to oath).

Plaintiff Must Provide Evidence Upon Denial
Ruling on requiring evidence from plaintiff when defendant denies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that the plaintiff must provide evidence upon the defendant's denial and identify the textual proofs from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Evidence is the plaintiff's duty; denial shifts the burden to the defendant's oath.

In Islamic judiciary, the burden of proof lies on the claimant. This lesson establishes the foundational procedure: when a defendant denies a claim, the judge must ask the plaintiff for evidence. If evidence is produced, the judge rules accordingly; if not, the defendant is asked to take an oath. This ruling is based on clear Quranic verses and authentic hadiths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
Recognize that a valid claim has been made and the defendant has denied it. The judge's action is triggered by the denial.
Without denial, the judge may proceed differently (e.g., confession).
2
State the Ruling
The judge must ask the plaintiff for evidence (bayyinah). This is wajib upon the plaintiff to provide.
Bayyinah typically means two male witnesses or one male and two females.
3
Locate the Quranic Proof
Refer to Surah al-Baqarah (2:282), which lays the foundation for witness testimony in financial transactions.
The verse emphasizes written documentation and witness requirements.
4
Locate the Hadith Proof
The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) with al-Hadrami shows the direct question: 'Do you have evidence?' Another hadith allows judgment with one witness and an oath.
The Prophet's practice confirms the procedure.
5
Apply the Rule
If plaintiff brings evidence, judge by it. If not, defendant takes an oath to deny the claim.
The oath is a serious matter; refusal to swear may imply admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان انكر سأل المدعى البيئة فان احضرها قضى بها. آیت میں ہے: واستشهدوا شهيدين من رجالكم فان لم يكونا رجلين فرجل وامرأتان ممن ترضون من الشهداء ان تضل احداهما فتذكر احداهما الاخرى. حدیث میں گزرا: فقال النبي الله للحضر مى الك بينة؟ قال لا. اور ایک حدیث میں ہے: عن ابن عباس ان رسول الله قضى بيمين و شاهد.
And if he denies, ask the plaintiff for evidence. If he brings it, judge by it. In the verse: 'And call to witness two witnesses from among your men, and if not two men, then a man and two women from those you approve as witnesses, so that if one of them errs, the other can remind her.' In the hadith: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to al-Hadrami: Do you have evidence? He said: No.' And another hadith: 'Ibn Abbas reported that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) judged by an oath and a witness.'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage states the basic procedure when a defendant denies a claim. The judge asks the plaintiff for evidence (bayyinah) – typically two male witnesses or one male and two females as per the Quranic verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:282). If the plaintiff produces evidence, the judge rules accordingly. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) with al-Hadrami shows that evidence is required from the plaintiff. Another hadith indicates that even a single witness with an oath suffices, so logically two witnesses are even stronger. This is a standard Hanafi ruling based on Quran and Sunnah.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence sufficient to prove a claim, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the judge ask the plaintiff for evidence in a lawsuit?
A) Immediately upon filing the case
B) After the defendant denies the claim
C) Before the defendant is heard
D) Only if the defendant requests it

Key Takeaway
The plaintiff must bring evidence; otherwise the defendant's oath is decisive.

Plaintiff May Demand Oath if No Evidence
Ruling on plaintiff requesting defendant's oath when unable to produce evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the plaintiff may demand the defendant's oath when unable to produce evidence and explain the hadith proof.

Core Idea
When a plaintiff lacks evidence, the defendant's oath becomes the decisive proof if demanded.

This lesson covers the procedure when a plaintiff cannot bring evidence. The Hanafi school holds that the plaintiff may request the defendant's oath, and the judge must administer it. The ruling is based on the hadith of al-Hadrami.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the plaintiff's request for the defendant's oath when unable to produce evidence. The ruling is that the judge must administer the oath (wajib).
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of al-Hadrami: the Prophet said 'falka yaminuh' (then you have his oath). This is a direct grant of right.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's statement explicitly gives the plaintiff the right to the defendant's oath. In Hanafi usul, such a direct directive from the Prophet indicates obligation for the judge to enforce it.
4
Note the practical implication
If the defendant takes the oath, the case is decided in his favor. The plaintiff cannot demand another oath or further evidence after the oath is administered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان عجز عن ذلك وطلب يمين خصمه استحلفه عليها. حديث میں ہے: فقال النبي الله للحضر مى الك بينة؟ قال لا! قال فلك يمينه.
And if he is unable to do that and requests an oath from his opponent, he shall oath him. In the hadith: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to al-Hadrami: Do you have evidence? He said: No. He said: Then you have his oath.'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage continues the procedure: if the plaintiff cannot produce evidence (e.g., no witnesses available or unable to bring them), he has the right to demand that the defendant take an oath denying the claim. The hadith of al-Hadrami is cited: the Prophet asked if he had evidence, and when he said no, the Prophet granted him the right to the defendant's oath. The Hanafi ruling is that if the defendant takes the oath, the case is decided in his favor. This is based on the principle 'al-bayyinatu ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu ala man ankara' (the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies).

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by the defendant in a lawsuit, affirming his denial of the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the plaintiff's right when he cannot produce evidence?
A) He loses the case
B) He can demand the defendant's oath
C) He can ask for a postponement
D) He can call the defendant as a witness

Key Takeaway
The plaintiff can demand the defendant's oath when he lacks evidence, and the judge must administer it.

Plaintiff with Witnesses Cannot Demand Oath
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath when witnesses are present

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a plaintiff has witnesses present and demands an oath, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn with their respective proofs.

Core Idea
The oath is a substitute for evidence; if witnesses are present, the substitute is not allowed.

When a plaintiff brings a claim, he must prove it. But what if he has witnesses ready yet demands the defendant's oath instead? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions disagree on whether this is permissible. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and the practical impact in the courtroom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine what Imam Abu Hanifa rules when the plaintiff says 'I have witnesses present' and demands the oath. Is the oath administered or not?
The ruling is clear: the defendant is not sworn.
2
Extract the legal basis
Find the hadith that Abu Hanifa uses as proof. Focus on the condition mentioned: the oath is only available when there is no evidence.
The hadith of al-Hadrami is the primary dalil.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's opposition
State the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani: that the plaintiff may choose between witnesses and oath even if witnesses are present. Identify their proof from the hadith of al-Ash'ath.
They rely on the general phrase 'your two witnesses or his oath'.
4
Analyze the response to the Sahibayn
Explain how Abu Hanifa's position handles the apparent conflict: the oath is only a substitute when evidence is absent, and the hadith of al-Ash'ath is interpreted in light of that principle.
The substitute cannot be used when the original is available.
5
Determine the operative ruling
Conclude which view is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school and why, based on the author's presentation and school tradition.
Abu Hanifa's view is the mufta bihi.

Source Text

[۲۷۳۸] (۹) وان قال لى بيئة حاضرة وطلب اليمين لم يستحلف عند ابي حنيفة رحمه حدیث میں گزرا کہ آپ نے حضرت حضرمی سے پوچھا کہ کیا آپ کے پاس گواہ ہے؟ فرمایا نہیں ! تو آپ نے فرمایا کہ پھر تو آپ کو کندی سے قسم لینے کا حق ہے۔ فقال النبی الا الله للحضر می الک بینة؟ قال لا! قال فلک یمینه . قال يا رسول الله انه فاجر ليس يبالي ما حلف ليس يتورع من شيء فقال ليس لك منه الا ذلك (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف ، باب الرجل يحلف على علمه فيما غاب عنه ص ۱۵۴ نمبر ۳۶۳۲۳ ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فی ان البرد على المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیہ ض ۲۲۹ نمبر ۱۳۸۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ اگر آپ کے پاس بینہ نہیں ہے تو آپ کو کندی سے قسم لینے کا حق ہے۔
خصم : مدمقابل ، مقدمے میں دوسرا فریق۔
[۲۷۳۸] (۹) اگر مدعی نے کہا میرے گواہ حاضر ہیں پھر بھی قسم طلب کرے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک قسم نہیں کھلائی جائے گی۔ مدعی کہتا ہے کہ میرے گواہ شہر میں ہیں ان کو پیش کر سکتا ہوں لیکن پھر بھی گواہ نہ پیش کر کے مدعی علیہ سے قسم لے کر فیصلہ کروانا چاہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ایسا نہیں کروا سکتا ہے۔ اس کے پاس گواہ موجود ہیں تو اب مدعی علیہ سے قسم نہیں لے سکتا۔ گواہی دلوا کر فیصلہ کروانا
ہوگا۔
کیونکہ مدعی علیہ سے قسم کھلوانے کا حق اس وقت ہے جب مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہ ہوں ۔ یا ہوں تو اتنی دوری پر رہتے ہوں کہ ان کو پیش کرنے میں دشواری ہو تب مدعی علیہ سے قسم لے سکتا ہے۔ اور یہاں گواہ قریب میں موجود ہیں (۲) حدیث میں ہے کہ گواہ نہ ہوں تب قسم کھلوا سکتا ہے۔ حضرت حضرمی والی حدیث میں حضور نے پوچھا ہے کہ کیا آپ کے پاس گواہ ہیں۔ انہوں نے فرمایا نہیں ! تب فرمایا کہ اب کندی سے قسم لے سکتے ہیں۔ حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ ہے۔ قال علقمة بن وائل بن حجر الحضرمي عن ابيه ... فقال النبي الله للحضرمي الك بينة؟ قال لا ! قال فلك يمينه (ب (ابوداؤد شریف نمبر ۳۶۲۳ / ترندی شریف نمبر ۱۳۴۰) اس حدیث میں فرمایا گوارہ نہیں ہیں تب آپ نے فرمایا کہ تم کو تم لینے کا حق ہے۔ اس لئے جب تک گواہ پیش کرنے کی گنجائش ہو تو مدعی علیہ سے قسم نہیں لے سکتے۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ گواہ موجود ہوں پھر بھی ان کو پیش نہ کر کے مدعی علیہ سے قسم لینا چاہے تو لے سکتا ہے۔ اس کو دونوں کا اختیار ہے وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ دوسری حدیث میں اس کی گنجائش معلوم ہوتی ہے۔ حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ ہے۔ ثم ان الاشعث بن قيس خرج الينا فقال ما يحدثكم ابو عبد الرحمن ... فقال رسول الله الله شاهداک او یمینه (ج) ( بخاری شریف ، باب اذا اختلف الراهن والمرتهن ونحوه فالی یہ علی المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیه ص ۳۲۲ نمبر ۲۵۱۶ر مسلم شریف ، باب وعيد من اقتطع حق مسلم بیمین فاجرة بالنارص ۸۰ نمبر ۳۵۶/۱۳۸) اس حدیث میں دونوں اختیار دیئے گئے ہیں یا تمہارے دو گواہ ہوں یا اس کی قسم ہو۔ چونکہ دو میں سے ایک کا اختیار ہے حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے حضرت حضرمی سے پوچھا کیا تمہارے پاس گواہ ہے؟ کہا نہیں! آپ نے فرمایا پھر تو تمہارے لئے اس کی قسم ہے۔ کہا یا رسول اللہ! وہ فاجر آدمی ہے وہ قسم کی پرواہ نہیں کرتا۔ کسی چیز سے پر ہیز بھی نہیں کرتا۔ پس آپ نے فرمایا تمہارے لئے قسم کے علاوہ کچھ نہیں ہے ( ب ) آپ نے حضرت حضرمی سے کہا
کیا تمہارے پاس گواہ ہے؟ کہا نہیں ! آپ نے فرمایا پھر تمہارے لئے اس کی قسم ہے (ج) آپ نے فرمایا تمہارے دو گواہ ہوں یا پھر اس کی قسم ہو۔
[2738] (9) And if he said, 'I have witnesses present' and he demands the oath, he will not be sworn according to Abu Hanifa. In the hadith it passed that the Prophet asked Hadrami: 'Do you have a witness?' He said: 'No.' Then he said: 'Then you have the right to take an oath from your opponent.' The Prophet said to Hadrami: 'Do you have evidence?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'Then you have his oath.' He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, he is a sinful person, he does not care about what he swears, he does not refrain from anything.' He said: 'You have nothing from him except that.' (Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi). In this hadith it says that if you do not have evidence, then you have the right to take an oath from your opponent. Opponent: the other party in the lawsuit. [2738] (9) If the plaintiff says, 'My witnesses are present,' and still demands the oath, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the oath will not be administered. The plaintiff says he has witnesses in the city and can produce them, but he does not produce them and wants to settle the case by taking an oath from the defendant. Imam Abu Hanifa says that he cannot do this. Since he has witnesses present, he cannot take an oath from the defendant; he must produce the witnesses and get a verdict. Because the right to have the defendant sworn exists only when the plaintiff has no witnesses, or if he has witnesses but they are at such a distance that producing them is difficult. Here the witnesses are present nearby. (2) In the hadith it says that if there are no witnesses, then he can have the defendant sworn. In the Hadrami hadith, the Prophet asked if he had witnesses. He said no. Then he said that now he can take an oath from the opponent. The portion of the hadith is: 'Alqamah ibn Wa'il ibn Hajar al-Hadrami narrated from his father... The Prophet said to Hadrami: Do you have evidence? He said: No. He said: Then you have his oath.' (Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi). In this hadith it says when there are no witnesses, then the Prophet said that you have the right to take an oath. Therefore, as long as there is a possibility of producing witnesses, he cannot take an oath from the defendant. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that even if witnesses are present, if he wants to take an oath from the defendant without producing them, he may do so. He has the option of both. They say that another hadith indicates this concession. The portion of the hadith is: 'Then al-Ash'ath ibn Qays came out to us and said: What is Abu Abd al-Rahman narrating to you? ... The Messenger of Allah said: Your two witnesses or his oath.' (Bukhari, Muslim). In this hadith both options are given: either your two witnesses or his oath. Since one of the two is optional.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a plaintiff who has witnesses present can demand an oath from the defendant instead of producing those witnesses. The author begins by stating Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling: if the plaintiff says he has witnesses present and demands an oath, the defendant is not sworn. The reasoning is that the right to demand an oath from the defendant is conditional upon the plaintiff not having accessible witnesses. The primary evidence is the hadith of al-Hadrami, where the Prophet explicitly asked if he had evidence and, upon receiving a negative response, granted him the right to an oath. This implies that if evidence were present, the option of oath would not be available. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) differ, holding that the plaintiff has the choice between presenting witnesses or demanding the oath, even if witnesses are present. They rely on the hadith from al-Ash'ath ibn Qays where the Prophet said 'Your two witnesses or his oath,' indicating an option. Imam Abu Hanifa's position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, and it is based on the principle that the oath is a substitute for evidence, and a substitute cannot be used when the original is available.

Key Terms
بيئة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically two male witnesses in legal claims.
يمين (yamin): Oath taken by the defendant (or plaintiff in some cases) to deny a claim.
مدعى (mudda'i): Plaintiff, the one who brings a claim.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayh): Defendant, the one against whom a claim is made.
صاحبين (sahibayn): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if a plaintiff says he has witnesses present but demands an oath from the defendant?
A) The defendant must be sworn immediately.
B) The plaintiff must produce his witnesses and cannot demand an oath.
C) The plaintiff has the choice between witnesses and oath.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
When a plaintiff has accessible witnesses, he must present them; the option of demanding the defendant's oath is only available when evidence is absent or difficult to produce.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
The Oath Does Not Shift to the Plaintiff
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on oath shifting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oath shifting, identify the evidence for both Hanafi and Shafi'i positions, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in a court case.

Core Idea
The plaintiff brings proof; the defendant takes the oath — these roles never reverse.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, the allocation of the oath is a cornerstone of procedural justice. The matn states emphatically: 'The oath is not returned upon the plaintiff.' This lesson unpacks the rationale behind this ruling, the two practical scenarios it governs, and the contrasting view of Imam al-Shafi'i. You will learn how the Hanafi school derives its position from explicit prophetic hadith and why it rejects the alternative.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position for each scenario
Identify the two scenarios: (1) plaintiff has one witness and wants to take an oath as substitute; (2) plaintiff has two witnesses but is asked to take an oath after presenting them. For both, Hanafi ruling is that the plaintiff cannot be required to take an oath.
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i allows the plaintiff to take an oath when he has only one witness (to substitute for the second) and also permits the judge to impose an oath on the plaintiff even after two witnesses for reassurance.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
The primary proof is the hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayh'. Analyse how this text divides roles exclusively. Also the hadith that the Prophet decided by an oath on the defendant.
The taqsim (division) in the hadith is key: each party has a specific duty.
4
Examine the Shafi'i dalil and Hanafi response
The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'qada bi yamin wa shahid' and athar from Ali and Shuraih. The Hanafi response interprets the 'yamin' in that hadith as the defendant's oath, not the plaintiff's, and considers the athar weaker than the explicit prophetic text.
Hanafis argue that the clear division in the primary hadith cannot be overridden by ambiguous reports.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice: a Hanafi plaintiff with one witness cannot win by taking an oath; he must either produce a second witness or demand the defendant's oath. A Shafi'i plaintiff in the same situation can take an oath and win the case.
This difference directly affects litigation strategy.

ان امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ قاضی چاہے تو اطمینان کے لئے گواہ کی پیشگی کے بعد بھی مدعی سے قسم کھلائے ۔
ان کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے۔ ان عليا كان يرى الحلف مع البينة۔ اور دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ عن ابن سیرین ان رجلا ادعی قبل رجل حقا واقام عليه البينة فاستحلفه شريح فكانه يابی الیمین فقال شریح بئس ما تثنی علی شهودک (1) ماشیہ : (الف) آپ نے اپنے خطبے میں فرمایا گواہ مدعی پر ہے اور قسم مدعی علیہ پر ہے (ب) آپ نے قسم کا فیصلہ فرمایا مدعی علیہ پر (ج) آپ نے قسم اور ایک گواہ کے ذریعہ فیصلہ فرمایا (د) حضرت علی گواہ کے ساتھ مدعی کا قسم کھانا بھی ضروری سمجھتے تھے۔ دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ حضرت ابن سیرین سے منقول (باقی اگلے صفحہ پر )

Allah the Exalted 239 (10) And the oath is not returned upon the plaintiff. Therefore, while a witness exists, the plaintiff cannot take an oath. [2739] (10) And the oath will not be imposed on the plaintiff. Book of Claims. This issue has two scenarios: One, that the plaintiff has one witness; now to substitute for the second witness, if he takes an oath, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa he cannot do so. Either he brings two witnesses or he takes an oath from the defendant. The hadith divides: that upon the plaintiff is only to present witnesses, and if that is not available, then upon the plaintiff is an oath? Actually, the hadith says: 'The burden of proof is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the defendant.' This hadith divides: that the plaintiff must provide evidence, and the defendant must take the oath. Another hadith: from Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, decided that the oath is upon the defendant. Imam Shafi'i says that if the plaintiff has only one witness, then as a substitute for the second witness, the plaintiff will take an oath, and this will be in the rank of a second witness, upon which the judge will decide in favor of the plaintiff. Their evidence is the hadith: from Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, decided by [the combination of] an oath and a witness. The second scenario is that the plaintiff has two witnesses and he has presented them; then, despite that, should the plaintiff also take an oath that this thing is mine? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the plaintiff does not need to take an oath now. He only needed to present witnesses, which he did. The above hadiths are their evidence. Imam Shafi'i says that the judge, if he wishes, for reassurance, after the presentation of witnesses, can also make the plaintiff take an oath. Their evidence is this athar: that Ali used to consider the oath along with evidence. And in another narration: from Ibn Sirin that a man claimed a right against a man and presented evidence, then Shuraih wanted to make him take an oath, but he started refusing, so Shuraih said: 'How badly you speak of your witnesses.' Footnotes: (a) The Prophet said in his sermon: 'Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the defendant.' (b) The Prophet decided the oath upon the defendant. (c) The Prophet decided by an oath and a witness. (d) Ali considered it necessary for the plaintiff to take an oath along with a witness. Another narration: from Ibn Sirin... (continued on next page) That a man claimed a right against a man and presented evidence, then Shuraih wanted to make him take an oath, but he started refusing, so Shuraih said: 'You are speaking ill of your witnesses.' (a) The Prophet said to Hadrami: 'Do you have a witness?' He said: 'No.' The Prophet said: 'Then you have the right to his oath.' Another narration: The Prophet said in his sermon: 'Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the defendant.'
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether the oath (yamin) can be transferred to the plaintiff. The base ruling (matn) states: 'The oath is not returned upon the plaintiff.' The commentator (sharh) explains two scenarios: (1) When the plaintiff has only one witness and wishes to supplement it with an oath—this is not permitted according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The plaintiff must either produce two witnesses or demand an oath from the defendant. (2) When the plaintiff already has two witnesses and presents them—again, no oath is required from the plaintiff. The Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'The burden of proof is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the defendant' (al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayh). This divides the roles: the plaintiff brings evidence, the defendant takes the oath. Another hadith states that the Prophet decided by an oath on the defendant. Imam Shafi'i disagrees, citing the hadith 'judgment by oath and one witness' (qada bi yamin wa shahid), allowing the plaintiff to take an oath when he has only one witness. For the second scenario, Shafi'i also allows the judge to impose an oath on the plaintiff even after witnesses, relying on the practice of Ali and Shuraih. The Hanafi response reaffirms that the plaintiff's duty is limited to presenting evidence; oath is exclusively the defendant's duty. The footnote further clarifies the hadith cited, noting the incident of Hadrami and Kindi where the Prophet accepted the plaintiff's evidence and required the defendant's oath, rejecting the defendant's evidence.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): Oath taken by a litigant in court to support his claim or defense.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
مدعي (mudda'i): The plaintiff who brings a claim and bears the burden of proof.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant against whom a claim is made and who takes the oath if the plaintiff lacks evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a plaintiff has only one witness, what can he do to prove his claim?
A) He can take an oath to supplement the witness.
B) He must bring a second witness or demand the defendant's oath.
C) He can request the judge to decide based on his single witness.
D) He can present a documentary evidence instead.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the oath is never transferred to the plaintiff; the plaintiff's sole duty is to present evidence, and the defendant alone bears the obligation of the oath.

Testimony of the Possessor Not Accepted in Absolute Ownership
Ruling with definitions and inter-madhhab dispute on possessor's testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the testimony of the possessor in absolute ownership claims and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In absolute ownership claims, possession does not prove ownership; the plaintiff's testimony is preferred.

When two parties claim ownership of an item without specifying how they acquired it, whose witnesses should the judge accept? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that rejects the possessor's testimony and the Shafi'i disagreement. We will analyze the key terms, the hadith evidence, and the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Define the three key terms
Identify dhu al-yad (possessor/defendant), kharj (non-possessor/plaintiff), and milk mutlaq (absolute ownership claim without stating cause). Understand how these terms frame the dispute.
The passage defines these terms explicitly.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the testimony of the kharj (plaintiff) is accepted over the dhu al-yad in absolute ownership. This is the core ruling.
The matn states: 'لا تقبل بينة صاحب اليد في الملك المطلق'.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The hadith of Hadrami and Kindi: the Prophet accepted Hadrami's testimony (plaintiff) and required an oath from Kindi (possessor). Also the principle 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على المدعى عليه'.
The passage quotes part of the hadith and references Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud.
4
Understand the Shafi'i counter-position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the possessor's testimony is given preference because possession is a stronger indicator of ownership.
This is the opposing school's view.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In Hanafi, judgment is for the plaintiff; in Shafi'i, judgment is for the possessor. Identify which party wins under each school.
This step shows the real-world impact of the disagreement.

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الرابع ۹۱
كتاب الدعوى .
۱۴۰) ولا تقبل بينة صاحب اليد في الملك المطلق.
[۲۷۴۰] (۱۱) نہیں قبول کیا جائے گا قبضے والے کا بینہ ملک مطلق میں۔
یہاں تین اصطلاح ہیں جن کو پہلے سمجھیں ۔ ذی الید : جس کے قبضے میں دعوی کی چیز ہو اس کو ذی الید کہتے ہیں۔ چونکہ اس سے چیز لینے کا مطالبہ ہے اس لئے اس کو مدعی علیہ کہتے ہیں۔ خارج : جس کے قبضے میں دعوی کی چیز نہ ہو اس کو خارج کہتے ہیں۔ چونکہ وہ چیز لینے کا مطالبہ کر رہا ہے اس لئے وہ مدعی بھی ہے۔ ملک مطلق : آدمی دعوی کرے کہ یہ چیز میری ہے لیکن یہ بیان نہ کرے کہ میری ملکیت کس سبب سے ہوئی ہے۔ خریدا ہے یا ہبہ کیا ہے یا اس کے گھر پیدا ہوئی ہے؟ مالک ہونے کے سبب کو بیان نہ کرے تو اس کو ملک مطلق کہتے ہیں۔ اور مالک بننے کا سبب بیان کرے تو اس کو ملک مقید کہتے ہیں۔ صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ خارج اور ذی الید دونوں دعوی کرتے ہیں کہ یہ چیز میری ہے اور میری ہونے کا سبب بیان نہیں کرتے بلکہ ملک مطلق کا دعوی کرتے ہیں۔ پھر دونوں اپنے اپنے گواہ پیش کرتے ہیں تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک خارج کے بینہ کو مانیں گے اور اسی کے مطابق فیصلہ کیا جائے گا۔
چیز کی ملکیت کا سبب بیان نہیں کرتے اس لئے کسی ایک کے ہونے کی وجہ نہیں معلوم ہوسکی۔ اس لئے اب دارو مدار صرف بینہ پر ہوگا (۲) پہلے حدیث گزر چکی ہے کہ مدعی یعنی خارج کے بینہ کا اعتبار ہے۔ کیونکہ ذی السید یعنی مدعی علیہ پر تو قسم ہے نہ کہ اس پر بینہ۔ حضرت حضرمی والی حدیث میں حضرت حضرمی اور کندی دونوں نے زمین کا دعوی کیا اور زمین کس طرح ان کی ہے اس کا سبب بیان نہیں فرمایا یعنی ملک مطلق کا دعوی کیا تو آپ نے حضرت حضرمی یعنی خارج کے بینہ کا اعتبار کیا اور ذی الید یعنی کندی کے بینہ کا اعتبار نہیں کیا بلکہ ان پر قسم لازم کی ۔ حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ ہے۔ فقال النبی الا الله للحضر می الک بینة؟ قال لا! قال فلک یمینه . دوسری روایت میں ہے. ان النبی الله قال في خطبته البينة على المدعى واليمين على المدعى عليه (الف) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی ان البی یہ علی المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیه ص ۲۴۹ نمبر ۱۳۴۱/۱۳۴۰/ ابو داؤد شریف، باب الرجل يحلف علی علمه فیما غاب عنه ص ۱۵۴ نمبر ۳۶۲۳) اس حدیث میں حضرت حضرمی جو خارج بھی ہیں اور مدعی بھی ہیں ان کے بینہ کا اعتبار کیا اور حضرت کندی جو ذی الید ہے جس کے قبضے میں زمین ہے اور مدعی علیہ ہے اس کے بینہ کا اعتبار نہیں کیا بلکہ اس پر ستم لازم کی ۔ اس لئے ہمارے یہاں ملک مطلق میں خارج کے بینہ اور گواہ کو تر جیح دی جائے گی۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ ذی الید یعنی قبضہ والا اور مدعی علیہ کے بینہ کو ترجیح ہوگی۔
Al-Sharh al-Thamiri, Part 4, 91. Book of Claims. 140) And the testimony of the person in possession is not accepted in absolute ownership. [2740] (11) The testimony of the possessor will not be accepted in absolute ownership. Here there are three terms to understand first: Dhu al-yad: the one in whose possession the claimed item is; he is called dhu al-yad. Since the item is demanded from him, he is called the defendant. Kharj: the one not in possession of the claimed item; he is called kharj. Since he is demanding the item, he is also the plaintiff. Milk mutlaq: when a person claims that this item is mine but does not state the cause of ownership—whether he bought it, was gifted it, or it was born in his house; if he does not state the cause of being the owner, this is called milk mutlaq. If he states the cause of ownership, it is called milk muqayyad. The scenario is that both the kharj and dhu al-yad claim that the item is theirs, and they do not state the cause, but rather claim absolute ownership. Then both present their witnesses: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, we accept the testimony of the kharj and decide accordingly. Because the cause of ownership is not stated, it cannot be known which one is correct, so the decision depends solely on the evidence. The previous hadith mentioned states that the plaintiff, i.e., the kharj, his testimony is considered, because the dhu al-yad, i.e., the defendant, only has an oath, not testimony. In the hadith of Hadrami and Kindi, both claimed land, and they did not state the cause of ownership—i.e., they claimed absolute ownership—so the Prophet accepted the testimony of Hadrami, who was the kharj, and did not accept the testimony of Kindi, who was dhu al-yad, but imposed an oath on him. The part of the hadith is: 'The Prophet said to Hadrami: Do you have a witness? He said: No. He said: Then you have his oath.' Another narration: 'The Prophet said in his sermon: The burden of proof is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the defendant.' In this hadith, Hadrami, who is both kharj and plaintiff, his testimony was accepted, and Kindi, who is dhu al-yad, in whose possession the land is, and is the defendant, his testimony was not accepted, but an oath was imposed on him. Therefore, in our madhhab, in absolute ownership, the testimony of the kharj is preferred. Imam Shafi'i says that the testimony of dhu al-yad, i.e., the possessor and defendant, is given preference.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the acceptance of testimony in claims of absolute ownership (milk mutlaq). The ruling (matn) states: 'The testimony of the person in possession (sahib al-yad) is not accepted in absolute ownership.' The commentator defines three key terms: dhu al-yad (the possessor/defendant), kharj (the non-possessor/plaintiff), and milk mutlaq (absolute ownership claim without stating the cause). The scenario: both the kharj and dhu al-yad claim absolute ownership of an item and each presents witnesses. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, only the testimony of the kharj (plaintiff) is accepted. Why? Because since neither states the cause of ownership, the only basis for judgment is the evidence (bayyinah). The hadith of the Prophet with Hadrami and Kindi supports this: both claimed land without stating the cause, and the Prophet accepted the testimony of Hadrami (the kharj) and required an oath from Kindi (the possessor). Thus, the Hanafi position gives priority to the plaintiff's testimony in cases of absolute ownership. Imam Shafi'i disagrees, holding that the testimony of the possessor (dhu al-yad) takes precedence. The commentary closes by reiterating the Hanafi reliance on the hadith and the principle that the plaintiff's evidence is preferred.

Key Terms
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who has physical possession of the claimed item; considered the defendant.
خارج (kharj): The person who is not in possession of the claimed item; considered the plaintiff.
ملك مطلق (milk mutlaq): A claim of ownership without specifying the cause (e.g., purchase, gift, inheritance).
ملك مقيد (milk muqayyad): A claim of ownership with specification of the cause (e.g., 'I bought it').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a claim of absolute ownership (milk mutlaq), whose testimony does Imam Abu Hanifa accept?
A) The testimony of the person in possession (dhu al-yad).
B) The testimony of the person not in possession (kharj).
C) The testimony of both equally.
D) The testimony of neither; only documentary evidence is accepted.

Key Takeaway
In absolute ownership claims, the plaintiff's testimony is accepted over the possessor's because the burden of proof is on the claimant, not the defendant.

The Hanafi Rule: Outsider's Evidence Preferred in Absolute Ownership
Defining terms and stating Hanafi rule with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting evidence in absolute ownership claims and identify the three key terms: dhu al-yad, kharj, and mulk mutlaq.

Core Idea
In absolute ownership claims, the outsider's evidence prevails because both parties have equal footing on cause.

This lesson introduces the foundational Hanafi rule on evidence in ownership disputes where neither party states how they acquired the item. You will learn the definitions of possessor, outsider, and absolute ownership, and see how the Hanafi school derives the rule from the hadith of Hadrami and Kindi.

Guided Path
1
Define the three key terms
Identify dhu al-yad (possessor), kharj (outsider), and mulk mutlaq (absolute ownership). Understand the role of each in a dispute.
The possessor is the defendant; the outsider is the plaintiff.
2
Understand the scenario
Both parties claim absolute ownership (no cause stated) and both produce witnesses. The question is whose evidence is accepted.
3
State the Hanafi rule
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the evidence of the outsider (kharj) is accepted and judgment is given accordingly.
4
Examine the primary proof
The hadith of Hadrami and Kindi: the Prophet accepted the outsider's evidence and required the possessor to take an oath. Also the general principle: al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'i 'alayh.
The hadith is from Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud.
