Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Oath Refusal in Istilad
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on oath in istilad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on oath refusal in istilad and explain the legal reasoning (talil) that underlies each position.

Core Idea
In istilad, refusal to take an oath is not treated as admission according to Abu Hanifa, to avoid legitimizing false paternity.

This lesson examines a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on how to interpret a woman's refusal to take an oath when her husband claims a child is his. The disagreement hinges on whether such refusal constitutes an implicit admission (iqrar) or a legally impermissible voluntary grant of lineage (bazl). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to trace how a legal principle is applied—and contested—within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario of istilad
The husband claims paternity of a child born from his wife. The wife denies. The husband has no evidence. The wife is offered an oath but refuses.
2
State the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa
Imam Abu Hanifa says the oath is not administered; the refusal is seen as a voluntary grant (bazl) of lineage, which is invalid because lineage must reflect reality.
3
State the ruling of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn say the oath may be administered because refusal implies admission (iqrar) that the child is his.
4
Analyze the legal reasoning (talil) of each
Abu Hanifa: lineage cannot be based on a fiction; Sahibayn: implicit admission is valid if the child could be his.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: According to Abu Hanifa, the child's lineage is not established from the husband; according to the Sahibayn, it is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵] الاستيلاد : استیلاد کی صورت یہ ہے کہ شوہر کہے کہ یہ میرا بیٹا ہے۔ کیونکہ میں نے اس کو تم سے پیدا کیا ہے۔ اور عورت انکار کرے۔ شوہر کے پاس بینہ نہ ہو۔ اب عورت کو قسم دے اور وہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کرے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک بذل ہوگا یعنی یہ بچہ آپ کا تو نہیں ہے لیکن چلو آپ سے نسب ثابت کر دیتی ہوں۔ اور نسب ثابت کرنے کا مسئلہ ایسا نہیں ہے کہ بچہ اس کا نہ بھی ہو پھر بھی اس سے نسب ثابت کر دے۔ اس لئے عورت سے استیلاد کے سلسلے میں قسم نہیں کھلائی جائے گی۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک اس کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ میں دبے زبان سے اقرار کرتی ہوں کہ بچہ آپ کا ہی ہے۔ جب بچہ حقیقت میں شوہر کا ہو تو اس سے نسب ثابت کرنے میں کائی حرج نہیں ہے۔
[5] Al-Istilad: The situation of istilad is that the husband says, 'This is my son, because I have fathered him from you.' And the woman denies. The husband has no evidence. Now the woman is given the oath, and if she refuses to take the oath, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it will be brazil (voluntary grant), i.e., 'This child is not yours, but I will establish lineage from you anyway.' And the issue of establishing lineage is not such that if the child is not his, he can still establish lineage from him. Therefore, for istilad, the woman will not be made to take an oath. According to the Sahibayn, the meaning of her refusal is that she implicitly admits that the child is his. When the child is indeed the husband's, there is no harm in establishing lineage from him.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a husband claims paternity of a child and the wife denies, with no witnesses. The key dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn concerns the legal interpretation of the wife's refusal to take an oath. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if she refuses, it cannot be taken as an admission of the child's lineage because lineage is a matter of reality and cannot be established by a voluntary fiction (bazl). Therefore, the oath is not administered to her. The Sahibayn, however, view the refusal as an implicit admission, and since the child might actually be from the husband, the oath can be administered. This difference stems from the principle of whether refusal to take an oath can be considered a quasi-admission in matters of lineage.

Key Terms
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): A claim by a husband that a specific child is his offspring from his wife, with the wife denying it.
بذل (bazl): Voluntary grant or concession; here it means the act of willingly granting lineage to someone, even if the reality is otherwise.
صاحبین (Sahibayn): Refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is a woman not made to take an oath in a claim of istilad?
A) Because she is a woman and cannot take oaths.
B) Because her refusal would imply she is voluntarily granting lineage, which is impermissible.
C) Because she has no evidence.
D) Because the husband must bring two male witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the interpretation of a wife's refusal to swear in a paternity claim is disputed: Abu Hanifa views it as an invalid voluntary grant, while the Sahibayn view it as an admission.

Oath Refusal in Lineage Claims: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath administration in lineage claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on oath refusal in lineage claims and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Refusal to take an oath does not automatically establish lineage; the Hanafi school prioritizes preventing false lineage.

When a man claims another is his son but has no witnesses, the alleged son denies. Should the judge administer an oath? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, known as the Sahibayn) disagreed on the implication of refusal. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the ruling that the alleged son is not made to take an oath. Understand the reasoning: refusal would be interpreted as a voluntary grant of lineage (bazl), which is prohibited by hadith.
The hadith: 'Whoever claims a father other than his own, knowingly, Paradise is forbidden to him' (Bukhari, Muslim).
2
Identify the Sahibayn's position
State that the Sahibayn hold that refusal implies implicit admission. Therefore, the judge can administer an oath to confirm the truth.
Their view treats refusal as evidence of admission.
3
Analyse the proofs
Abu Hanifa relies on the prohibition of false lineage; the Sahibayn rely on the legal maxim that refusal to take an oath is tantamount to admission in civil claims.
Hanafi law distinguishes lineage claims from property claims due to the gravity of lineage.
4
Determine the practical difference
If the alleged son refuses, under Abu Hanifa, lineage is not established; under the Sahibayn, it is established. This affects inheritance, custody, and marital rights.
5
Identify the relied-upon view
Though not explicitly stated in the passage, Abu Hanifa's view is often preferred in lineage cases because of the strong prohibition against false lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲] النسب : ایک آدمی دعوی کرے کہ یہ آدمی میرالڑکا ہے اور میں اس کا باپ ہوں۔ اور وہ اس کا انکار کرے اور دعوی کرنے والے کے پاس گواہ نہ ہو تو اس آدمی کو قسم نہیں کھلائیں گے، کیونکہ قسم سے انکار کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ میں تمہارا بیٹا تو نہیں ہوں لیکن چلو بیٹا بن جاتا ہوں۔ اور بیٹا نہ ہو اور بذل کے طور پر نسب ثابت کر دے تو جائز نہیں ہے۔ حدیث میں منع فرمایا ہے۔ اس لئے نسب کے سلسلے میں لڑکے کو قسم نہیں دی جائے گی۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک قسم سے انکار کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دبے زبان اقرار کر رہا ہے کہ حقیقت میں میں آپ کا بیٹا ہوں اس لئے لڑکے سے قسم لے سکتا ہے۔
[2] Al-Nasab: A man claims that this person is his son and he is his father. And the other denies, and the claimant has no witnesses. Then that person will not be made to take an oath, because refusal of the oath would mean 'I am not your son, but let me become a son.' And if he is not a son, and he establishes lineage by way of brazil, it is not permissible. The hadith has prohibited it. Therefore, in matters of lineage, the boy will not be administered an oath. According to the Sahibayn, the meaning of refusal is that he implicitly admits that in reality he is his son, so the boy can be made to take an oath.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage deals with a claim of lineage where one person says another is his son. If the alleged son denies and no witnesses exist, Imam Abu Hanifa says he is not made to take an oath, because his refusal would be interpreted as a voluntary concession of lineage (bazl), which is invalid. The Sahibayn again view the refusal as an implicit admission, so the oath can be administered. The hadith cited against bazl in lineage underscores the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage or paternity; the legal relationship between father and child.
بذل (bazl): Voluntary grant or concession; here, voluntarily conceding lineage even though one is not actually a son.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the claimant has no witnesses.
B) Because refusal would be a voluntary grant of lineage, which is impermissible.
C) Because the person is free and cannot be forced to take an oath.
D) Because lineage cannot be established without two male witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, lineage is not established by mere refusal to take an oath; the view of Imam Abu Hanifa prevails to prevent false lineage.

Oath Refusal in Claim of Wala': Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Dispute on oath in claim of clientage (wala')

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa prohibits administering an oath in a claim of wala' upon refusal, and distinguish the Sahibayn's position on implicit admission.

Core Idea
A free person cannot be enslaved by consent or legal fiction.

When a claimant seeks to establish wala' (clientage) without evidence and the defendant refuses an oath, a legal dilemma arises: does refusal constitute admission? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the oath cannot be administered because the refusal would imply voluntary enslavement, which is forbidden. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that refusal implies admission and, if the defendant is actually a freed slave, the oath can confirm the truth. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling: the oath is not administered to a defendant who refuses in a claim of wala'. Understand why: because refusal would be interpreted as voluntary acceptance of enslavement, which is impermissible.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's position
State the opposing view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani: refusal implies an implicit admission of the wala' relationship, so the oath can be administered to clarify the truth.
3
Analyse the proof of each side
Abu Hanifa's proof: the principle that a free person cannot be enslaved by consent. The Sahibayn's proof: refusal is akin to admission in other legal contexts; if the defendant is truly a freed slave, the oath can establish the truth.
Both sides rely on analogical reasoning (qiyas) but with different analogies.
4
Examine the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa responds that the prohibition of enslaving a free person overrides any implication from refusal. The potential harm of falsely enslaving a free person is too great to allow the oath.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the defendant refuses, according to Abu Hanifa wala' is not established; according to the Sahibayn it is established. This affects inheritance rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۷] الولاء : آزاد کردہ غلام مر جائے اور اس کا کوئی وارث نہ ہوتو وہ مال آزاد کرنے والے آقا کو ملتا ہے اس مال کو ولاء کہتے ہیں۔ یہاں ولاء کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی دوسرے آدمی پر دعوی کرے کہ یہ میرا آزاد کردہ غلام ہے اور اس کا ولاء مجھے ملے گا۔ وہ اس کو انکار کرے کہ میں اس کا آزاد کردہ غلام نہیں ہوں اور نہ میری ولاء اس کو ملے گی۔ دعوی کرنے والے کے پاس بینہ نہیں ہے۔ اب مدعی علیہ کو قسم کھلائیں اور وہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کر جائے تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ میں تمہارا آزاد کردہ غلام تو نہیں ہوں لیکن چلو بن جاتا ہوں ۔ تو شرعی اعتبار سے ایسا نہیں کر سکتا کیونکہ آزاد جان کو غلام بنانا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے مدعی علیہ کو ولاء کی قسم بھی نہیں کھلا سکتا۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک قسم سے انکار کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ دبے زبان اس کا اقرار کرتا ہوں کہ میں اس کا آزاد کردہ غلام ہوں اور میری ولاء ان کو ملنی چاہئے ۔ اور حقیقت میں آزاد کردہ غلام ہو تو قسم دی جاسکتی ہے۔
[7] Al-Wala': A freed slave dies and has no heir; then his wealth goes to the master who freed him; this wealth is called wala'. The situation of wala' here is that a man claims against another man that he is his freed slave and his wala' belongs to him. The other denies, saying he is not his freed slave and his wala' does not belong to him. The claimant has no evidence. Now the defendant is made to take an oath, and if he refuses to take the oath, then its meaning would be 'I am not your freed slave, but let me become one.' This is not permissible from a legal perspective because it is not allowed to enslave a free person. Therefore, the defendant cannot be made to take an oath regarding wala'. According to the Sahibayn, the meaning of refusal would be that he implicitly admits that he is his freed slave and his wala' belongs to him. And if in reality he is a freed slave, then an oath can be administered.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage discusses a claim where one person says another is his freed slave, entitling him to inheritance rights (wala'). If the defendant denies and no evidence exists, Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the defendant cannot be made to take an oath because refusing the oath would imply he voluntarily accepts being a slave, which is impermissible since enslavement of a free person is forbidden. The Sahibayn again view refusal as an implicit admission, and if the defendant is truly a freed slave, the oath can be administered.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): The right of inheritance belonging to the master who freed a slave when the freed slave dies without heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because wala' is not established by oath.
D) Because the defendant is already free.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the protection of a person's freedom takes precedence over procedural implications from oath refusal in claims of wala'.

Oath Refusal in Hadd Qadhf Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath in qadhf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn both rule that no oath is administered in a claim for hadd qadhf, and distinguish between their two lines of reasoning.

Core Idea
Hudud are dropped by doubt; the body cannot be used as currency for punishment.

When someone claims you owe them a fixed punishment (hadd) for false accusation of adultery (qadhf), and you deny it without witnesses, should the judge make you swear an oath? This passage from Al-Sharh al-Sameri reveals a beautiful intra-Hanafi harmony: Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (the Sahibayn) both say no, but for different reasons. Their difference teaches us deep principles about bodily inviolability and the role of doubt in Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Understand the case: a claimant demands hadd qadhf from a defendant who denies. No witnesses exist. The question is whether the judge should ask the defendant to take an oath (yamin) denying the claim.
This is a procedural question in qada (judicial procedure).
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Imam Abu Hanifa says no oath. First, because if the defendant refuses the oath, it would imply he is willing to be punished, which is impermissible as the body cannot be offered for punishment (the concept of 'ibaha' does not apply to bodily harm). Second, because hudud are dropped by any semblance of doubt (shubha).
The second reason is a fundamental Hanafi principle: avert hudud by doubts.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani also say no oath. Their reasoning: refusal of the oath amounts to an implicit admission that the hadd is due. But implicit admission contains doubt, and hudud are dropped by doubt.
Note the difference: the Sahibayn treat refusal as an admission of sorts, but then apply the doubt principle.
4
Compare the two lines of reasoning
Abu Hanifa's primary reason is that the body is not a fungible thing (mal) that can be voluntarily surrendered. The Sahibayn's reason turns on the very nature of the admission being implicit and therefore doubtful. Both reach the same practical result.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is nil here, but the usul principles differ.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
The rule 'hudud are dropped by doubt' applies in many other hadd cases. For example, if there is any ambiguity in the evidence, or in the defendant's admission, the hadd is not applied. This protects the inviolability of the Muslim's body.
The Hanafi school is known for its expansive application of shubha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۸] الحدود : کوئی آدمی کسی آدمی پر دعوی کرے کہ میرا تمہارے اوپر حد قذف ہے۔ اور دوسرا آدمی اس کا انکار کرے اور دعوی کرنے والے کے پاس گواہی نہیں ہے تو مدعی علیہ کو تم نہیں کھلوائیں گے۔ کیونکہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کر جائے تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ مجھ پر آپ کا حد قذف تو نہیں ہے لیکن چلو اسی کوڑے مارلیں۔ ایسا نہیں کر سکتا کیونکہ جسم ایسی چیز نہیں ہے جس کو بذل کے طور پر کوڑے اور حد لگانے کے لئے پیش کیا جائے۔ دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ حد قذف میں حد کا شائبہ ہے اور حدود شبہ سے بھی ساقط ہو جاتی ہے ۔ اس لئے قسم سے انکار کے بعد بھی حد ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ امام صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ قسم سے انکار کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ دبے زبان اقرار کرتا ہوں کہ مجھ پر حد قذف ہے۔ اور دبے زبان اقرار میں عدم اقرار کا شبہ ہے اور شبہ سے حد ساقط ہو جاتی ہے۔
[8] Al-Hudud: A man claims against another man that he owes him the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of adultery). The other denies, and the claimant has no witnesses. Then the defendant will not be made to take an oath. Because if he refuses to take the oath, its meaning would be 'I do not owe you the hadd, but let me be whipped anyway.' This cannot be done because the body is not a thing that can be offered as a brazil for whipping and punishment. The second reason is that the hadd for qadhf has an element of doubt, and hudud are dropped due to doubt. Therefore, even after the refusal of the oath, the hadd is dropped. Imam Sahibayn say that the meaning of refusal is that he implicitly admits that the hadd for qadhf is due on him. And in an implicit admission, there is doubt about the absence of admission, and due to doubt, the hadd is dropped.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage deals with a claim for the fixed punishment (hadd) for qadhf (false accusation of adultery). If the accused denies and no witnesses exist, Imam Abu Hanifa says the defendant is not made to take an oath. Two reasons: (1) Refusing the oath would be like voluntarily offering one's body for punishment, which is impermissible. (2) Hadd punishments are dropped by the slightest doubt (shubha). The Sahibayn also agree that the hadd is dropped, but their reasoning differs: they say refusal implies an implicit admission, which contains doubt, so the hadd is dropped anyway. The outcome is the same: no oath is administered.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Islamic law for specific crimes (like qadhf, theft, etc.).
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The prescribed punishment of 80 lashes for falsely accusing a chaste person of adultery.
شبہ (shubha): Doubt or semblance that can prevent the application of hadd punishments.
بذل (badhl): Voluntary offering or surrender, especially of something valuable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is the hadd dropped if the defendant refuses the oath?
A) Because the body cannot be offered for punishment.
B) Because the refusal is an implicit admission that contains doubt.
C) Because the claimant has no witnesses.
D) Because qadhf is not a hadd crime.

Key Takeaway
Both Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that no oath is administered in a claim for hadd qadhf without witnesses, grounded in the principle that hudud are averted by doubt.

Claim of Li'an: Oath Refusal
Ruling on oath in claim of li'an by wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a husband must take an oath when his wife claims he accused her of adultery without evidence, and explain the two reasons.

Core Idea
A husband is not made to take an oath in li'an claim without evidence because li'an is like hadd, dropped by doubt.

In this lesson, we examine a specific procedural ruling in the law of li'an (mutual imprecation). When a wife claims her husband accused her of adultery and demands li'an, but has no evidence, the Hanafi school holds that the husband is not made to take an oath. This ruling is based on the unique nature of li'an, which is classified akin to a hadd punishment, and on the logical impossibility of interpreting an oath refusal as voluntary submission to li'an. Understanding this ruling clarifies the strict procedures surrounding accusations of adultery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The wife claims her husband accused her of adultery and demands li'an. The husband denies. The wife has no evidence (bayyinah). This is the specific factual context of the ruling.
2
State the ruling
The husband will not be made to take an oath. The qadi cannot compel an oath in this situation.
This is a procedural prohibition.
3
Understand the first reason
If the husband refuses to take the oath, that refusal cannot be interpreted as voluntary acceptance of li'an ('let me do it anyway'). Such an interpretation is legally impossible because li'an requires specific actions from both parties.
The refusal does not trigger li'an.
4
Understand the second reason
Li'an is legally categorized as akin to a hadd punishment. Hadd is dropped when doubt (shubhah) exists. The husband's refusal creates doubt, so li'an is dropped and the oath is not administered.
This is the primary legal reasoning.
5
Note the agreement
The passage states that this ruling is by agreement (ittifaq) among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. There is no ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school on this point.
This makes the ruling highly authoritative (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹) لعان : اس کی صورت یہ ہے کہ عورت شوہر پر دعوی کرتی ہے کہ آپ نے مجھ پر زنا کی تہمت لگائی ہے اور آپ پر لعان ہے۔ اور شوہر اس کا انکار کرتا ہے۔ عورت کے پاس بینہ نہیں ہے تو شوہر سے قسم نہیں لی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کرے تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ لعان تو نہیں ہے لیکن چلو کر لیتا ہوں اور ایسا کر نہیں سکتا (۲) نیز لعان مرد کی جانب سے حد کے درجے میں ہے اور حد شبہ سے ساقط ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے لعان بھی ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے شوہر سے قسم نہیں لی جائے گی۔ یہ مسئلہ بھی بالا تفاق ہے۔
(9) Li'an: Its situation is that a woman claims against her husband that he has accused her of adultery and that li'an is due upon him. And the husband denies it. The woman has no evidence, so the husband will not be made to take an oath. Because if he refuses to take the oath, its meaning would be 'Li'an is not due, but let me do it anyway,' and this cannot be done. Also, li'an is in the category of hadd from the husband's side, and hadd is dropped due to doubt, so li'an will also be dropped. Therefore, the husband will not be made to take an oath. This issue is by agreement (ittifaq).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses the situation where a wife claims her husband accused her of adultery and demands li'an (the process of mutual imprecation). If the husband denies and the wife has no evidence, Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that the husband is not made to take an oath. Two reasons: (1) Refusing the oath would be like voluntarily submitting to li'an, which is not permissible. (2) Li'an is akin to a hadd punishment, and hadd is dropped by doubt. Since the husband's refusal contains doubt, li'an is dropped. The text notes it is by agreement (ittifaq), meaning no dispute within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
لعان (li'an): A process of mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of adultery without witnesses, leading to separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the husband is the one who initiates li'an.
D) Because the wife's claim is automatically accepted.

Key Takeaway
The husband is not made to take an oath in a li'an claim without evidence because li'an is akin to hadd and dropped by doubt.

Two Claimants with Evidence: Division of Property
Ruling when two claimants bring evidence for the same object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present equal evidence for the same property and explain the prophetic proof.

Core Idea
When evidence is equal and no one possesses the property, divide it equally.

This passage clarifies a key principle in Hanafi qada: when both parties bring equally strong proof, and neither has possession, the judge divides the property between them. The ruling is anchored in the Prophet's own judgment in a similar case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and its ruling
The text addresses two men claiming the same property in a third person's hand, each with two witnesses. The ruling is that the property is split half-and-half.
2
Understand the rationale for equal division
Since both have equally strong evidence and neither has possession, there is no basis to prefer one over the other, so equality is the only fair outcome.
3
Locate the dalil from the Sunnah
The hadith of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari reports that the Prophet divided a camel equally between two claimants who each brought two witnesses.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud.
4
Apply the rule to different possession scenarios
The rule applies the same when both claimants are in possession. However, if one has possession, his evidence takes precedence over an external claimant's proof.
This exception was covered in a previous lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۴۶] (۱۷) اگر دو آدمیوں نے ایک خاص چیز میں دعوی کیا جو دوسرے کے ہاتھ میں ہو۔ ہر ایک گمان کرتے ہوں کہ اس کی ہے اور دونوں نے بعینہ قائم کیا تو فیصلہ کیا جائے گا دونوں کے درمیان۔ ایک چیز تیسرے آدمی کے ہاتھ میں ہے اور دو آدمی اس کا دعوی کر رہے ہیں اور دونوں نے دو دو گواہ پیش کر دیئے تو دونوں کے لئے آدھی آدھی چیز کا فیصلہ کیا جائے گا۔ جب دونوں کے پاس بینہ ہیں اور کسی کے قبضے میں نہیں تو کوئی وجہ ترجیح نہیں ہے اس لئے دونوں کے لئے آدھی آدھی چیز ہوگی (۱) حدیث میں ایسا ہی فیصلہ ہے۔ عن أبي موسى الاشعرى ... ان رجلين ادعيا بعيرا على عهد النبي الله فبعث كل واحد منهما شاهدين فقسمه النبي الا الله بينهما نصفين (الف) ( ابو داؤد شریف ، باب الرجلین یدعیان هیئا ولیس بینھما بیته ص ۱۵۳ نمبر ۳۶۱۵) اس حدیث میں آپ نے آدھے آدھے اونٹ کا فیصلہ فرمایا۔ اس لئے دونوں کے لئے آدھے آدھے ہوں گے۔ اگر دونوں کے قبضے میں ہو تب بھی دونوں کے لئے آدھا آدھا فیصلہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر ایک کے قبضے میں ہو تو خارج کے بینہ کا اعتبار ہوگا پہلے گزر چکا ہے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضور کے زمانے میں دو آدمیوں نے ایک اونٹ کا دعوی کیا۔ پس ہر ایک نے دو دو گواہ بھیجے تو حضور نے اونٹ کو آدھا آدھا تقسیم فرمایا۔
[2746] (17) If two men claim a specific thing in the hand of another, each thinking it is his, and both establish proof (bayyinah), then a judgment will be given between them. A thing is in the hand of a third person, and two men claim it, and both produce two witnesses each, then the thing will be judged half-and-half for both. When both have proof and neither has possession, there is no reason for preference, so each gets half. This is what is decided in the hadith: from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari... that two men claimed a camel during the time of the Prophet ﷺ, and each brought two witnesses, so the Prophet divided the camel between them equally. (Reference: Abu Dawud, chapter on two men claiming an animal with no evidence between them, p. 153, no. 3615) In this hadith, the Prophet decided half-and-half for the camel. So for them, it will be half-and-half. If both are in possession, the judgment is also half-and-half. If one is in possession, the proof of the external one is considered, as passed earlier. Footnote: (a) During the Prophet's time, two men claimed a camel, and each brought two witnesses, so the Prophet divided the camel half-and-half.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when two claimants both bring evidence for the same property in a third person's hand. Since their evidence is equally strong and there is no basis to prefer one over the other, the property is divided equally between them. The ruling is supported by a hadith where the Prophet divided a camel between two claimants with equal witnesses. The passage also notes that if one claimant is in possession, his evidence prevails over an external claimant's.

Key Terms
بینہ (bayyinah): During the Prophet's time, two men claimed a camel and each brought two witnesses, so the Prophet divided the camel half-and-half.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The property is given to the third person.
D) The property is escheated to the state.

Key Takeaway

Two Claimants for Marriage of One Woman: Hanafi Judicial Ruling
Ruling when two men claim marriage to the same woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two men each claim marriage to the same woman and both bring proof, and identify the decisive role of the woman's confirmation.

Core Idea
A woman cannot be given in marriage to two men; when proof is equal, her confirmation decides.

This passage from a Hanafi text addresses a peculiar Qada (judicial) scenario: two men both claim to have married the same woman and each produces a valid proof (bayyinah). The judge cannot divide the woman like property, nor can he give preference arbitrarily. The solution, as stated, is to refer to the woman's own confirmation. This lesson explains this ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal problem
Two men each claim marriage to one woman, each with a valid proof (bayyinah). The problem is that a woman cannot be the wife of two men simultaneously.
This is a qadi's dilemma.
2
State the incapacity to divide a woman in marriage
Unlike property, a woman is not divisible; she cannot be given half to each claimant. This is the fundamental principle.
The text says 'they cannot be given half a wife each'.
3
Explain why the judge cannot give preference without basis
Both proofs are equal in strength, so there is no ground for tarjih (preference). The judge cannot choose arbitrarily.
The passage is incomplete but suggests no preference is possible.
4
Describe the recourse to the woman's confirmation
The judge refers to the woman herself to confirm which marriage she acknowledges. Her statement (tasdiq) decides the valid husband.
This respects her personal status and avoids an impasse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۴۷] (۱۸) اور اگر دعوی کیا ہر ایک نے عورت کے نکاح کا اور دونوں نے بعینہ قائم کیا تو فیصلہ نہیں کیا جائے گا کسی کے بعینہ پر رجوع کیا جائے گا عورت کی تصدیق کی طرف دونوں میں سے کسی ایک کے لئے۔ ایک عورت دو آدمیوں کی بیوی نہیں ہوسکتی اس لئے دونوں نے مبینہ قائم کیا تو دونوں کو آدھی آدھی بیوی نہیں دی جاسکتی۔ اور ترجیح کی کوئی
[2747] (18) And if each claims the marriage of a woman and both establish proof (bayyinah), then no judgment will be given, and recourse will be had to the woman's confirmation for one of the two. A woman cannot be the wife of two men, therefore even if both have established proof, they cannot be given half a wife each. And there is no preference...
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where two men both claim to have married the same woman and each brings evidence. Since a woman cannot be married to two men simultaneously, the judge cannot divide her. Unlike property, a woman is not divisible. The text indicates that the judge refers to the woman herself to confirm which marriage is valid. The passage is incomplete, but the ruling is clear: no division and the woman's affirmation is sought.

Key Terms
نکاح (nikah): Marriage contract under Islamic law.
تصدیق (tasdiq): Confirmation or affirmation by the woman regarding her marriage.
بینہ (bayyinah): Proof, usually two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Divide the woman's time between them.
B) Give the woman to the first claimant.
C) Ask the woman for confirmation (tasdiq).
D) Annul both marriages.

Key Takeaway
When two men each claim marriage to the same woman with equal proof, the woman's own confirmation settles the matter.

Equal Evidence in Competing Claims: Half-and-Half Option
Ruling on two claimants each proving purchase of same slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present equal evidence for the same property and identify the option given to each claimant.

Core Idea
When evidence is equal and no priority exists, each claimant receives the option to take half the property for half the price.

This passage addresses a common judicial dilemma: two individuals each claim to have purchased the same slave from the same seller, and both produce valid evidence without any indication of priority (such as date). The Hanafi school provides a clear, equitable solution rooted in prophetic practice. Understanding this ruling is essential for appreciating how the school resolves competing claims when evidence is perfectly balanced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Two buyers each claim they bought the same slave from the same seller. Both present evidence (bayyinah) – typically two male witnesses each.
The scenario assumes no difference in date or any other basis for preference between the two claims.
2
Determine the condition of equal evidence
The evidence is equally strong and there is no sign (e.g., earlier date, witness credibility) to prefer one over the other. The judge cannot favor one arbitrarily.
This condition triggers the ruling of equal division.
3
State the ruling: option to take half or leave
Each claimant is given an option (khiyar): either take half the slave for half the price, or completely abandon the claim. If both take half, they each become owners of half and must pay half the price.
The option is a judicial ruling; the claimants may also waive their claim entirely.
4
Note the proof from hadith
The Prophet (ﷺ) divided a camel equally between two claimants who each had two witnesses, setting the precedent for equal division when evidence is equal.
This hadith is cited from Abu Dawud and is the primary dalil for this ruling.
5
Recognize the underlying principle
The principle is that when evidence is balanced and no tarjih (preference) is possible, the property is split equally (nisfan). This avoids injustice and follows the prophetic model.
This principle applies generally in Hanafi qada for competing claims with equal proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى اثنان كل واحد منهما انه اشترى منه هذا العبد و اقاما البينة فكل واحد منهما بالخيار ان شاء اخذ نصف العبد بنصف الثمن وان شاء ترک [۲۷۴۸] (۱۹) اگر دعوی کیا دو آدمیوں نے کہ ان میں سے ہر ایک نے خریدا ہے اس غلام کو فلاں سے، اور دونوں نے بینہ قائم کیا تو دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کو اختیار ہے کہ اگر چاہے تو آدھا غلام آدھی قیمت میں خرید لے اور اگر چاہے تو چھوڑ دے۔ مثلا زید اور عمر دونوں کہتے ہیں کہ خالد غلام کو ساجد بائع سے خریدا ہے۔ اور دونوں نے بعینہ پیش کیا اور دونوں نے مقدم مؤخر تاریخ کا بینہ پیش نہیں کیا بلکہ دونوں کی تاریخ بھی ایک ہی ہے۔ اب چونکہ کسی ایک کے بینہ کو ترجیح نہیں ہے اس لئے یہ فیصلہ کیا جائے گا کہ آدھا آدھا غلام دونوں نے خریدا ہے اور دونوں پر آدھی آدھی قیمت لازم ہوگی۔ اب چاہے تو آدمی قیمت دے کر آدھا غلام لے اور چاہے تو چھوڑ دے۔ او پر حدیث گزر چکی ہے کہ دونوں کے بینہ برابر ہوں اور ترجیح کے لئے کوئی علامت نہ ہو تو دونوں کو آدھا آدھا دیا جائے گا۔ عن ابی موسى الاشعرى... ان رجلين ادعيا بعيرا على عهد النبي الا الله فبعث كل واحد منهما شاهدين فقسمه النبي الا الله بينهما نصفين (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف ، باب الرجلین یدعیان هیا ولیس لهما بی ص ۱۵۳ نمبر ۳۶۱۵) اس حدیث میں دونوں کو آدھا آدھا دیا گیا۔
If two men each claim that he bought this slave from him, and both produce evidence, then each has the option: if he wishes, he may take half the slave for half the price, and if he wishes, he may leave it. (19) If two men claim that each of them bought this slave from so-and-so, and both produce evidence, then each has the option: if he wishes, he may take half the slave for half the price, and if he wishes, he may leave it. For example, Zayd and 'Umar both say that they bought the slave Khalid from the seller Sajid. Both produce the same evidence and neither presents evidence of an earlier or later date, and their dates are the same. Since no one's evidence is given preference, it is decided that each bought half the slave and each is liable for half the price. Then each may either pay the price and take half the slave or leave it. There is a hadith that when two people's evidence is equal and there is no sign for preference, they are given half each. From Abu Musa al-Ash'ari... that two men claimed a camel during the time of the Prophet (ﷺ), so each sent two witnesses, and the Prophet divided the camel between them in half. (Abu Dawud, chapter on two men claiming an animal with no evidence, p. 153, no. 3615). In this hadith, both were given half each.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling in the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa). The case involves two individuals who each claim to have purchased the same slave from the same seller, and both present valid evidence (bayyinah) for their claim, but without any indication of which purchase occurred first (no dates or the same dates). Since the evidence is equally strong and there is no basis for preference (tarjih), the judge offers each claimant an option: either take half the slave for half the price, or waive the claim entirely. The Urdu commentary elaborates that the judge adjudicates that each bought half the slave and must pay half the price. The hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari illustrates the principle: the Prophet (ﷺ) divided the camel equally between two claimants who each had two witnesses. This is a standard Hanafi ruling: in cases of competing claims with equal evidence and no priority, the disputed property is split equally (nisfan).

Key Terms
بينه (bayyinah): Evidence; in this context, two witnesses testifying to the claim.
اختيار (ikhtiyar): Option; each claimant has the choice to take half or leave the claim entirely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is given to the one who first filed the claim.
B) Each claimant may take half the slave for half the price, or leave it.
C) The judge decides based on the trustworthiness of witnesses.
D) The slave is confiscated by the state.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, equal evidence with no tarjih leads to an equal share option, based on the Prophet's division of the camel.

Rejection of Adjudicated Share Does Not Transfer to Other Claimant
Effect of one claimant rejecting his share after judge's division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one claimant rejects his adjudicated share and explain why the other claimant cannot take the whole.

Core Idea
Rejection of an adjudicated share does not transfer it to the other claimant; a fresh sale from the seller is required.

This lesson examines a scenario where a judge divides a disputed property equally, then one claimant rejects his half. The Hanafi school clarifies that the rejection does not automatically give the other claimant the entire property. Instead, the rejected half remains with the seller, and the other claimant may only acquire it through a new purchase. This ruling underscores the binding nature of the judge's adjudication and the principle that a sale is executed per the judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
After the judge divides the slave equally, one claimant says 'I do not choose' (rejects his half). The question: can the other claimant take the whole slave? The ruling is no.
The act here is the rejection, and the ruling is a prohibition on the other taking the whole.
2
Analyze the legal effect of qada'
The judge's adjudication (qada') created a sale of half the slave to each claimant. This sale is binding and considered executed.
In Hanafi law, a judge's ruling is final and creates legal consequences unless contradictory to clear texts.
3
Interpret the rejection statement
The phrase 'la akhtar' is a simple refusal to take the awarded share. It is not a transfer of rights or a form of divorce or manumission.
Rejection does not nullify the judge's judgment or transfer ownership to the other claimant.
4
Determine the consequence
The rejected half remains with the seller (the original owner). The other claimant may only acquire that half by buying it anew from the seller.
A fresh sale is required because the original sale was only for half to each claimant.
5
Apply the ruling to the footnote
The footnote cites a hadith where the Prophet divided a camel equally between two claimants, supporting that division is the proper course and rejection does not transfer the share.
The hadith is used as evidence for the permissibility of the division, not for the rejection ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان قضی القاضي به بينهما فقال احدهما لا اختار لم يكن للآخر ان ياخذ جميعه [۲۷۴۹] (۲۰) پس اگر قاضی نے اس چیز کا دونوں کے درمیان فیصلہ کیا، پس ان میں سے ایک نے کہا کہ نہیں لیتا ہوں تو دوسرے کے لئے جائز نہیں کہ لے کل کو ۔ قاضی نے دونوں کے لئے آدھے آدھے غلام کا فیصلہ کیا۔ اب ایک فریق کہتا ہے کہ میں آدھا غلام نہیں لوں گا تو اب دوسرے فریق کو حق نہیں ہے کہ پورا غلام لے لے، ہاں نئے سرے سے باقی آدھے غلام کو بائع سے خریدے گا تو لے سکتا ہے۔ جب دوسرے فریق کے لئے آدھے غلام کا فیصلہ ہوا تو اس کے لئے آدھے غلام کی ہی بیع ہوئی پورے غلام کی بیچ نہیں ہوئی۔ اس لئے پورا حاشیہ : (الف) حضور کے زمانے میں دو آدمیوں نے ایک اونٹ کا دعوی کیا ، پس دونوں میں سے ہر ایک نے دو دو گواہ بھیجے تو حضور نے اونٹ کو آدھا آدھا تقسیم کر دیا۔
If the judge adjudicates it between them, then one of them says, 'I do not choose,' it is not permissible for the other to take all of it. (20) So if the judge adjudicated half the slave to each, and one party says, 'I will not take half the slave,' then the other party does not have the right to take the entire slave; rather, he may buy the remaining half from the seller anew. When half the slave was adjudicated to the second party, only half the slave was sold to him, not the entire slave. Therefore, the whole... Footnote: In the time of the Prophet, two men claimed one camel, each sent two witnesses, and the Prophet divided the camel between them half and half.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This ruling follows the previous one. The judge has already divided the slave equally between the two claimants as a binding adjudication (qada'). Now, after the division, one of the claimants says, 'I do not choose' (i.e., he rejects his half). The question: can the other claimant now take the entire slave? The Hanafi ruling is clear: the other cannot take the whole slave. The judge's judgment created a sale (bay') of half the slave to each claimant. Rejection by one does not transfer his half to the other; the rejected half remains in the ownership of the seller (ba'i'). The other claimant may only acquire that half by a fresh purchase from the seller. The Urdu commentary explains that when the judge adjudicated, only half the slave was sold to each, not the whole. So the other claimant's right is limited to his own half. The footnote reiterates the hadith from the previous lesson.

Key Terms
قضی (qada): The judge's binding adjudication; his ruling is considered final and creates legal effects.
لا اختار (la akhtar): I do not choose; a statement of rejection of the adjudicated share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a judge divides a disputed slave equally between two claimants, one claimant says 'I do not choose' (rejects his half). What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The slave goes entirely to the other claimant.
B) The slave reverts to the seller, and both claimants lose their right.
C) The other claimant may take the other half only by buying it anew from the seller.
D) The judge must retract his judgment and start over.

Key Takeaway
When a judge divides property between claimants, rejection by one does not automatically transfer the share to the other; it must be purchased from the seller.

Priority by Earlier Date of Purchase
Ruling when both claimants provide a purchase date

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when two claimants each provide a date of purchase, the property is awarded to the one with the earlier date, and cite the hadith proof from Samurah ibn Jundub.

Core Idea
The earlier purchase prevails because the seller no longer owned the property for the later sale.

This lesson addresses a common property dispute: two buyers each claim to have purchased the same item from the same seller, and both present witnesses and a specific date. How does a qadi decide? Hanafi law gives a clear rule backed by a prophetic hadith. Understanding this requires grasping the logic of ownership transfer and the weight given to chronological priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two claimants (buyers) each assert they bought the same slave (or any property) from the same seller, and both produce witnesses and a date (tārīkh) for their transaction.
The seller is not a party in this dispute; only the two buyers present their claims.
2
State the ruling
The qadi awards the entire property to the claimant whose date is earlier. The later buyer receives nothing.
This is a 'first in time, first in right' principle.
3
Explain the rationale
At the earlier date, the other claimant was not a competing buyer, so the sale was valid and ownership transferred. The second sale is invalid because the seller no longer owned the property.
The seller's ownership ends at the moment of the first valid sale.
4
Cite the dalil
The hadith from Samurah ibn Jundub (may Allah be pleased with him) states: 'If a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first one.' This is reported in Ibn Majah.
The hadith is a clear textual proof (dalīl) for this ruling.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, the qadi examines the dates on the evidence. If both have valid dates, the earlier one wins. If only one provides a date, that one wins. If neither provides a date, other factors (like possession) are considered.
The date of the transaction, not the date of filing the lawsuit, determines priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ذكر كل واحد منهما تاريخا فهو للاول [۲۷۵۰] (۲۱) اگر دونوں فریق میں سے ہر ایک نے تاریخ ذکر کی تو وہ مقدم تاریخ والے کو ملے گا۔ دونوں فریقوں نے غلام پر دعوی کیا کہ میں نے فلاں سے پورا پورا غلام خریدا ہے۔ اور دونوں نے گواہ پیش کئے اور تاریخ بھی بتائی تو جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہے پورا غلام اسی کا ہوگا۔ جس مقدم تاریخ میں ایک نے خریدنے کا دعوی کیا اس تاریخ میں دوسرا فریق مزاحم نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کی بیع ہوگئی اور غلام اس کا ہو گیا۔ اب دوسرے کی بیع نہیں ہوگی (۲) حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن سمرة بن جندب عن النبي الله قال اذا بيع البيع من رجلين فالبيع للاول (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب اشتراط الخلاص ص ۱۳۳۵ نمبر ۲۳۴۴، کتاب الاحکام ) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ جس سے پہلے بیچ بیچ اس سے ہو گئی۔ اس لئے جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہوئی بیج اس سے ہو گئی۔ اس لئے اب دوسرے کے لئے نہیں ہوگی۔
If each of them mentions a date, then it (the property) belongs to the earlier one. (21) If each of the two parties mentions a date, then it is given to the one with the earlier date. Both parties claimed the slave: 'I bought the entire slave from so-and-so.' Both presented witnesses and also gave a date. Then whoever's date is earlier gets the entire slave. At the earlier date when one claimed the purchase, the other party is not contesting, so the sale was completed and the slave became his. Now the other's sale will not occur. There is proof in the hadith: from Samurah ibn Jundub from the Prophet (ﷺ) who said: 'If a sale is made to two men, then the sale is for the first one.' (Ibn Majah, book of rulings, no. 2344). In this hadith, the first one to buy gets it. So the earlier date prevails.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This ruling addresses the situation where two claimants both produce evidence and also specify the date (tārīkh) of their alleged purchase. The Hanafi law gives the entire property to the claimant whose date is earlier. The rationale is that at the earlier date, the other claimant was not a competing buyer, so the sale from the seller to the first buyer is valid and transfers ownership. The second purchase would be invalid because the seller no longer owned the slave. The hadith from Samurah ibn Jundub clearly states: 'If a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first one.' This hadith is used as evidence (dalil) for this ruling.

Key Terms
تاريخ (tarikh): Date; the specific date when the purchase was allegedly made, used to determine priority.
اول (awwal): The first; the one with the earliest date gets the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The one who first produced witnesses.
B) The one with the earlier date of purchase.
C) Both get half each.
D) The judge decides by the credibility of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The earlier date of purchase decides ownership when both claimants produce witnesses and a date.

Priority by Possession When No Date
Ruling when no date but one claimant has possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on property disputes when neither claimant provides a date but one has possession, and explain the evidentiary basis from the hadith.

Core Idea
When two claimants have equal evidence but no dates, possession tips the scale.

In property disputes with competing claims, dates often resolve priority. But what if neither party gives a date? Hanafi law looks to physical possession as a strong indicator of prior valid ownership. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof, showing how the judge awards the item to the claimant in possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute Scenario
Two claimants each present evidence (bayyina) that the disputed property, e.g. a slave, belongs to them, but neither mentions a date of purchase.
If a date were given, the earlier date would decide.
2
Recognise the Decisive Factor: Possession
One claimant currently holds physical possession (qabd) of the property. Hanafi law treats possession as a sign of prior valid transaction.
Possession is not proof of ownership per se, but a strong indicator.
3
Understand the Reasoning
Possession suggests that the holder purchased it earlier and completed the sale; otherwise, the seller would not have delivered it.
This reasoning is based on customary practice (urf).
4
Consider the Hadith Proof
The Prophet (ﷺ) adjudicated a disputed animal to the one in whose hand it was, even though both produced evidence. This is the primary dalil.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Bayhaqi and is ahad (single-chain).
5
Apply the Ruling Practically
In a court, the judge inspects possession; if one party has it and dates are absent, he awards the property to that party.
This is a preference (tarjih) based on qabd, not a definitive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان لم يذكر تاريخا ومع احدهما قبض فهو اولی به [۲۷۵۱] (۲۲) اور اگر دونوں نے تاریخ ذکر نہیں کی اور ان میں سے ایک کا قبضہ ہے تو وہ اولی ہوگا۔ اگر تاریخ ذکر کرتا تو دیکھا جاتا کہ کس کی تاریخ مقدم ہے اس کے لئے غلام کا فیصلہ کرتے لیکن تاریخ کسی نے ذکر نہیں کی ۔ البتہ ایک فریق کا غلام پر قبضہ بھی ہے تو قبضہ کرنا اس بات کی علامت ہے کہ اس نے پہلے خریدی ہے تب ہی تو اس کا قبضہ ہے، اور اس کے ساتھ بیع تام ہو چکی ہے تب ہی تو غلام اس کے قبضے میں ہے۔ اس لئے غلام کا قبضہ اس کے لئے ہوگا (۲) حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن جابر بن عبد الله ان رجلين تداعيا بدابة فاقام كل واحد منهما البينة انها دابته فقضى بها رسول الله الا الله الذي هي في يديه (ب) (سنن بیہقی ، باب المتد اعین میتازعان شیما فی ید احدهما و يتقیم کل واحد منهما علی ذلک بیت ج عاشر ص ۲۳۳ نمبر ۲۱۲۲۳) اس حدیث میں جس کا قبضہ تھا اس کو ایک گونہ ترجیح تھی اس لئے حضور نے جانور کا فیصلہ اسی کے لئے فرمایا۔
If they do not mention a date, and one of them has possession, then he is more entitled to it. (22) If both did not mention a date, and one of them has possession of the slave, then he is preferred. If they had mentioned a date, we would see whose date is earlier and decide accordingly, but no one mentioned a date. However, one party has possession of the slave. Possession is a sign that he purchased it earlier, because only then would he have possession, and the sale would have been completed. Therefore, the slave will go to the one in possession. There is proof in the hadith: from Jabir ibn Abdullah that two men disputed over an animal, and each produced evidence that it was his animal, so the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) adjudicated it to the one in whose hand it was. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on two disputants, no. 21223). In this hadith, the one in possession was given priority, so the Prophet adjudicated the animal to him.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This ruling covers the situation where two claimants each present evidence but neither gives a date (tarīkh). In such a case, Hanafi law looks to physical possession (qabd). The claimant who is in possession of the disputed item is preferred (awla) and the property is awarded to him. The reasoning is that possession is a strong indicator that a valid sale occurred earlier, as people do not normally give possession without a valid transaction. The hadith from Jabir supports this: the Prophet adjudicated the animal to the one who had it in his hand. Note that this is a preference (tarjih) based on possession; it does not mean the other claimant's evidence is false, but the judge decides based on the available indication.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Physical possession; holding the property.
اولی (awla): More entitled; preferred; the one in possession is more entitled to the property.
تاريخ (tarikh): Date of transaction; used to determine priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men both claim a slave and present evidence, but neither mentions a date. However, one of them has the slave in his possession. According to Hanafi law, who gets the slave?
A) Both get half.
B) The one in possession is preferred.
C) The judge must ask for a date before deciding.
D) The slave is confiscated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when two claimants have equal evidence and no date, physical possession (qabd) breaks the tie and the property is awarded to the possessor.

Preference of Purchase over Gift in Judicial Claims
Ruling when one claims purchase and other claims gift, both with possession and evidence but no dates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when two claimants both have possession and evidence but no dates, the purchase claim is preferred over the gift claim, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
When possession and dates are equal, purchase prevails over gift because it involves exchange of value.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, disputes over property ownership often arise between claimants. This passage addresses a specific scenario: two individuals each claim the same property, one alleging a purchase and the other a gift, with both in possession and both producing evidence but lacking transaction dates. The ruling establishes that the purchase claim takes priority, reflecting the law's inclination toward commutative contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify claimants and claims
Determine who is claiming what: one party claims purchase (shira'), the other claims gift (hiba). Both have possession (qabd) and evidence (bayyinah).
The property is the same in both claims.
2
Confirm the common conditions
Both have taken possession and both have evidence. Neither has a date for the transaction. These conditions trigger the specific rule.
If a date existed, that would be the decisive factor.
3
Apply the preference rule
State that the purchase claim is preferred (awla) over the gift claim. The judge awards the property to the purchaser.
4
Understand the rationale
Purchase is a commutative contract involving consideration (thaman), making it stronger than a gift, which is gratuitous. Hanafi law treats commutative contracts as more credible.
5
Recognize limitations
This rule only applies when both have possession, both have evidence, and no dates are available. If dates exist, the earlier transaction prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى احدهما شراء والآخر هبة وكل واحد منهما قبض واقام البينة ولا تاريخ لهما فالشراء اولی من الهبة [۲۷۵۲] (۲۳) اگر ایک نے خریدنے کا دعوی کیا اور دوسرے نے ہبہ کا اور دونوں نے قبضہ کیا اور دونوں نے بینہ قائم کیا اور دونوں کے پاس تاریخ نہیں ہے تو خرید اولی ہو گی دوسرے سے۔ دو آدمی دعوی کر رہے ہیں کہ فلاں سے لیا لیکن ایک آدمی دعوی کرتا ہے کہ فلاں سے خریدا ہے اور دوسرا دعوی کرتا ہے کہ فلاں نے مجھے ہبہ کیا ہے۔ اور دونوں نے قبضہ بھی کر لیا۔ اور اپنے اپنے دعوی پر بینہ پیش کیا۔ لیکن کسی کے پاس خرید نے اور ہبہ کرنے کی تاریخ نہیں ہے کہ کس نے پہلے خرید ہے اور کس کو بعد میں ہبہ کیا ہے تو ایسی صورت میں خریدنے کو ترجیح ہوگی اور مال خریدنے والے کا ہوگا ہبہ کئے ہوئے کا نہیں ہوگا۔
If one claims purchase and the other claims gift, and each has possession and has produced evidence, and there is no date for either, then the purchase is preferred over the gift. (23) If one claims purchase and the other claims gift, and both have taken possession and both have produced evidence, and neither has a date, then the purchase is preferred over the other. Two men claim to have taken from so-and-so: one says he bought it, the other says he was given it as a gift. Both have taken possession and both present evidence, but neither has a date for when the purchase or gift occurred. In this situation, the purchase claim is given preference, and the property belongs to the purchaser, not the donee.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This ruling addresses a situation where two claimants have possession of the same property, each provides evidence, but one claims purchase (shira') and the other claims gift (hiba). Neither claimant can provide a date. Hanafi law gives preference (awla) to the purchase claim over the gift claim. The rationale is that purchase involves an exchange of value (thaman), making it a stronger transaction than a gift, which is gratuitous. Presumption (istishab) may also support the purchase as a more probable acquisition. Additionally, the seller is more likely to have transferred ownership to a buyer than to a donee if there is doubt. So the property is awarded to the claimant who asserts purchase. Note that this ruling applies only when both have possession and evidence and no dates; otherwise, other rules (e.g., earlier date) would apply.

Key Terms
شراء (shira'): Purchase; acquisition of property in exchange for price.
هبة (hiba): Gift; gratuitous transfer of ownership without exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling, when two claimants both have possession and evidence but no dates, which claim is given preference?
A) Gift claim
B) Purchase claim
C) Neither; property is divided
D) The date of the evidence decides

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when possession and evidence are equal and dates are missing, the purchase claim prevails over the gift claim because purchase is a stronger transaction.

Priority by Date in Mutual Claims
Ruling on priority when both claimants provide dates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on priority when both claimants provide dates and explain how the hadith supports this ruling.

Core Idea
When both claimants have dates, the earlier sale prevails—the first buyer owns the item.

Consider a dispute where two people each claim to have bought the same item from the same seller on different dates, and both produce witnesses and a documented date. How does a judge decide? This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling: the earlier date determines ownership. We will examine the legal reasoning and the prophetic hadith that serves as its foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states: 'If each of them mentions a date, then it belongs to the earlier one.' This is the primary rule: the earlier date gives the item to that claimant.
The ruling applies only when both parties present a date (tarikh) as part of their evidence.
2
Understand the condition of the scenario
Both claimants have witnesses (bayyinah) and specific dates. The absence of a date or possession changes the ruling, as indicated by adjacent statements in the text.
If one party has possession and no dates, possession gives priority.
3
Locate the hadith proof
The commentator cites the hadith: 'If a thing is sold to two men, the sale is for the first.' This is a clear textual evidence (dalil naqli) from the Prophet (peace be upon him).
The hadith is narrated by Samurah ibn Jundub and recorded in Ibn Majah.
4
Apply the proof to the ruling
The word 'al-awwal' (the first) directly indicates priority by sequence. Since the earlier date corresponds to the first sale, that claimant's ownership is established, and the later sale is invalid because the item was no longer owned by the seller.
The Hanafi school treats this hadith as zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalala—its meaning is definitive.
5
Consider the practical consequence
The ruling leads to clear adjudication: the judge awards the entire item to the earlier‑date claimant. The later claimant has no claim, since the sale could not have taken place.
This prevents endless disputes and aligns with the principle of certainty in contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ذكر كل واحد منهما تاريخا فهو للاول [2750] (21) وان ذكر كل واحد منهما تاريخا فهو للاول [2751] (22) وان لم يذكر تاريخا ومع احدهما قبض فهو اولی به [2752] (23) وان ادعى احدهما شراء غلام نہیں لے سکتا ہے۔ باقی کے لئے از سر نو بیع کرنی ہوگی۔ اصول قاضی کا فیصلہ جتنے غلام کے لئے ہوا بیج اتنے ہی غلام کی ہوگی۔ [2750] (21) اگر دونوں فریق میں سے ہر ایک نے تاریخ ذکر کی تو وہ مقدم تاریخ والے کو ملے گا۔ دونوں فریقوں نے غلام پر دعوی کیا کہ میں نے فلاں سے پورا پورا غلام خریدا ہے۔ اور دونوں نے گواہ پیش کئے اور تاریخ بھی بتائی تو جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہے پورا غلام اسی کا ہوگا۔ جس مقدم تاریخ میں ایک نے خریدنے کا دعوی کیا اس تاریخ میں دوسرا فریق مزاحم نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کی بیع ہوگئی اور غلام اس کا ہو گیا۔ اب دوسرے کی بیع نہیں ہوگی (۲) حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن سمرة بن جندب عن النبي الله قال اذا بيع البيع من رجلين فالبيع للاول (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب اشتراط الخلاص ص ۱۳۳۵ نمبر ۲۳۴۴، کتاب الاحکام ) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ جس سے پہلے بیچ بیچ اس سے ہو گئی۔ اس لئے جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہوئی بیج اس سے ہو گئی۔ اس لئے اب دوسرے کے لئے نہیں ہوگی۔
And if each of them mentions a date, then it belongs to the earlier one. [2750] (21) If both parties each mention a date, then it will be given to the one with the earlier date. The two parties claimed the slave, each saying 'I bought the entire slave from so-and-so.' Both brought witnesses and also gave a date, then the one with the earlier date will get the entire slave. Since at the earlier date one claimed to have bought, the other party was not opposing at that date, so his sale took place and the slave became his. Now the other's sale will not occur. (2) There is proof in the hadith: From Samurah ibn Jundub from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'If a thing is sold to two men, the sale is for the first.' (Ibn Majah, Kitab al-Ahkam) This hadith states that whoever sells first, the sale is for him. Therefore, the one with the earlier date, the sale was for him, so now it will not be for the other.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Claims. When two people both claim to have purchased the same item and each provides a witness with a specific date, the earlier date determines ownership. The reasoning is that at the earlier date, the other claimant was not in contention, so the sale was valid and ownership transferred. The text cites a hadith from Samurah ibn Jundub where the Prophet ruled that if a thing is sold to two people, it goes to the first buyer. This is a clear proof (dalil naqli) for the priority of the earlier date.

Key Terms
تاريخ (tarikh): The date mentioned by each claimant for the transaction.
بينة (bayyinah): Proof, generally two male witnesses or one male and two females, establishing the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) It goes to the one in possession

Key Takeaway
Where two claimants each have a valid sale contract with a date, the earlier sale prevails, as per the hadith 'the sale is for the first.'

Possession as a Tiebreaker in Property Disputes
Ruling on possession as tiebreaker in property disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants have equal evidence but one has possession, and explain the proof from the hadith of Jabir.

Core Idea
Possession tips the scale when evidence is equal and dates are absent.

In property disputes, when both claimants produce witnesses but neither provides a purchase date, the school gives priority to the one who currently holds the item. This ruling is based on the principle that possession indicates a prior completed transaction, and is supported by a hadith where the Prophet ruled for the possessor. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the exact conditions: two claimants, both have witnesses (bayyinah), neither mentions a date, and one has physical possession (qabd) of the disputed item.
The ruling applies only when dates are absent; if dates exist, the earlier date takes precedence.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The judge awards the item to the one in possession. This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
The ruling is based on the maxim 'al-ghanim bil-yad' (the gain is with the hand).
3
Explain the reasoning
Possession is a sign of prior ownership because one would only have physical control after a valid purchase. It suggests the sale was completed earlier.
This reasoning is qiyas jali: possession implies a prior transaction over absence of possession.
4
Analyse the hadith proof
The hadith of Jabir shows that when two men disputed over an animal and each brought witnesses, the Prophet gave it to the one in whose hand it was. This establishes possession as a determining factor when evidence is equal.
The hadith is reported by Bayhaqi and is used as a dalil for the ruling.

وان لم يذكر تاريخا ومع احدهما قبض فهو اولی به [2751] (22) وان لم يذكر تاريخا ومع احدهما قبض فهو اولی به۔ اگر دونوں نے تاریخ ذکر نہیں کی اور ان میں سے ایک کا قبضہ ہے تو وہ اولی ہوگا۔ اگر تاریخ ذکر کرتا تو دیکھا جاتا کہ کس کی تاریخ مقدم ہے اس کے لئے غلام کا فیصلہ کرتے لیکن تاریخ کسی نے ذکر نہیں کی ۔ البتہ ایک فریق کا غلام پر قبضہ بھی ہے تو قبضہ کرنا اس بات کی علامت ہے کہ اس نے پہلے خریدی ہے تب ہی تو اس کا قبضہ ہے، اور اس کے ساتھ بیع تام ہو چکی ہے تب ہی تو غلام اس کے قبضے میں ہے۔ اس لئے غلام کا قبضہ اس کے لئے ہوگا (۲) حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن جابر بن عبد الله ان رجلين تداعيا بدابة فاقام كل واحد منهما البينة انها دابته فقضى بها رسول الله الا الله الذي هي في يديه (ب) (سنن بیہقی ، باب المتد اعین میتازعان شیما فی ید احدهما و يتقیم کل واحد منهما علی ذلک بیت ج عاشر ص ۲۳۳ نمبر ۲۱۲۲۳) اس حدیث میں جس کا قبضہ تھا اس کو ایک گونہ ترجیح تھی اس لئے حضور نے جانور کا فیصلہ اسی کے لئے فرمایا۔
And if he did not mention a date and one of them has possession, then he is more entitled to it. [2751] (22) If both did not mention a date and one of them has possession, then he will be more entitled. If a date was mentioned, it would be seen whose date is earlier and the slave would be decided for them, but since no one mentioned a date, and one party has possession of the slave, then possession is a sign that he purchased first, because only then would he have possession, and the sale would have been completed for him. So the slave will go to the one in possession. (2) Proof is in the hadith: From Jabir ibn Abdullah that two men disputed over an animal, and each brought evidence that it was his animal, so the Messenger of Allah decided it for the one in whose hand it was. (Bayhaqi, Sunan) In this hadith, the one who had possession was preferred, so the Prophet decided the animal for him.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides another ruling from the same section. When neither claimant provides a date, but one of them has physical possession of the disputed item, that possession gives priority. The reasoning is that possession is a sign of a prior completed transaction. The text supports this with the hadith of Jabir, where the Prophet ruled for the one in possession when both had witnesses. This illustrates the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-ghanim bil-yad' (possession is dominant).

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Physical possession of the disputed item.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses.
أولى (awla): More entitled, having priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two claimants both have witnesses but no dates, and one has the item, who gets it according to the Hanafi school?
A) The one with the earlier date
B) The one with possession
C) They split the item
D) The item is confiscated

Key Takeaway
When evidence is equal and no purchase date is given, the Hanafi school awards the disputed item to the one in possession.

Purchase Prevails Over Gift in Conflict of Claims
Ruling with reasoned justification (talil) for conflict of claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a claim of purchase conflicts with a claim of gift, explain the reasoning based on mutual consideration, and apply the underlying principle to similar conflicting claims.

Core Idea
Purchase is stronger than gift because it involves mutual exchange.

In Hanafi civil law, disputes over ownership often arise when two parties each claim a different type of contract. This lesson examines a scenario where one party claims purchase and the other claims gift, both have possession and evidence but no dates. The Hanafi ruling gives priority to the buyer, based on a key principle that transactions with consideration are more robust than gratuitous ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute Scenario
Two parties each claim to have acquired an item from a third party: one via purchase (shira'), the other via gift (hibah). Both have possession and produce witnesses, but no dates are available to determine the sequence. Note the critical absence of dates as it triggers the rule.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The ruling is that the claim of purchase takes priority over the claim of gift. The property is awarded to the buyer, not the donee. This is the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school.
3
Analyse the Reasoning (Talil)
The reasoning is that purchase involves consideration (thaman) from both sides, making it a commutative contract. Gift is gratuitous, relying solely on one-sided benevolence. Because purchase involves a stronger basis of exchange, it is given precedence when evidence is otherwise equal.
This reasoning reflects the qawida that commutative contracts are stronger than non-commutative ones.
4
Extract the Underlying Principle
The underlying legal principle (asl) is that a transaction with mutual consideration outweighs a purely gratuitous transfer in conflicts. This principle applies in other areas where two transactions compete and one involves compensation.
Examples include conflicts between sale and loan, or between hire and borrowing.
5
Apply to Similar Cases
If the conflicting claims were between purchase and loan (ariyah), purchase would similarly prevail because the lender does not receive consideration. If both claims are commutative (e.g., two sales), the analysis would differ and may require other factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى احدهما شراء والآخر هبة وقبضا واقاما البينة ولا تاريخ معهما فالشراء اولى من الآخر [2752] (23) وان ادعى احدهما شراء والآخر هبة وقبضا واقاما البينة ولا تاريخ معهما فالشراء اولى من الآخر۔ دو آدمی دعوی کر رہے ہیں کہ فلاں سے لیا لیکن ایک آدمی دعوی کرتا ہے کہ فلاں سے خریدا ہے اور دوسرا دعوی کرتا ہے کہ فلاں نے مجھے ہبہ کیا ہے۔ اور دونوں نے قبضہ بھی کر لیا۔ اور اپنے اپنے دعوی پر بینہ پیش کیا۔ لیکن کسی کے پاس خرید نے اور ہبہ کرنے کی تاریخ نہیں ہے کہ کس نے پہلے خرید ہے اور کس کو بعد میں ہبہ کیا ہے تو ایسی صورت میں خریدنے کو ترجیح ہوگی اور مال خریدنے والے کا ہوگا ہبہ کئے ہوئے کا نہیں ہوگا۔ خریدنا ہبہ سے مضبوط ہے۔ کیونکہ خریدنے میں دونوں طرف سے مال ہے اور ہبہ میں ایک طرف سے مال ہے اور احسان ہے دوسری طرف سے مال نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے خرید نا زیادہ مضبوط ہے۔ اس لئے خریدنے والے کا ہوگا۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ خریدنا ہبہ سے زیادہ مضبوط ہے۔
And if one of them claims purchase and the other claims gift, and both have possession and bring evidence, but no date is with them, then the purchase takes priority over the other. [2752] (23) Two men claim to have taken from so-and-so: one says he bought, the other says he was given as a gift. Both have possession and bring evidence, but neither has a date. In such a case, the purchase is given priority and the property goes to the buyer, not the donee. Purchase is stronger than gift because in purchase there is consideration from both sides, while in gift there is only one-sided transfer and grace, no consideration from the other side. So purchase is stronger, thus the buyer gets it. The principle is that purchase is stronger than gift.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage deals with a conflict between a claim of purchase and a claim of gift, both supported by witnesses and possession, but without dates. The ruling is that the buyer prevails. The reasoning (talil) is that purchase involves mutual exchange (consideration), making it a stronger transaction than a gift, which is a gratuitous transfer. This is based on a general legal principle (qawida) that transactions with consideration are more robust than those without. No specific hadith is cited; the ruling is derived from the nature of the contracts.

Key Terms
شراء (shira'): A contract of sale with consideration.
هبة (hibah): A gratuitous transfer of ownership without exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When both parties have evidence and possession but no date, which claim prevails?
A) Gift
B) Purchase
C) They divide equally
D) The item goes to the original owner

Key Takeaway
In a conflict between a claim of purchase and a claim of gift with equal evidence and no dates, the Hanafi school awards the property to the buyer because purchase involves mutual consideration.
