Purchase vs Marriage Dower Claims: Equality or Priority?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on judicial priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default position on conflicting purchase and dower claims, explain Imam Muhammad's opposing view, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Purchase and dower are equal unless one side's obligation is more specific—Imam Muhammad says purchase is stronger.

When a man claims he bought a slave and a woman claims the same slave was given as her marriage dower, how does a judge decide? This passage from al-Sameri presents two views within the Hanafi school: the majority holds them equal, splitting the item; Imam Muhammad gives priority to the buyer. Understanding the reasoning behind each position sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi principles in judicial conflicts.

Guided Path
1
State the conflicting claims
Identify the two parties: one claims purchase of an item, the woman claims the same item was her dower in marriage. Note that both claims involve the same physical object.
The item in the text is a slave, but the rule applies to any specific chattel.
2
Analyze the default Hanafi position
The text first states that they are equal ('فهما سواء'). Understand the reasoning: purchase involves property on both sides, dower involves property from the husband and the vulva (bud') from the wife, which is treated as property. Thus, both transactions involve a property-for-property exchange, making them equal.
The principle is that dower is a valid exchange for the vulva, which has value.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees: he holds purchase is stronger. His dalil is that in a sale, the specific item must be delivered; in dower, the husband can substitute another item or pay the value. Thus, the dower claim is less binding on the specific item.
This is a pure intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the default view, the item is divided equally. Under Imam Muhammad's view, the buyer gets the item, and the woman receives its value (price) from the husband. This is the practical difference in the court's judgment.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi; later Hanafi practice may follow Imam Muhammad.
5
Reinforce the underlying principle
The dispute reduces to whether the specificity of the object in a sale gives it greater weight than a dower claim that can be satisfied by substitution. The default position equates them because both involve consideration (mu'awada).
Remember: the key concept is that bud' is treated as property in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى احدهما الشراء وادعت أمرأة انه تزوجها عليه فهما سواء [2753] (24) وان ادعى احدهما الشراء وادعت أمرأة انه تزوجها عليه فهما سواء۔ مثلا ایک نے دعوی کیا کہ فلاں نے مجھے غلام بیچا ہے اور عورت دعوی کرتی ہے کہ فلاں نے غلام کو مہر دینے کے بدلے مجھ سے شادی کی ہے۔ تو دونوں کا درجہ برابر ہے اور غلام آدھا آدھا ہو جائے گا۔ خریدنا اور مہر قوت کے اعتبار سے دونوں برابر درجے کے ہیں۔ کیونکہ خریدنے میں دونوں طرف مال ہیں اور مہر میں ایک طرف مال ہے اور دوسری طرف بضعہ ہے جو گویا کہ مال ہے اس لئے اگر کوئی اور علامت نہ ہوتو دونوں کا درجہ برابر ہوگا۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ خریدنا اور مہر دینا دونوں برابر درجے کے ہیں۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ خرید نا مہر سے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔ کیونکہ خریدنے میں غلام کا متعین کرنا ضروری ہوتا ہے اور مہر میں دوسرے کا غلام تعین کر دے اور پھر اس کی قیمت ادا کر دے تب بھی چل جائے گا۔ اس لئے خریدنے کے دعوی کرنے والے کو غلام دے دیا جائے گا اور مہر کے دعوی کرنے والی کو غلام کی قیمت دلوا دی جائے۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ خریدنا اور مہر دونوں برابر درجے کے نہیں ہیں۔
And if one of them claims purchase, and a woman claims that he married her with it as dower, then they are equal. [2753] (24) For example, one claims that so-and-so sold him a slave, and a woman claims that so-and-so married her by giving that slave as dower. Then both are equal in rank, and the slave will be split half and half. Purchase and dower are equal in strength, because in purchase there is property from both sides, and in dower there is property from one side and the vulva from the other, which is like property. So if there is no other indication, they are equal. The principle is that purchase and dower are equal. Imam Muhammad says that purchase is better than dower, because in purchase the slave must be specified, whereas in dower, he could specify another slave and pay its value. Therefore, the claimant of purchase should be given the slave, and the woman should be given its value. The principle is that purchase and dower are not equal.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage addresses a conflict between a claim of purchase and a woman's claim that the item was given as dower (mahr) in marriage. The text first states that they are equal, so the item is divided. The reasoning (talil) is that the dower is considered like property because it is exchanged for the vulva (buda'), which has value. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute is then presented: Imam Muhammad holds that purchase is stronger because the specific item must be delivered in sale, whereas in dower, the husband can substitute another item or pay its value. Thus, he rules for the buyer. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is what the text indicates? The text does not explicitly give tarjih but presents both views. This is a classic example of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): Dower given by husband to wife in marriage; can be a specific item or its value.
بضع (bud'): The vulva, considered as having property value in the context of dower.
دعوى (da'wa): Claim or lawsuit in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the default Hanafi ruling when a purchase claim and a dower claim conflict over the same item?
A) The buyer gets the item.
B) The item is split equally.
C) The woman gets the item.
D) The item is given to the state.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a purchase claim and a dower claim on the same item are equal by default, but Imam Muhammad gives purchase priority due to the specificity of delivery.

Why Pledge Prevails Over Gift in Claims
Ruling that a pledge is stronger than a gift in a claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a pledge prevails over a gift when both claim possession, and explain the principle of mutual consideration (iwad) that underpins this ruling.

Core Idea
A pledge prevails over a gift because it involves mutual exchange, making it stronger in claims.

Imagine two people both hold the keys to the same car. One says, 'This was pledged to me as security for a loan.' The other says, 'No, it was given to me as a gift.' Both claim possession. How does a Hanafi judge decide? This lesson unravels the reasoning: the pledge is backed by a debt (consideration), making it a commutative transaction, while a gift is gratuitous and thus weaker. The rule is based on the principle that contracts with exchange from both sides are stronger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
Recognize that one party claims a pledge (rahn) with possession, the other claims a gift (hibah) with possession. Both assert possession of the same item.
The dispute is over entitlement, not over ownership per se.
2
Classify each contract by nature
A pledge is a security contract where the pledgor owes a debt; it involves exchange (debt for possession as collateral). A gift is gratuitous – no exchange (iwad) from the donee.
In Hanafi fiqh, commutative contracts (mu'awadat) are generally stronger than gratuitous ones.
3
Apply the principle of strength based on consideration
The rule: where both sides give something in exchange, the transaction is stronger; where only one side gives without return, it is weaker. Hence the pledge is stronger.
This principle is derived from the hadith and general legal reasoning (talil).
4
Conclude the ruling
The judge rules in favour of the pledgee, because his claim is supported by a debt (consideration), making it more credible and entitled.
The ruling applies only when there is no other evidence (like a sign or witness) that would indicate the true nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى احدهما رهنا وقبضا والآخر هبة وقبضا فالرهن اولی [2754] (25) وان ادعى احدهما رهنا وقبضا والآخر هبة وقبضا فالرهن اولی۔ رہن کے بدلے میں مرتین کا قرض ہوتا ہے اس لئے وہ بدلے کی چیز ہو گئی اور ہبہ کے بدلے میں کچھ نہیں ہوتا اس لئے وہ رہن سے کمزور ہوا اس لئے ایک نے رہن کا دعوی کیا اور دوسرے نے ہبہ کا دعوی کیا اور کوئی علامت نہیں ہے تو رہن والے کے لئے غلام کا فیصلہ ہوگا۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ جہاں دونوں طرف سے بدلے ہوں وہ زیادہ مضبوط ہے اور جہاں ایک طرف سے مال ہو اور اس کے بدلے میں کچھ نہ ہو تو وہ کمزور ہے۔
And if one of them claims a pledge (rahn) with possession, and the other claims a gift (hibah) with possession, then the pledge is more entitled. [2754] (25) A pledge involves a debt from the pledgor, so it becomes something in exchange, whereas a gift has no exchange. Therefore, the pledge is stronger. So if one claims pledge and another claims gift, and there is no other sign, the ruling will be for the pledgee. The principle is that where there is exchange from both sides, it is stronger; where one side gives property without exchange, it is weaker.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage rules on a conflict between a claim of pledge (rahn) and a claim of gift (hibah). The pledge is given priority because it is backed by a debt (consideration), making it a commutative transaction. A gift, being gratuitous, is weaker. The reasoning (talil) is based on the presence or absence of mutual exchange. This applies the same principle as earlier: transactions with consideration are stronger.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge or collateral for a debt; involves possession by the pledgee.
ہبہ (hibah): A gift; a gratuitous transfer of ownership without compensation.
بدل (badal): Consideration or exchange; something given in return for something else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The donee
B) The pledgee
C) They split
D) The item returns to the original owner

Key Takeaway
In claims, a pledge prevails over a gift because it is backed by consideration, following the Hanafi principle that commutative transactions are stronger than gratuitous ones.

Priority by Date for Outside Claimants
Ruling that two outsiders with evidence, the earlier date wins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding two outsider claimants with different dates and apply it to determine rightful ownership.

Core Idea
Earlier date of evidence decides ownership between two outsiders.

This lesson extends the principle of date-based priority to a new scenario: claimants who do not possess the disputed item. The item is held by a third party. Both claimants produce witnesses and state a purchase date. Which one prevails? The earlier date, because the prior transaction is valid and later ones cannot supersede it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize who is an 'outsider' (kharij): a claimant who does not possess the disputed item. The item is in the hands of a third party.
2
Examine the evidence
Both claimants bring witnesses (bayyinah) and specify a date of acquisition (e.g., purchase date). The date is crucial.
3
Compare dates
Compare the two dates. The earlier date indicates prior ownership. Later claims are invalid if the earlier ownership is established.
If dates are identical, further investigation may be needed.
4
Apply the ruling
The claimant with the earlier date is more entitled (ahaqq) to the item. The judge rules in their favor.
This follows the same logic as the earlier lesson: time establishes priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا اقام اثنان خارجان بينة على الملك والتاريخ فصاحب التاريخ المقدم أحق [2755] (26) إذا اقام اثنان خارجان بينة على الملك والتاريخ فصاحب التاريخ المقدم أحق۔ چیز کسی تیسرے کے ہاتھ میں تھی ان دونوں کے ہاتھ میں نہیں تھی اس لئے ان دونوں کو خارج کہتے ہیں۔ ان دونوں نے دعوی کیا کہ یہ چیز میری ہے اور دونوں نے گواہ پیش کئے اور تاریخ بھی بتائی کہ فلاں تاریخ کو میں نے خریدا تو جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہے وہ زیادہ حق رکھتا ہے۔
If two outsiders bring evidence of ownership and date, then the one with the earlier date is more entitled. [2755] (26) The item was in the hand of a third person, not in the hands of these two, so they are called outsiders. Both claim 'this item is mine' and both bring witnesses and also give a date, saying 'I bought it on such-and-such date', then the one with the earlier date has more right.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage extends the earlier principle to 'outsiders' (kharijan) — claimants who do not have possession of the disputed item. The item is in the hands of a third party. Both claimants bring witnesses and each provides a date. The ruling is that the earlier date decides ownership. This follows the same logic as in lesson 1: the earlier transaction is valid and later ones are not. The term 'kharijan' is explained: they are outsiders because they do not hold the item.

Key Terms
خارج (kharij): An outsider claimant who does not possess the disputed item; the item is with a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when two outsiders bring evidence of ownership with different dates?
A) The one with the earlier date wins
B) They split
C) The possessor keeps it
D) It is given to the state

Key Takeaway
The earlier date of evidence determines ownership between two outsider claimants.

Claim of Pledge vs. Claim of Gift – Priority of Pledge
Ruling on conflicting claims of pledge and gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a claim of pledge with possession conflicts with a claim of gift with possession, and explain the underlying principle that a transaction with a countervalue (badal) is stronger than a gratuitous transfer.

Core Idea
A pledge with possession prevails over a gift with possession because a pledge involves a countervalue, making it a stronger transaction.

In a dispute where two claimants each present evidence of possession—one claiming the item was given as a pledge (rahn) for a debt, and the other claiming it was a gift (hiba)—the Hanafi school gives priority to the pledge. This ruling is based on a general principle: transactions that involve exchange (a countervalue on both sides) are considered stronger and more binding than those that are purely gratuitous. Understanding this principle helps in resolving similar conflicts of evidence in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
Note that both claimants assert possession (qabd) of the same property—one as a pledge, the other as a gift. The dispute is about the basis of possession.
Possession alone does not decide ownership; the underlying contract matters.
2
Recognize the presence of a countervalue
In a pledge, the property is given as security for a debt; the debt is the countervalue (badal) received by the pledgor. In a gift, there is no countervalue.
The countervalue makes the pledge a transaction of exchange (mu'awada), even though it is not a sale.
3
Apply the principle of strength based on exchange
The underlying principle: a transaction with exchange on both sides is stronger than one where wealth is transferred without return. Therefore, the pledge claim is stronger.
4
Determine the outcome
When there is no other evidence or indicator (e.g., a sign distinguishing the claims), the judge awards the property to the pledge claimant.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position with no known intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۵۴] (۲۵) وان ادعى احدهما رهنا وقبضا والآخر هبة وقبضا فالرهن اولی رہن کے بدلے میں مرتین کا قرض ہوتا ہے اس لئے وہ بدلے کی چیز ہو گئی اور ہبہ کے بدلے میں کچھ نہیں ہوتا اس لئے وہ رہن سے کمزور ہوا اس لئے ایک نے رہن کا دعوی کیا اور دوسرے نے ہبہ کا دعوی کیا اور کوئی علامت نہیں ہے تو رہن والے کے لئے غلام کا فیصلہ ہوگا۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ جہاں دونوں طرف سے بدلے ہوں وہ زیادہ مضبوط ہے اور جہاں ایک طرف سے مال ہو اور اس کے بدلے میں کچھ نہ ہو تو وہ کمزور ہے۔
(25) And if one of them claims a pledge (rahn) and possession (qabd), and the other claims a gift (hiba) and possession, then the pledge is preferred. Because in a pledge there is a debt as a countervalue, so it becomes a transaction with exchange, while in a gift there is no countervalue, so it is weaker than a pledge. Therefore, if one claims a pledge and the other claims a gift and there is no other indicator, the slave is decided for the pledge claimant. The principle of this issue is: where there is exchange on both sides, it is stronger; where there is wealth on one side and no countervalue on the other, it is weaker.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage addresses a conflict between two claimants: one claims a pledge (rahn) with possession, and the other claims a gift (hiba) with possession over the same item (a slave). The ruling prioritizes the pledge over the gift. The reasoning is based on the presence of a countervalue (badal) in the pledge: the pledge is given as security for a debt, so it involves an exchange. In contrast, a gift is a gratuitous transfer with no countervalue. Therefore, the pledge transaction is considered stronger, and the judge awards the item to the pledge claimant if there is no other distinguishing evidence. The underlying principle is that transactions involving bilateral exchange are stronger than those involving unilateral transfer of wealth.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge or mortgage, where property is given as security for a debt.
هبة (hiba): A gift, a gratuitous transfer of property without compensation.
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking delivery of the property, essential for certain claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two claimants present evidence, one of a pledge and possession and the other of a gift and possession, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The gift claimant is preferred because gift is a transfer of ownership.
B) The pledge claimant is preferred because the pledge involves a countervalue (debt).
C) Both are equal and the property is divided.
D) The judge decides based on his discretion.

Key Takeaway
In a conflict between a claim of pledge with possession and a claim of gift with possession, the pledge takes priority because it involves a countervalue, making it a stronger transaction.

Preference for Earlier Date in Claims by Two Outsiders
Ruling on priority of earlier date for outsider claimants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two outsiders present evidence with dates and apply the analogy from the hadith to determine priority.

Core Idea
When two non-possessors claim ownership, the earlier date prevails—priority in time establishes right.

In Hanafi qada, when two individuals who do not possess an item each claim ownership and provide evidence with specific dates, the judge must decide based on priority. This lesson examines the rule that the earlier date gives precedence, drawing on a hadith about priority in sales. Understanding this principle helps resolve ownership disputes when possession is neutral.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize that both claimants are 'kharij' (outsiders) because the item is in the hand of a third party. Neither claimant has possession.
2
Confirm evidence and dates
Both claimants present bayyinah (evidence) and specify dates when they claim ownership began. The validity of evidence is assumed.
3
Apply the priority principle
The claimant with the earlier date is given the item because ownership that is proven first takes precedence. Later ownership cannot override an earlier established right.
Example: June 1 vs June 7 – June 1 wins.
4
Identify the dalil and analogy
The ruling is supported by the hadith 'When a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first.' This is applied by qiyas (analogy) to ownership claims with dates.
The hadith shows that priority in time creates a legal right.
5
Understand the scope
This ruling applies when both claimants are outsiders and no other factor (e.g., possession, stronger evidence) distinguishes them. If one were a possessor, different rules apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۵۵] (۲۶) إذا اقام الخارجان البينة على الملك والتاريخ فصاحب التاريخ الاقدم اولى چیز کسی تیسرے کے ہاتھ میں تھی ان دونوں کے ہاتھ میں نہیں تھی اس لئے ان دونوں کو خارج کہتے ہیں۔ ان دونوں نے دعوی کیا کہ یہ چیز میری ملکیت ہے اور دونوں نے تاریخ بھی بیان کی تو جن کی تاریخ مقدم ہے اس کے لئے چیز کا فیصلہ ہوگا۔ جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہے مثلا ایک کہتا ہے کہ پہلی جون کو میں اس چیز کا مالک بنا اور دوسرا کہتا ہے کہ ساتویں جون کو میں اس چیز کا مالک بنا تو یقینی بات ہے کہ پہلی جون والے کی ملکیت پہلے ہوئی اور جب اس کی ملکیت ہو گئی تو دوسرے کی ملکیت نہیں ہو سکے گی اس لئے پہلے کے لئے فیصلہ ہو گا (۲) حدیث میں اس کا اشارہ ہے۔ عن سمرة بن جندب عن النبي عل الله قال اذا بيع البيع من رجلين فالبيع للاول (الف) ( ابن ماجہ شریف، باب من اشترط الخلاص ص ۳۳۵ نمبر ۲۳۴۴) اس حدیث میں جس آدمی نے پہلے بیع کی ہے چیز اس کی ہوگی۔ اسی قاعدے پر قیاس کر کے جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہوگی چیز اس کی ہوگی۔
(26) When the two outsiders (kharijan) produce evidence of ownership and date, then the one with the earlier date is preferred. The item was in the hand of a third party, not in the hands of these two, so they are called outsiders. Both claimed that this item is my property, and both stated a date. So whichever has the earlier date, the item is decided for him. For example, one says 'I became owner on June 1st' and the other says 'I became owner on June 7th'. It is certain that the June 1st owner's ownership occurred first, and when his ownership was established, the other cannot own it. So the decision is for the first. (2) There is an indication of this in the hadith: From Samurah ibn Jundub from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'When a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first.' (Ibn Majah, Bab man ishtarat al-khalas, p.335 no.2344). In this hadith, the person who made the first sale, the item is his. By analogy to this rule, whichever has the earlier date, the item is his.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute between two outsiders (kharijan) – individuals who do not have physical possession of the item but claim ownership. Both present evidence (bayyinah) of their ownership and provide specific dates. The ruling is that the claimant with the earlier date is given priority because ownership is established first in time. The text gives an example: if one became owner on June 1st and the other on June 7th, the earlier ownership prevails and the item is awarded to the June 1st claimant. This ruling is supported by a hadith: 'When a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first.' The principle is that priority in time establishes precedence in right, by analogy (qiyas) to the hadith. This ruling applies when there is no other indicator to distinguish the claims.

Key Terms
خارج (kharij): An outsider, a claimant who does not have possession of the disputed item.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually two witnesses or a combination of proof establishing a claim.
تاريخ (tarikh): Date, the time when the claim of ownership is asserted to have begun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two outsiders (kharijan) each present evidence of ownership of a slave with different dates. One claims ownership from June 1st, the other from June 7th. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The slave is divided equally between them regardless of dates.
B) The claimant with the later date is given priority because his evidence is more recent.
C) The claimant with the earlier date is given the slave.
D) The judge determines the owner by examining the witnesses' credibility.

Key Takeaway
When two outsiders claim ownership with evidence and dates, the earlier date gives priority, based on the principle that temporal precedence creates a legal right.

Earlier Date Preference in Conflicting Purchase Claims
Ruling that when two claimants prove purchase from the same person with different dates, the earlier date is preferred

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants prove purchase from the same seller with different dates, and apply the principle that the earlier purchase transfers ownership.

Core Idea
The first purchase transfers ownership; later purchases from the same seller are invalid.

This lesson examines a specific judicial ruling: when two parties claim to have bought the same item from the same seller on different dates, the earlier date prevails. This is not about priority in possession but about when ownership transferred. We will explore the evidence from hadith and the legal reasoning behind the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Two claimants both assert purchase from the same seller, each with evidence of a different date.
The claim is based on a specific transaction, not absolute ownership.
2
Recall the general rule of ownership transfer
In a valid sale, ownership transfers at the time of the contract, not at delivery.
This is the foundation of the ruling.
3
Apply precedence to the first purchase
The first purchase in time effectively transferred ownership away from the seller.
Thus the seller no longer owned the item at the time of the second sale.
4
Examine the proof from hadith
The hadith 'the sale is for the first' and the principle of priority in taking something support this ruling.
These hadiths are ahad but accepted as evidence in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Determine the consequence for the later buyer
The later buyer has no claim on the item and can only seek recourse from the seller for breach.
The second purchase is invalid due to lack of ownership in the seller.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۵۶] (۲۷) وان ادعيا الشراء من واحد واقاما البينة على تاريخين فالاول اولی اوپر کے مسئلے میں مطلق ملکیت کا دعوی تھا اس لئے اس میں خریدنے کی وجہ سے ملکیت کا دعوی کیا اور دونوں نے دو تاریخیں بتلائیں تو کا تھا اس لئے سے کا کیا اور نے دو جن کی تاریخ مقدم ہوگی چیز اس کی ہوگی۔ جس نے پہلے خریدنے کا ثبوت دیا چیز اس کی ہوگئی بعد میں خریدنے والا کیا چیز خریدے گا۔ اس لئے پہلے والے کے لئے خریدنا کا فیصلہ ہوگا (۲) حدیث مسئلہ نمبر ۲۶ میں گزرگئی فالبيع للاول (ابن ماجہ شریف، نمبر ۲۳۴۴) (۳) ایک حدیث سے بھی پتا چلتا ہے کہ کوئی کسی چیز کو پہلے لے لے تو اسی کی ہوگی۔ عن عن اسمر بن مضرس عن النبى الله من سبق الى مالم يسبقه اليه مسلم فهو له يريد به احياء الموات (ب) (سن للبیہقی ، باب القاضي يقدم الناس الاول فالاول الخ ج عاشرص ۲۳۵ نمبر ۴ ۲۰۴۸) ، الخرج
(27) And if they both claim purchase from one person and present evidence with two dates, then the first is preferred. In the previous issue (26), it was a claim of absolute ownership; here the claim of ownership is based on purchase, and both mention two dates. So whichever has the earlier date, the item will be his. The one who first proved purchase obtained ownership, and the later purchaser cannot purchase it. Therefore, the earlier one is decided the purchase. (2) The hadith from issue 26 has already passed: 'the sale is for the first' (Ibn Majah, no. 2344). (3) Another hadith also indicates that whoever takes something first, it becomes his: From Asmar ibn Mudarris from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever gets to something that no other Muslim has preceded him to, it is his' – meaning the revival of dead lands (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab al-qadi yuqaddimu al-nas al-awwal fa al-awwal, vol.10 p.235 no. 42048).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This ruling is similar to the previous one but specifies that both claimants are asserting they purchased the item from the same seller. Each presents a purchase contract with a different date. The earlier date prevails because the first purchase transferred ownership to the first buyer, so the second buyer cannot acquire it from the same seller. The same hadith from Samurah ibn Jundub is cited as proof. Additionally, another hadith from Asmar ibn Mudarris is mentioned, which states that whoever precedes others in taking something (specifically in reviving dead land) gains ownership. This supports the principle that priority in time gives right.

Key Terms
بيعة (bay'ah): Sale contract, specifically a purchase transaction.
أول (awwal): The first in time or order; the party with the earlier date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two claimants present evidence that they each purchased the same slave from the same seller, with different dates. According to Hanafi fiqh, which claimant is entitled to the slave?
A) The one with the later date, because his purchase is more recent.
B) Both are equally entitled, and the slave is divided.
C) The one with the earlier date, because the first purchase established ownership.
D) The seller's testimony decides.

Key Takeaway
In conflicting claims of purchase from the same seller, the earlier date determines ownership because the first contract transfers ownership.

Equal Claims of Purchase from Each Other
Ruling that when two claimants each prove purchase from the other with the same date, they are equal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants each prove purchase from the other with the same date and explain the option given to them.

Core Idea
When two equal purchases conflict, the disputed item is divided equally, with an option to accept or rescind.

In this lesson, we examine a scenario where two independent buyers claim ownership of the same item, each alleging purchase from a different seller on the same date. The Hanafi rule treats their claims as equally strong, leading to a half-and-half division with a special option. Understanding this ruling requires careful analysis of the evidence and the principle of equality in conflicting proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed object and claimants
Recognize that each claimant presents evidence of having purchased the item from the other (or from a different seller). Both claims are independent.
The example: Zayd claims he bought the slave from Khalid; Umar claims he bought the same slave from Majid.
2
Note the date and equality of evidence
Both claimants mention the same date, and there is no other sign to give priority. Thus, both pieces of evidence are equally strong.
If dates differed, the earlier purchase would prevail.
3
State the ruling of equality
Since neither claim can be preferred, the claims are equal. The Hanafi ruling is that the item is divided equally between the two claimants.
Each claimant gets half ownership of the slave.
4
Explain the option (khiyar)
Each claimant has the option to either accept half the item for half the price, or to rescind the transaction entirely.
This option allows them to avoid a forced partnership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۵۷] (۲۸) وان قام كل واحد منهما بينة على الشراء من الآخر وذكرا تاريخا فهما سواء دو آدمیوں نے خریدنے کا دعوی کیا اور ہر ایک نے کہا کہ میں نے دوسرے سے خریدا ہے۔ مثلا زید نے کہا کہ میں نے یہ غلام خالد سے خریدا ہے اور عمر کہتا ہے کہ میں نے یہ غلام ماجد سے خریدا ہے اور دونوں نے ایک تاریخ بیان کی تو دونوں کا درجہ برابر ہے۔ جب زید کہتا ہے کہ خالد سے خریدا اور عمر کہتا ہے کہ ساجد سے خریدا تو دونوں نے ایک ہی وقت میں اپنے اپنے بائع کے لئے ملکیت ثابت کی اور کوئی دوسری علامت نہیں ہے جس کی وجہ سے ترجیح دی جائے اس لئے دونوں برابر درجے کے ہوں گے اور دونوں کے لئے آدھے آدھے غلام کا فیصلہ ہوگا آدھی آدھی قیمت کے بدلے میں۔ اور اختیار ہوگا چاہے تو آدھا غلام آدھی قیمت میں لیں اور چاہے تو چھوڑ دیں (۲)
(28) And if each of them establishes evidence of purchase from the other, and they mention a date, then they are equal. Two men claimed purchase, each said 'I bought this slave from the other'. For example, Zayd said 'I bought this slave from Khalid' and Umar said 'I bought this slave from Majid', and both mentioned one date. So they are equal in rank. Since each claims ownership from a different seller, both have established ownership for their respective sellers at the same time, and there is no other sign to give preference. Therefore, they are equal, and the decision is that the slave is divided half-and-half, for half the price each. And they have the option: if they wish, they can take half the slave for half the price, or they can leave it.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This ruling addresses a scenario where two claimants each present evidence that they purchased the same item from the other claimant (i.e., each claims the other was the seller). The text gives an example where Zayd claims he bought from Khalid, and Umar claims he bought from Majid (different sellers). Both mention the same date. Since each is claiming ownership through purchase from a different seller, and there is no priority in time or other distinguishing factor, both claims are considered equal. The outcome is that the disputed slave is divided equally between them, and each pays half the price. They are also given the option (khiyar) to either accept half the slave for half the price or to rescind the transaction. This is because neither claim is stronger than the other.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, here each claimant presents evidence of purchase from the other.
اختيار (ikhtiyar): Option or choice given to the claimants to either take half or leave the transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Zayd and Umar each present evidence that they purchased a slave from the other, with the same date. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Both claims are false and the slave is returned to the original owner.
B) The older claimant gets the entire slave.
C) The slave is divided equally between them, and each has an option to take half for half price or leave it.
D) The judge rejects both claims due to contradiction.

Key Takeaway
When two claimants each prove purchase from the other with the same date, the Hanafi rule divides the property equally and gives each an option to accept half or rescind.

Preference for Earlier Date in Ownership Claims
Ruling on two outsiders claiming ownership with dated evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two outsiders present dated evidence of ownership and explain the proof from hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
When two outsiders claim ownership with dated evidence, the earlier date prevails.

In property disputes where both claimants are outsiders (not in possession), the court must decide based on evidence. How does the Hanafi school resolve competing claims when both parties present documentary proof with dates? This lesson examines the ruling and its basis in hadith and analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their status
Determine that both claimants are kharij (outsiders) – neither is in possession of the disputed property. This distinction is crucial because for outsiders, the burden of proof is solely on their evidence, not on possession.
In Hanafi law, a person in possession (dhu al-yad) has a presumption in their favor.
2
State the general ruling
The text rules: 'If two outsiders present evidence of ownership with a date, then the one with the earlier date is more entitled.' This establishes priority by time as the decisive factor.
The ruling applies when both claimants produce bayyinah (evidence) that includes a specific date.
3
Examine the main proof (hadith)
The author cites the hadith: 'When a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first.' Although the hadith concerns a single sale to two buyers, the principle of priority by time is clear.
The hadith is from Sunan Ibn Majah and is ahad (single-transmitter), so its thubut is zanni, but it is used as a primary proof.
4
Apply qiyas to ownership claims
The Hanafi jurist extends the ruling from the specific case of a sale to two buyers to the general case of competing ownership claims. The illa (effective cause) is priority by time – whoever proves their right earlier has a stronger claim.
Qiyas is valid here because the underlying reason (priority) is common to both cases.
5
Note the sub-case
The text also covers a sub-case: if both claimants purchase from the same seller with different dates, the earlier purchase still prevails. The same hadith and reasoning apply.
This reinforces the principle that the date of the transaction, not the date of the claim, is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اكام الخارجان البيئة على الملك والتاريخ فصاحب التاريخ الاقدم اولی [۲۷۲۷۵۶) وان ادعيا الشراء من واحد واقاما البينة على تاريخين فالاول اولی [۲۷۵۷ (۲۸) وان قام كل واحد منهما بينة على الشراء من الآخر وذكرا تاريخا فهما سواء.
میری ملکیت ہے اور دونوں نے تاریخ بھی بیان کی تو جن کی تاریخ مقدم ہے اس کے لئے چیز کا فیصلہ ہوگا۔ جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہے مثلا ایک کہتا ہے کہ پہلی جون کو میں اس چیز کا مالک بنا اور دوسرا کہتا ہے کہ ساتویں جون کو میں اس چیز کا مالک بنا تو یقینی بات ہے کہ پہلی جون والے کی ملکیت پہلے ہوئی اور جب اس کی ملکیت ہو گئی تو دوسرے کی ملکیت نہیں ہو سکے گی اس لئے پہلے کے لئے فیصلہ ہو گا (۲) حدیث میں اس کا اشارہ ہے۔ عن سمرة بن جندب عن النبي عل الله قال اذا بيع البيع من رجلين فالبيع للاول (الف) ( ابن ماجہ شریف، باب من اشترط الخلاص ص ۳۳۵ نمبر ۲۳۴۴) اس حدیث میں جس آدمی نے پہلے بیع کی ہے چیز اس کی ہوگی۔ اسی قاعدے پر قیاس کر کے جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہوگی چیز اس کی ہوگی۔ [۲۷۵۶] (۲۷) اگر دونوں نے ایک آدمی سے خریدنے کا دعوی کیا اور دونوں نے دو تاریخوں پر بعینہ قائم کیا تو مقدم تاریخ والا اولی ہو گا۔ اوپر کے مسئلے میں مطلق ملکیت کا دعوی تھا اس لئے اس میں خریدنے کی وجہ سے ملکیت کا دعوی کیا اور دونوں نے دو تاریخیں بتلائیں تو کا تھا اس لئے سے کا کیا اور نے دو جن کی تاریخ مقدم ہوگی چیز اس کی ہوگی۔ جس نے پہلے خریدنے کا ثبوت دیا چیز اس کی ہوگئی بعد میں خریدنے والا کیا چیز خریدے گا۔ اس لئے پہلے والے کے لئے خریدنا کا فیصلہ ہوگا (۲) حدیث مسئلہ نمبر ۲۶ میں گزرگئی فالبيع للاول (ابن ماجہ شریف، نمبر ۲۳۴۴) (۳) ایک حدیث سے بھی پتا چلتا ہے کہ کوئی کسی چیز کو پہلے لے لے تو اسی کی ہوگی۔ عن 1 عن اسمر بن مضرس عن النبى الله من سبق الى مالم يسبقه اليه مسلم فهو له يريد به احياء الموات (ب) (سن للبیہقی ، باب القاضي يقدم الناس الاول فالاول الخ ج عاشرص ۲۳۵ نمبر ۴ ۲۰۴۸) ، الخرج
If two outsiders present evidence of ownership with a date, then the one with the earlier date is more entitled. [272756) And if they both claim purchase from one person and present evidence with two dates, then the first is more entitled. [2757 (28) And if each of them presents evidence of purchase from the other and they mention a date, then they are equal.
[Urdu] Both claim ownership and both have mentioned a date, so whichever has the earlier date will be decided in his favor. For example, one says 'I became owner on June 1' and the other says 'I became owner on June 7', then it is certain that the June 1 owner's ownership came first, and when his ownership occurred, the other's ownership cannot happen. So judgment will be for the first. (2) There is an indication of this in the hadith: From Samurah ibn Jundub from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'When a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first.' (Ibn Majah, chapter on conditions, p.335 no.2344) In this hadith, the item belongs to the one who first made the sale. By analogy on this principle, the one with the earlier date will get the item. [2756] (27) If both claim purchase from one person and both establish evidence on two dates, then the earlier date is more entitled. In the above issue, the claim was of absolute ownership, so here the claim of ownership by purchase is made, and both mention two dates, so whichever date is earlier, the item will be his. The one who first proved purchase, the item becomes his; what can the later purchaser buy? So judgment will be for the first. (2) The hadith already passed in issue no.26: 'The sale is for the first.' (Ibn Majah, no.2344) (3) Another hadith indicates that whoever first takes something, it becomes his: From Asmar ibn Mudarris from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever precedes to something that no Muslim has preceded him to, it is for him.' He meant reviving dead land. (Al-Bayhaqi, chapter on judge prioritizing the first, vol.10 p.235 no.20484), etc.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on competing ownership claims when both parties present evidence with dates. The text first states the general principle: if two outsiders (not in possession) claim ownership and each provides evidence with a date, the one with the earlier date is preferred. The reasoning is that the earlier owner's right takes precedence; once ownership is established, the later claim cannot supersede it. The author supports this with a hadith from Samurah ibn Jundub: 'When a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first.' Although this hadith is about a specific sale to two buyers, the principle of priority by time is analogically extended to ownership claims. Additionally, a hadith from Asmar ibn Mudarris about precedence in taking unowned land is cited. The commentary then addresses a sub-case (27) where both claim purchase from the same seller with different dates; again, the earlier date prevails. The text notes that the same hadith applies. The language is a mixture of Arabic matn and Urdu sharh, typical of later Hanafi works.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or proof, typically testimony of two witnesses or documentary evidence, presented in court to establish a claim.
خارج (kharij): An outsider; a person not in possession of the disputed property who claims ownership.
تاريخ (tarikh): Date; the recorded time when a transaction or event allegedly occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between two outsiders claiming ownership of the same property, each presenting evidence with a date, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The property is divided equally between them.
B) The one with the earlier date of evidence is preferred.
C) The property goes to the one who first files the lawsuit.
D) The judge decides based on the credibility of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
When two outsiders present dated evidence of ownership, the earlier date determines the right in Hanafi law.

Equal Division When Claims Are Balanced
Ruling with proof on equal claims from different sellers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for equal claims from different sellers with identical evidence and apply it to a similar case.

Core Idea
When outsiders claim purchase from different sellers with equal proof, the property is split equally.

This lesson examines a scenario where two individuals each claim to have bought the same item from different sellers, both presenting evidence with identical dates. Since no preference can be established, Hanafi law treats them equally, dividing the item in half. The ruling is rooted in a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
Recognize that both claimants are outsiders (neither is in possession) and each asserts purchase from a different seller.
Possession would break equality.
2
Examine the evidence condition
Both present evidence (e.g., witnesses) and the dates are identical. No other factor gives preference.
If dates differ, the earlier date wins.
3
Apply the rule of equality
Since the evidence is balanced, the judge treats them as equal and divides the disputed property half each, with each paying half the value.
The rule is based on the hadith of the camel.
4
Connect to the prophetic precedent
The Prophet divided a camel equally between two claimants who each brought two witnesses without any preference, establishing this principle.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud.

Source Text

(۲۸) وان قام كل واحد منهما بينة على الشراء من الآخر وذكرا تاريخا فهما سواء.
دو آدمیوں نے خریدنے کا دعوی کیا اور ہر ایک نے کہا کہ میں نے دوسرے سے خریدا ہے۔ مثلا زید نے کہا کہ میں نے یہ غلام خالد سے خریدا ہے اور عمر کہتا ہے کہ میں نے یہ غلام ماجد سے خریدا ہے اور دونوں نے ایک تاریخ بیان کی تو دونوں کا درجہ برابر ہے۔ جب زید کہتا ہے کہ خالد سے خریدا اور عمر کہتا ہے کہ ساجد سے خریدا تو دونوں نے ایک ہی وقت میں اپنے اپنے بائع کے لئے ملکیت ثابت کی اور کوئی دوسری علامت نہیں ہے جس کی وجہ سے ترجیح دی جائے اس لئے دونوں برابر درجے کے ہوں گے اور دونوں کے لئے آدھے آدھے غلام کا فیصلہ ہوگا آدھی آدھی قیمت کے بدلے میں۔ اور اختیار ہوگا چاہے تو آدھا غلام آدھی قیمت میں لیں اور چاہے تو چھوڑ دیں (۲)

۲۷۵ (۲۹) وان اقام الخارج البينة على ملك مؤرّخ واقام صاحب اليد البينة على حديث گزر چکی ہے۔ عن ابی موسى الاشعری ... ان رجلين ادعيا بعيرا على عهد النبي الله فبعث كل واحد منهما شاهدين فقسمه النبي الا الله بينهما نصفين (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الرجلین یدعیان شیعا ولیس بینھما بیته ص ۱۵۳ نمبر ۳۶۱۵) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ تربیج کی کوئی علامت نہ ہو تو دونوں کو آدھا آدھا ملے گا۔
(28) And if each of them presents evidence of purchase from the other and they mention a date, then they are equal. [Urdu] Two men claimed purchase, and each said 'I bought from the other.' For example, Zaid said 'I bought this slave from Khalid' and Umar said 'I bought this slave from Majid' and both mention one date, then both are equal in rank. When Zaid says he bought from Khalid and Umar says he bought from Sajid, both have established ownership for their respective sellers at the same time, and there is no other sign to give preference, so they are equal in rank. Judgment will be for half the slave each in exchange for half the price. And they have the option: if they wish, they can take half the slave for half the price, or they can leave it. (2) [Footnote: (a) The Prophet said: 'When an item is sold to two men, the sale is for the first.' (b) It is narrated from him that 'Whoever takes something that no Muslim's hand has reached, it becomes his.' He meant reviving dead land.] [Page 110] ... This hadith shows that when there is no sign of preference, both get half. (From Abu Dawud, chapter on two men claiming something without evidence between them, p.153 no.3615).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage addresses a situation where both claimants are outsiders and each claims to have purchased the disputed property from a different seller, with both providing evidence bearing the same date. Since the dates are identical and no other factor (e.g., possession or additional witnesses) gives preference, the Hanafi ruling is that they are equal. Consequently, the property is divided equally between them, each taking half and paying half the value. This is supported by a hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari where the Prophet divided a camel equally between two claimants who each brought two witnesses but had no preference. The footnote also references the earlier hadith about the first sale. The key point is that without a basis for priority (such as an earlier date or other indicia), equality prevails.

Key Terms
سواء (sawa'): Equal; when both claimants have identical strength of evidence and no preference, they are considered equal before the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two outsiders each claim to have purchased the same slave from different sellers, both presenting evidence with the same date, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The claim of the one with better witnesses is accepted.
B) The slave is divided equally between them, each paying half the price.
C) The seller who is named in both claims must decide.
D) The slave is given to the one who files first.

Key Takeaway
When equal claims arise from different sellers with identical evidence, the Hanafi solution is equal division.

Preference for Occupant's Earlier Evidence
Ruling when outsider's evidence is later than occupant's

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which party's evidence is preferred when the occupant's evidence has an earlier date than the outsider's, and justify the ruling using the Hanafi principle of priority by time.

Core Idea
Earlier date wins: occupant's earlier evidence overrides the default plaintiff-defendant rule.

In Hanafi fiqh, the burden of proof normally lies with the plaintiff. However, when the defendant (occupant) possesses evidence with an earlier date than the plaintiff's, a special rule applies: the earlier date indicates prior ownership, so the occupant's evidence is accepted. This lesson examines the reasoning and hadith that establish this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their evidence
Recognize who is the outsider (plaintiff) and who is the occupant (defendant). Note the dates on their respective evidence.
The outsider's evidence has a later date; the occupant's evidence has an earlier date.
2
State the general procedural rule
Recall that normally the plaintiff's evidence is accepted first, and the defendant's evidence is not considered without an oath.
This default is based on the principle that the defendant is presumed innocent.
3
Identify the exception for earlier date
Recognize that when the occupant's evidence bears an earlier date, the general rule is overridden. The earlier date suggests prior ownership, so the occupant's evidence is preferred.
The exception is justified by the maxim 'priority by time establishes prior rights.'
4
Examine the hadith proofs
Review the three hadith cited: (1) 'the sale is for the first,' (2) 'whoever precedes to unowned land,' and (3) Hudhayfah's judgment based on a sign (tied camel). Understand how each supports priority by time.
These hadith establish a general principle of priority by time applicable to ownership claims.
5
Understand the legal reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
Analyze how the hadith are applied to this case: the earlier date is analogous to being 'first' in the hadith, so the occupant's claim is stronger even though he is the defendant.
The principle of priority by time (al-tarikh al-muqaddam) is derived from these texts and applied via qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۹) وان اقام الخارج البينة على ملك مؤرّخ واقام صاحب اليد البينة على حديث گزر چکی ہے۔ عن ابی موسى الاشعری ... ان رجلين ادعيا بعيرا على عهد النبي الله فبعث كل واحد منهما شاهدين فقسمه النبي الا الله بينهما نصفين (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الرجلین یدعیان شیعا ولیس بینھما بیته ص ۱۵۳ نمبر ۳۶۱۵) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ تربیج کی کوئی علامت نہ ہو تو دونوں کو آدھا آدھا ملے گا۔ [۲۷۵۸) (۲۹) اگر بعینہ قائم کیا خارج نے ملک مورخ پر اور قبضے والے نے بعینہ قائم کیا ملکیت پر جو تاریخ کے اعتبار سے مقدم ہے تو وہ اولی ہو گا۔ جس آدمی کے قبضے میں چیز نہیں تھی جس کو خارج کہتے ہیں اس نے اپنی ملکیت ہونے پر بینہ قائم کیا اور ایسی تاریخ بیان کی جو بعد میں ملکیت کا ثبوت ہوتا ہے۔ اور جس کے قبضے میں چیز تھی جس کو ذی الید کہتے ہیں اس نے ایسی تاریخ میں اپنی ملکیت ہونے پر بینہ پیش کیا جو اس سے پہلے تھی تو ایسی صورت میں قبضے والے اور ذی الید کے بعینہ کو ترجیح ہوگی۔ یوں ذی الید اور قبضے والا ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ مدعی علیہ ہے۔ اس لئے اس پر قسم کھا نالازم ہے اس کے بینہ کا اعتبار نہیں ہونا چاہئے ۔ خارج کے بینہ کا اعتبار ہونا چاہئے کیونکہ وہ مدعی ہے۔ لیکن اس عام قاعدے کے علاوہ اس مسئلے میں یہ نکتہ کار فرما ہے کہ قبضے والی کی تاریخ مقدم ہے اس لئے بہت ممکن ہے کہ اس کی ملکیت پہلے ہوئی ہو۔ اور جب اس کی ملکیت کے وقت دوسرے کی ملکیت مزاحم نہیں ہے تو پہلی تاریخ والے کی گواہی کو ترجیح ہوگی اور اسی کے لئے اس چیز کا فیصلہ ہوگا (۲) او پر یہ حدیث گزری۔ عن سمرة بن جندب عن النبي الا الله قال بيع البيع من رجلين فالبيع للاول (ب) (ابن ماجہ شریف، نمبر ۲۳۴۴) (۳) ایک حدیث میں ہے کہ اگر کسی کی ملکیت نہیں ہے یا ہے لیکن کسی کا ہاتھ وہاں تک پہلے پہنچا تو وہ چیز اس کی ہوگی۔ یہاں دوسرے والے کا ہاتھ بعد میں پہنچا ہے اور پہلے والے کا ہاتھ پہلے پہنچا ہے اس لئے پہلی تاریخ والے کا بینہ مقبول ہوگا۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن اسمر بن مضرس عن النبي له من سبق الى مالم يسبقه اليه مسلم فهو له يريد به احياء الموات (ج) (سنن البیہقی، باب القاضي يقدم الناس الاول فالاول الخ ج عاشر ص ۲۳۵ نمبر ۲۰۴۸۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ جس کی ملکیت پہلے ہوئی اسی کا فیصلہ ہوگا (۴) ایک نکتہ یہ بھی ہے ملکیت ہونے کی کوئی اور واضح علامت موجود ہو تو قاضی اس علامت پر غور کرے اور اس کے ذریعہ اصل مالک کے۔ لیہ فیصلہ کرے۔ ایک حدیث میں اس کی نشاندہی کی گئی ہے۔ فبعث حذيفة يقضى بينهم فقضى للذين يليهم القمط فلما رجع الى النبي الله اخبره فقال اصبت و احسنت (و) (ابن ماجه شریف، باب الرجلان یدعیان فی خص ص ۳۳۵ نمبر ۲۳۴۳) اس حدیث میں حضرت حذیفہ نے جھونپڑے کا فیصلہ اس آدمی کے لئے کیا جس کے اونٹ کی قطار بندھی ہوئی تھی۔ قریب میں اونٹ باندھنا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ یہ جھونپڑی اسی کی ہوگی۔ اسی طرح مقدم تاریخ ہونا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ اس کی ملکیت پہلے ہے۔ اس لئے مدعی علیہ ہونے کے باوجود قبضے والے کا بینہ مقبول ہوگا۔
(29) And if the outsider presents evidence of ownership with a date, and the possessor presents evidence of a recent [ownership]... [Hadith already mentioned] From Abu Musa al-Ash'ari... that two men claimed a camel in the time of the Prophet, and each brought two witnesses, so the Prophet divided it between them in halves. (Abu Dawud, chapter on two men claiming something without evidence between them, p.153 no.3615) This hadith shows that when there is no sign of preference, both get half. [2758] (29) If the outsider establishes evidence of dated ownership and the possessor establishes evidence of ownership that is earlier in date, then the latter is more entitled. The person not in possession (the outsider) presented evidence of his ownership with a later date, while the person in possession (dhu al-yad) presented evidence of his ownership with an earlier date. In such a case, the evidence of the possessor is given preference. Normally, the possessor is the defendant and should take an oath, and his evidence should not be accepted; rather the outsider's evidence should be accepted because he is the plaintiff. But besides this general rule, in this issue the operative point is that the possessor's date is earlier, so it is very likely that his ownership came first. And when at the time of his ownership, the other's ownership did not conflict, then the earlier dated testimony is given preference, and judgment will be for him. (2) The hadith has passed earlier: from Samurah ibn Jundub from the Prophet: 'When a sale is made to two men, the sale is for the first.' (Ibn Majah, no.2344) (3) Another hadith: 'Whoever precedes to something that no Muslim has preceded him to, it is for him.' He meant reviving dead land. (Al-Bayhaqi, chapter on judge prioritizing the first, vol.10 p.235 no.20484) This hadith indicates that whoever's ownership came first gets the judgment. (4) Another point: if there is any other clear sign of ownership, the judge should consider that sign and decide in favor of the real owner. A hadith indicates this: Hudhayfah was sent to judge between them, and he judged for those near the rope. When he returned to the Prophet and informed him, he said 'You did right and good.' (Ibn Majah, chapter on two men claiming a hut, p.335 no.2343) In this hadith, Hudhayfah decided the hut for the man whose camel was tied nearby. Tying the camel nearby is evidence that the hut belongs to him. Similarly, having an earlier date is evidence that ownership is earlier. Therefore, despite being the defendant, the evidence of the possessor is accepted.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage deals with a situation where the outsider (claimant not in possession) presents evidence of ownership with a later date, while the occupant (defendant in possession) presents evidence of ownership with an earlier date. In Hanafi law, normally the burden of proof is on the plaintiff (the outsider), and the defendant's evidence is not considered unless he takes an oath. However, here a special rule applies: because the occupant's evidence is earlier, it indicates that his ownership predates the outsider's claim. Therefore, the earlier date gives preference, and the occupant's evidence is accepted. This is supported by the hadith 'the sale is for the first' and the hadith about preceding to unowned land. Additionally, the author mentions that judges can consider other signs of ownership, such as the example of Hudhayfah deciding a hut based on the proximity of tied camels. Thus, the general principle of plaintiff having the burden can be overridden by earlier evidence from the defendant.

Key Terms
صاحب اليد (sahib al-yad): The person in possession of the disputed property; also called dhu al-yad. In claims, he is the defendant.
تاريخ مقدم (tarikh muqaddam): Earlier date; the date that comes first chronologically, giving priority in evidence.
خارج (kharaj): Outsider; the person not in possession who claims ownership (the plaintiff).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an outsider claims ownership with evidence dated 2023, and the occupant claims ownership with evidence dated 2020, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The outsider wins because he is the plaintiff.
B) The occupant wins because his evidence is earlier.
C) The property is divided equally.
D) The outsider must take an oath to win.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the earlier date of evidence takes precedence over the plaintiff-defendant distinction in property disputes.

Preferring the Possessor's Evidence with an Earlier Date
Ruling and proof for preferring possessor's evidence when his date is earlier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exception to the general rule of evidence in property disputes when the possessor's evidence has an earlier date, and identify the hadith proofs that support this ruling.

Core Idea
When the possessor's evidence shows an earlier date of ownership, it is preferred over the external claimant's.

In Hanafi fiqh, the general rule is that the plaintiff (external claimant) bears the burden of proof, and the defendant (possessor) merely takes an oath. However, this passage presents an exception: if the possessor can produce evidence of ownership with a date earlier than the claimant's, his evidence is given preference. This ruling is based on the principle that an earlier date indicates prior ownership, supported by several ahadith. In this lesson, we will analyse the reasoning and the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recall that in property disputes, the external claimant (khaarij) is the plaintiff and his evidence is normally accepted, while the possessor (dhu al-yad) is the defendant who only takes an oath.
2
Recognize the exception
Note the passage states: when the possessor's evidence has an earlier date, it is preferred over the external claimant's evidence, even though the possessor is the defendant.
The earlier date acts as a predominant sign (qareenah raajihah) of prior ownership.
3
Examine the rationale
Understand that the earlier date indicates that the possessor's ownership occurred before the other's, so there is no conflict at the time of his ownership. This overrides the default rule.
4
Analyze the hadith proofs
Review the ahadith cited: (1) the hadith of equal division when no preference (Abu Dawud), (2) 'the sale is for the first' (Ibn Majah), (3) 'whoever gets to something first' (al-Bayhaqi), and (4) the hadith of Hudhayfah using external signs. Note how each supports giving preference based on a prior claim or sign.
The hadith of equal division implies that when a sign exists (like an earlier date), it should be used to give preference.
5
Apply the rule
Conclude that a Hanafi judge should prefer the possessor's evidence when it bears an earlier date, and decide the property in his favour.
If no sign exists, the general rule of dividing equally or accepting the plaintiff's evidence applies.

If the external claimant brings evidence of ownership with a date, and the possessor brings evidence of a hadith that has passed... from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari (RA) that two men claimed a camel in the time of the Prophet (SAW), and each of them produced two witnesses, so the Prophet (SAW) divided it between them in half. From this hadith it is understood that when there is no sign of preference, both get half. [2758] (29) If the external claimant produces evidence of ownership with a later date, and the possessor produces evidence of ownership with an earlier date, then the possessor is preferred. The person who does not have possession, called the external claimant, produces evidence of his ownership with a date that shows later ownership. And the one in possession, called the possessor, produces evidence of his ownership with an earlier date. In such a case, the evidence of the possessor is given preference. Thus, because the possessor is the defendant, he should take an oath, and his evidence should not be accepted; the external claimant's evidence should be accepted because he is the plaintiff. But aside from this general rule, the operative point in this issue is that the possessor's date is earlier, so it is likely that his ownership occurred first. And when his ownership is not in conflict with the other's, the evidence of the one with the earlier date is preferred, and the thing is decided in his favor. (2) This hadith has passed: from Samurah ibn Jundub from the Prophet (SAW): 'If an item is sold to two men, the sale is for the first.' (Ibn Majah, no. 2344) (3) Another hadith: If someone's ownership is not established or his hand reached first, then the thing is his. Here the second person's hand reached later, and the first person's hand reached earlier, so the evidence of the one with the earlier date is accepted. The hadith: from 'Ali Allah al-Bayhaqi? from Asmar ibn Mudarris from the Prophet (SAW): 'Whoever gets to something before another Muslim, it is his' – referring to reviving dead land. (Sunan, chapter...). This hadith indicates that the one whose ownership occurred first gets the decision. (4) Another point: If there is any clear sign of ownership, the judge should consider it and decide for the true owner. A hadith indicates this: Hudhayfah was sent to judge between them, and he decided for those next to whom the rope was tied. When he returned to the Prophet (SAW) and informed him, he said: 'You are correct and did well.' (Ibn Majah, no. 2343). In this hadith, Hudhayfah decided the hut for the man whose camel line was tied. The camel tied nearby indicates that the hut is his. Similarly, having an earlier date is a sign that his ownership is earlier. Therefore, despite being the defendant, his witness is given preference. The principle is that if there is a predominant sign for someone, his witness is given preference. Property with an earlier date is preferred.
Footnote: (a) Two men claimed a camel in the time of the Prophet (SAW). Each produced his witnesses, so the Prophet (SAW) divided it equally between them. (b) He said: If an item is sold to two men, the sale is for the first. (c) It is reported from him: Whoever goes first to something that no Muslim has preceded him to, it is his. This refers to reviving dead land. (d) A group brought a dispute about a hut to the Prophet (SAW), so he sent Hudhayfah to judge between them. He decided for the one next to whose hut the camel line was tied. When he returned and informed the Prophet (SAW), he said: 'You did right and did well.'
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from hadith. It addresses a specific scenario in property disputes where both the external claimant (khaarij) and the possessor (dhu al-yad) produce evidence of ownership with dates. The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that the external claimant is the plaintiff (mudda'i) and the possessor is the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh). Usually, the plaintiff's evidence is accepted, and the defendant is only required to take an oath. However, in this case, the possessor's evidence is given preference because his date of ownership is earlier. The reasoning is that the earlier date indicates prior ownership, which is a strong indicator (dalil raajih). This exception is supported by multiple ahadith, including the hadith of Samurah that 'the sale is for the first' and the hadith of Asmar that 'whoever gets to something first deserves it'. The passage also mentions the principle that clear signs (qara'in) can override the default rules of evidence. The judge should consider such signs to arrive at the truth.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically the testimony of two witnesses or equivalent proof in legal claims.
خارج (khaarij): The external claimant: a person who claims ownership of something in another's possession without having possession themselves.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The possessor: the person who currently holds the property in dispute.

In Hanafi fiqh, when the possessor's evidence of ownership has an earlier date than the external claimant's, his evidence is preferred, as the earlier date is a predominant sign of prior ownership.

Preference of Possessor's Evidence in Birth Claims
Ruling and proof for preferring possessor's evidence when both claim birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both claimant and possessor present evidence of birth, and apply the hadith proof to similar cases.

Core Idea
When both parties claim an animal was born in their possession, the possessor's evidence prevails.

In disputes over ownership of livestock, birth is a strong indicator. A unique scenario arises when both the external claimant and the possessor bring evidence that the animal was born in their possession. While general principles would favour the plaintiff's evidence, the Hanafi school, based on a specific hadith, gives priority to the possessor. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
Two parties: the external claimant (plaintiff) and the possessor (defendant). Each claims the animal was born in their possession.
Birth (nataj) is a non-recurring origin, making it a strong indicator.
2
State the general principle
Normally, the plaintiff's evidence is accepted, because he is the claimant and the defendant is under oath.
3
Recognize the exception
When both present evidence of birth, the possessor is preferred over the plaintiff, contrary to the normal rule.
The exception is based on a specific hadith.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The Prophet (SAW) ruled in favor of the possessor when both claimed birth of a she-camel, as narrated by Jabir (RA).
Hadith from Sunan al-Bayhaqi.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, the judge accepts the possessor's evidence and decides the animal remains with the possessor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۵۹] (۳۰) وان اقام الخارج وصاحب اليد كل واحد منهما بينة بالنتاج فصاحب اليد اولی

[۲۷۵۹) (۳۰) اگر قائم کیا غیر قابض اور قابض میں سے ہر ایک نے بینہ پیدائش پر تو قبضہ والا زیادہ بہتر ہوگا۔ مثلا غلام پر ایک آدمی کا قبضہ تھا اور دوسرے آدمی کا قبضہ نہیں تھا۔ دونوں نے اس بات پر بینہ پیش کیا کہ میرے یہاں یہ غلام پیدا ہوا ہے تو جس کا قبضہ ہے اس کے گواہ کو تر جیح ہوگی۔ یہاں بھی قبضہ والا مدعی علیہ ہے اور جس کا قبضہ نہیں ہے وہ مدعی ہے اس لئے مدعی کے گواہ کو مانتا چاہئے ۔ کیونکہ قبضہ والا مدعی علیہ ہے اس لئے اس کے گواہ کا اعتبار نہیں ہونا چاہئے کیونکہ اس پر قسم ہے۔ لیکن یہاں ایک دوسری علامت ہے اس کے گھر غلام کا پیدا ہونا جس کی وجہ سے قبضے والے کے گوداہ کو ترجیح دی جائے گی (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن جابر ان رجلين اختصما إلى النبي الله في ناقة فقال كل واحد منهما نتجت هذه الناقة عندى واقام بينة فقضى بها رسول الله الا الله للذي هي في يديه (الف) (سن للبيبقى ، باب المتد امین بیت زعان شیئا فی ید احدهماج عاشر ص ۴۳۳ نمبر ۲۱۲۲۴) اس حدیث میں اونٹنی کی پیدائش کا دعوی ہے اس لئے جس کا قبضہ تھا آپ نے اسی کے لئے اونٹنی کا فیصلہ فرمایا۔ النتاج : پیدا ہونا۔

حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت جابر سے منقول ہے کہ دو آدمی حضور کے پاس اونٹنی کے بارے میں جھگڑا لے گئے ۔ پس ان میں سے ہر ایک نے کہا یہ اونٹنی میرے پاس پیدا ہوئی ہے اور بعینہ قائم کیا تو حضور نے اس کے لئے فیصلہ فرمایا جو اس کے قبضے میں تھی۔
[2759] (30) And if the external claimant and the possessor each bring evidence of birth (nataj), then the possessor is better.
[2759] (30) If both the non-possessor and the possessor each bring evidence of birth, then the possessor is more entitled. For example, a slave was in the possession of one man, and another man did not have possession. Both brought evidence that this slave was born in their place. Then the witness of the one in possession is given preference. Here too, the possessor is the defendant and the one without possession is the plaintiff, so the plaintiff's witness should be accepted. Because the possessor is the defendant, his witness should not be accepted because he is under oath. But here there is another sign: the birth of the slave in his house, due to which the possessor's witness is given preference. (2) There is a hadith: from Jabir (RA) that two men brought a dispute to the Prophet (SAW) about a she-camel. Each of them said: 'This she-camel was born in my place' and brought evidence. So the Messenger of Allah (SAW) decided it for the one in whose hand it was. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, no. 21224). In this hadith, the claim is about the birth of the she-camel, so the Prophet decisively gave it to the one who had possession. An-nataj: birth.
Footnote: (a) It is narrated from Jabir (RA) that two men came to the Prophet (SAW) disputing about a she-camel. Each said: 'This she-camel was born in my place' and produced evidence. So the Prophet (SAW) decided it for the one in whose possession it was.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that when both the external claimant and the possessor claim that the animal was born in their possession, the possessor's evidence is given preference. The general principle would require the plaintiff's evidence to be accepted, but here the possessor's possession combined with the claim of birth provides a strong indication (qarina) that he is the true owner. The hadith of Jabir directly supports this: the Prophet (SAW) decided in favor of the possessor when both claimed birth. This ruling is specific to cases of birth (nataj) and other non-recurring causes of ownership.

Key Terms
نتاج (nataj): Birth (of an animal). In legal context, it refers to a claim that the animal was born in one's possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when both an external claimant and the possessor bring evidence that an animal was born in their possession, who is preferred?
A) The external claimant, because he is the plaintiff
B) The possessor, because his possession and claim of birth are a strong indication
C) Both are equal and the animal is divided
D) The judge decides based on the number of witnesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, when both parties claim birth, the possessor's evidence is preferred due to the hadith of Jabir.

Preference for Non-Recurring Causes of Ownership
Extending birth ruling to weaving via qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that evidence of a non-recurring cause of ownership, combined with possession, gives preference to the possessor's witness over an external claimant.

Core Idea
For causes that happen only once, possession plus proof of that cause makes the possessor's case stronger.

In disputes over ownership, the Hanafi school gives preference to the possessor when the cause of ownership is non-recurring, such as birth or weaving. This lesson extends the ruling from the birth of an animal to the weaving of a cloth, establishing a general principle for all non-recurring causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause of ownership
Determine whether the event that creates ownership is one that can only happen once (e.g., birth, weaving, building) or can be repeated (e.g., purchase, gift).
2
Apply the preference rule
If the cause is non-recurring, the possessor's evidence is given priority over an external claimant's, even though the possessor is the defendant.
The hadith from issue 30 supports this.
3
Distinguish from recurring causes
For recurring causes, the normal rules of evidence apply without this special preference; the plaintiff must provide stronger proof.
4
Analogize to other cases
Use qiyas to apply the same ruling to other non-recurring causes like building a house or writing a book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۷۶۰ (۳۱) و کذلک النسج في الثياب التي لا تنسج الا مرة واحدة وكذلك كل سبب فى الملك لا يتكرر.

[۲۷۶۰ (۳۱) اسی طرح کپڑوں میں بناوٹ ہے جو نہیں بنا جاتا مگر ایک مرتبہ۔ اور ایسے ہی ملک میں ہر وہ سبب جو مکرر نہیں ہوتا۔ جس طرح کوئی جانور ایک ہی مرتبہ پیدا ہوتا ہے اب اس کا دعوی کرے تو ذی الید کے گواہ کا اعتبار ہے اسی طرح ایسے سبب کا دعوی کرے کہ وہ ایک ہی مرتبہ ہو سکتا ہے مثلا کپڑا ایک ہی مرتبہ بنا جاتا ہے۔ اب قبضہ والا دعوی کرے کہ یہ کپڑا میرا ہے کیونکہ اس کو میں نے بنا ہے اور اس پر بینہ قائم کرے تو قبضے والے کا گواہ قابل ترجیح ہو گا اور جس کے قبضے میں کپڑا نہیں ہے اس کا گواہ قابل ترجیح نہیں ہوگا۔ بنا ہونا اور پھر اس کے قبضے میں ہونا اس بات کی علامت راجہ ہے کہ وہ کپڑے کا پہلا مالک ہے۔ حالانکہ یہ مدعی علیہ ہے، اس پر قسم ہے اس پر گواہ نہیں ہے۔ پھر بھی اسی کے گواہ کو ترجیح ہوگی (۳) حدیث مسئلہ نمبر ۳۰ میں گزرگئی ۔ النسج : کپڑا بننا۔
[2760] (31) And similarly, weaving in clothes that are woven only once, and likewise every cause in ownership that does not recur.
[2760] (31) Similarly, weaving in clothes that are woven only once. And likewise every cause of ownership that is not repeated. Just as an animal is born only once, so when a claim is made, the witness of the possessor is accepted. Similarly, if the cause is such that it can occur only once, like a cloth is woven only once, then if the possessor claims that this cloth is mine because I wove it, and he brings evidence, then the witness of the possessor is preferred, and the witness of the one who does not have possession is not preferred. The fact that it was woven and then is in his possession is a strong sign that he is the first owner of the cloth. Even though he is the defendant and is under oath, his witness is still given preference. (3) The hadith in issue no. 30 has already passed. An-nasj: weaving.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage extends the ruling from the previous issue (birth) to other non-recurring causes of ownership, specifically using the example of weaving a cloth. Since a cloth is typically woven only once, if the possessor claims to have woven it and brings evidence, his evidence is given preference over an external claimant's. The rationale is that possession plus a non-recurring origin provides a strong indication that he is the original owner. This is analogical extension (qiyas) from the case of birth. The same principle applies to any cause that cannot be repeated, such as building a house or writing a book. The hadith from the previous issue is cited as the supporting proof.

Key Terms
نسج (nasj): Weaving. In legal context, it represents a non-recurring cause of ownership.
سبب لا يتكرر (sabab la yatakarrar): A non-recurring cause: an event that can only happen once to create ownership, such as birth, weaving, building, etc.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives evidentiary preference to the possessor when the cause of ownership is non-recurring, extending the principle beyond birth to all similar causes.

Two Witnesses and Four Witnesses Equal in Weight
Ruling that multiplicity of witnesses does not give preference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that multiplicity of witnesses does not create preference and cite the evidence from the practice of the Companions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, two witnesses suffice; extra witnesses merely confirm, they do not outweigh.

This lesson addresses a common misconception: that more witnesses strengthen a case. The Hanafi school, based on the practice of the early Muslim judges, holds that once the required number of witnesses (two) is met, additional witnesses do not tip the scale. We will examine the ruling and its proof from the letter of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Udhayna to Shuraih and a narration from Imam Ali.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
Consider two claimants presenting witnesses: one brings two, the other brings four. What is the legal question?
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The ruling is that both sets of witnesses are equal; no preference is given by number.
3
Examine the First Proof
Read the athar from Shuraih: in a dispute over an animal, the judge wrote that multiplicity is irrelevant; the animal belongs to the one in possession who proves their claim.
4
Examine the Second Proof
The narration from Imam Ali states explicitly that preference is not given by multiplicity of number.
5
Understand the Legal Reasoning
Why does the school not consider multiplicity? Because once the minimum requirement (two witnesses) is met, additional witnesses serve only as confirmation (tawthiq), not as a basis for preference.

Source Text

And if one of the two claimants presents two witnesses and the other presents four, then they are equal. (34) If two claimants, one brought two witnesses and the other four, then the ranks of both are equal. Two witnesses are complete; afterwards any additional witnesses are only for confirmation. In terms of being original, there is no consideration. So one presented two witnesses and the other [claimant?] presented four witnesses, then the extra two witnesses have no effect in the decision; both witnesses' ranks are equal. There is an athar: 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Udhayna wrote to Shuraih concerning people of Azd who claimed against people of Bani Asad. He said: When these people depart with their evidence, those people come with more. He (Shuraih) wrote to him: This is nothing from confusion and multiplicity; the animal belongs to whoever has it in their possession if they establish evidence.' And in another narration: 'From Ali, that preference is not given by multiplicity of number.' (Source: Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter: He who said preference is not given by multiplicity of number, volume 10, page 434, number 21227). From this athar it is known that after two witnesses, consideration of multiplicity and paucity of number is not valid.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Hanafi fiqh regarding evidence in court: when two claimants each present different numbers of witnesses, the court does not give preference based on the number of witnesses. The ruling is that two witnesses from one side and four from the other are equal in evidentiary weight; the extra witnesses merely support but do not tip the scale. The commentator provides proof from the practice of the Companions: the letter of Abd al-Rahman ibn Udhayna to the judge Shuraih, and a report from Imam Ali. These establish the principle that in judicial testimony, once the minimum required number (two) is met, additional witnesses do not create superiority. The passage type is a ruling with proof; the commentator expands the base ruling with these athar to justify the position.

بینہ (bayyinah): Proof, usually referring to two witnesses or other evidence.
تهاتر (tahatur): Confusion or nullification of claims due to equal evidence.
