The Personal Guarantor in Court: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the duration of personal guarantor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure when a claimant has witnesses present, distinguish between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the duration of the guarantor, and explain the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
When witnesses are claimed present, the defendant must give a personal guarantor — but for how long? Abu Hanifa gives three days for residents, the Sahibayn only the court session.

When a claimant asserts that his witnesses are available but not in court, the defendant cannot simply be left to flee. The Hanafi school prescribes a personal guarantor (kafil bin nafs) to secure the defendant’s presence while the witnesses are summoned. However, there is a well-known intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Abu Hanifa grants three days for a resident defendant, while his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), restrict the period to the duration of the court session for all defendants. In this lesson, we unpack the rationale and practical application of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering scenario
The claimant states 'I have witnesses present' (عندي بينة حاضرة), meaning they are ready but not physically in court. The defendant is not to be released without guarantee.
This is not a case where the claimant has no proof; it is a promise of immediate evidence.
2
Determine the default ruling for a resident defendant
The qadi orders the defendant to provide a personal guarantor (كفيل بالنفس) for three days. If he cannot, the claimant or his agent must physically surveil the defendant.
Three days: one to fetch witnesses, one to return, one to present.
3
Apply the exception for a traveler defendant
If the defendant is a traveler passing through, the guarantor is only required for the duration of that court session (مجلس القضاء), typically a few hours.
To avoid hardship on the traveler, the period is shortened.
4
Introduce the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the period should be the same for all defendants: only the court session. They do not differentiate between resident and traveler.
Their reasoning: delay is undesirable; if witnesses are truly present, they should be brought quickly.
5
Assess the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, a resident defendant is bound for up to three days; under the Sahibayn, only a few hours. This impacts the risk of flight and the feasibility of summoning distant witnesses.
The mufta bihi position is not settled in the text; both views are known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قال المدعى لى بينة حاضرة قيل لخصمه اعطه كفيلا بنفسك ثلثة ايام فان فعل والا امر بحفظه الا ان يكون مسافرا على الطريق فيحفظ بقدر مجلس القضاء [۲۷۶۵] (۳۶) اگر مدعی نے کہا میرابینہ حاضر ہے تو اس کے خصم سے کہا جائے گا کہ اس کو تین دن تک کے لئے کفیل بالنفس دیں۔ پس اگر ایسا کر لیا تو ٹھیک ہے ورنہ حکم دیا جائے گا اس کی نگرانی کرنے کا مگر یہ کہ راستے کا مسافر ہے تو اس کی نگرانی مجلس قضا کی مقدار کرے۔ اصول تو یہ ہے کہ مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہ ہو تو مدعی علیہ کو تم کھلائے اور وہ قسم کھا جائے تو اس کے حق میں فیصلہ کر دے لیکن یہاں گواہ ہے۔ لیکن شہر میں ہے اس لئے لانے میں دیر ہوگی۔ اس لئے صرف دعوی سے مدعی علیہ کو کفیل بالنفس دینے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا تا کہ گواہ لاتے لاتے مدعی علیہ کہیں بھاگ نہ جائے۔ اس لئے تین دن تک کے لئے کفیل بالنفس دے تا کہ مدعی علیہ کہیں چلے جائے تو کفیل اس کو تلاش کر کے مجلس قضا میں حاضر کرے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک تین دن تک اس لئے ہے کہ ایک دن گواہ لانے جائے ، دوسرا دن واپس آئے اور تیسرا دن گواہ پیش کرے۔ آج کل تو کئی کئی سال تک لوگ ضمانت پر چھوٹتے ہیں۔ اگر موجود لوگوں میں سے کوئی آدمی مدعی علیہ کے لئے کفیل بننے کے لئے تیار نہ ہوں تو مدعی سے کہا جائے گا کہ آپ یا آپ کا امین مدعی علیہ کی نگرانی کرتے رہیں اور جدھر وہ جائے آپ بھی پیچھے پیچھے جائیں۔ اور اگر مدعی علیہ مسافر ہو تو صرف مجلس قضا جو پانچ چھ گھنٹے جاری رہتی ہے وہاں تک کفیل بالنفس لے۔ اور کفیل نہ ملے تواتنی دیر تک مدعی نگرانی کرتار ہے۔ کیونکہ اس سے زیادہ دیر تک مقدمہ اور گواہ کا انتظار کرے گا تو مسافر کو نقصان ہوگا۔ اس لئے مجلس قضا تک ہی مدعی کو مہلت دی جائے گی۔
صاحبین کے نزدیک مدعی علیہ مسافر ہو یا مقیم ہر حال میں مجلس قضا تک ہی مدعی کو گواہ لانے کی مہلت دی جائے گی۔ کفیل بالنفس کی دلیل کتاب الکفالہ میں گزر چکی ہے وہ یہ ہے۔ سمعت ابا امامة قال سمعت رسول الله ... ثم قال
And when the claimant says 'I have witnesses present,' it is said to his opponent: Give him a personal guarantor for three days. If he does, fine; otherwise, an order is given to keep him under surveillance, unless he is a traveler on the road, then he is kept under surveillance for the duration of the court session. (36) If the claimant says 'my witnesses are present,' then the opponent is told to give a personal guarantor for three days. If he does, fine; otherwise, an order is given to keep him under surveillance, unless he is a traveler, then his surveillance is for the duration of the court session. The principle is that if the claimant has no witnesses, the defendant is made to take an oath, and if he takes it, judgment is given in his favor. But here there are witnesses, but they are in the city, so bringing them will take time. So based solely on the claim, the defendant is compelled to provide a personal guarantor so that while the claimant brings witnesses, the defendant does not flee. So a personal guarantor for three days is given so that if the defendant goes away, the guarantor can search for him and present him in court. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, three days are given because one day to fetch witnesses, second day to return, and third day to present them. Nowadays, people are released on surety for many years. If no one present is willing to be a guarantor for the defendant, then the claimant is told: You or your trustee keep the defendant under surveillance, and wherever he goes, you follow behind. If the defendant is a traveler, then only for the duration of the court session, which lasts five or six hours, a personal guarantor is taken. If no guarantor is found, the claimant maintains surveillance for that time. Because if he would wait longer for the case and witnesses, the traveler would be harmed. So the claimant is given time only until the court session. According to the Sahibayn, whether the defendant is a traveler or a resident, in all cases the claimant is given time only to the extent of the court session to bring witnesses. The proof for personal guarantor passed in the Book of Kafalah: I heard Abu Umamah say: I heard the Messenger of Allah... then he said
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage explains the procedure when a claimant states that his witnesses are present but not in court. The opponent (defendant) is required to provide a personal guarantor (kafil bin nafs) for three days to ensure he does not flee while the witnesses are being summoned. If no guarantor is available, the claimant or his trustee must physically keep the defendant under surveillance. For a traveler defendant, the period is shortened to the duration of the current court session to avoid inconvenience. An intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded: Imam Abu Hanifa gives three days based on the typical time needed to bring witnesses (one day to go, one to return, one to present). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that regardless of the defendant's status, only the duration of the court session should be allowed. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute with practical details on implementation.

Key Terms
کفیل بالنفس (kafil bin nafs): A personal guarantor who ensures the defendant's presence in court and can be held responsible if the defendant absconds.
مجلس قضاء (majlis al-qada): The court session, typically lasting a few hours.
صاحبین (al-Sahibayn): The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the leading students of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When a claimant claims witnesses are present, the defendant must provide a personal guarantor; the duration depends on the school's variation — Abu Hanifa gives three days for residents, while the Sahibayn limit it to the court session.

Effect of Refusing Oath in Qisas: Sahibayn vs. Abu Hanifa
Intra-Hanafi ruling on refusal of oath in qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal consequence of a defendant's refusal to swear an oath in a qisas case according to the Sahibayn, and distinguish their position from that of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Refusing an oath in qisas creates shubha, dropping qisas but requiring diyah because property can be established despite doubt.

This passage addresses a procedural situation in qisas cases: the defendant is asked to swear that he is not liable, but he refuses. The Hanafi school interprets this refusal as a tacit confession, but due to shubha, the severe penalty of qisas is not imposed. Instead, compensation (diyah/arsh) is required. Within the school, the Sahibayn and Imam Abu Hanifa differ on whether the same reasoning applies to limb injuries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural context
The defendant in a qisas claim is asked to take an oath denying liability. He refuses. The question is: what is the legal effect of this refusal?
The refusal is not silence; it is a nukul (refusal to swear).
2
Understand tacit confession and shubha
The refusal is interpreted as tacit confession (iqrar bi dalalat al-hal). However, tacit confession carries doubt (shubha) because it is not explicit. In Hanafi law, shubha prevents qisas.
Shubha is any doubt that would mitigate a severe penalty.
3
Apply the shubha principle to qisas
Since qisas is dropped by any shubha, and tacit confession has shubha, qisas cannot be established. Instead, diyah (blood money) is due because it is property, and property can be established even with shubha.
This is the Sahibayn's reasoning.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on limbs
Imam Abu Hanifa considers a limb to be in the category of property in some respects. Therefore, for limb injuries, he might allow qisas even with shubha, whereas the Sahibayn treat limbs like life: qisas dropped, but arsh (compensation) due as property.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether qisas for a limb is possible.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn: diyah/arsh is due for both life and limb. The text elaborates their reasoning, suggesting it is the preferred view.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

تى يقر او يحلف وقال ابو يوسف و محمد رحمهما الله تعالى يلزمه الارش فيهما [۲۷۶۵ (۳۶) واذا قال المدعى لى بينة حاضرة قيل لخصمه اعطه كفيلا بنفسك ثلثة تو نہیں ہے لیکن چلو میری جان ماردو ۔ اس لئے جان کے قصاص کے بارے میں قسم کھلائے اور قسم سے انکار کر جائے تو ابھی قصاص لازم نہیں ہوگا بلکہ مدعی علیہ کو قید کیا جائے گا تا کہ یا قصاص کا اقرار کرے یا قسم کھا کر کہے کہ مجھ پر قصاص نہیں ہے۔ تا کہ اگر اقرار کیا تو قصاص لازم ہوگا اور قسم کھا کر انکار کر دیا تو قصاص ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ ان صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ قصاص چاہے عضو کا ہو چاہے پوری جان کا ہو ہے قصاص ہے۔ اور قصاص شبہ سے ساقط ہو جاتا ہے۔ جب مدعی علیہ نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کیا تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ دبے زبان اقرار کرتا ہوں کہ مجھ پر قصاص ہے۔ اور دبے زبان اقرار کرنے میں شبہ ہے کہ قصاص کا اقرار کرتا ہوں۔ اور شبہ سے نہ جان کا قصاص ثابت ہو گا اور نہ عضو کا قصاص ثابت ہوگا۔ البتہ دبے زبان سے مدعی کی بات کا اقرار کیا ہے اس لئے جان یا عضو کے بدلے دیت لازم ہوگی۔ اور دیت چونکہ مال ہے اس لئے اقرار میں شبہ ہے پھر بھی لازم ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ مال شبہ کے ساتھ بھی ثابت ہو سکتا ہے۔ امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک عضو بعض مقام پر مال کے درجے میں ہے۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک عضو مال کے درجے میں نہیں ہے۔ ت الارش : عضو کی دیت ، عضو کے بدلے مال۔ [۲۷۶۵]
Either he confesses or swears. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: He is liable for the 'arsh (compensation) in both. [2765] (36) And if the plaintiff says, 'I have witnesses present,' it is said to his opponent, 'Give him a guarantor for yourself for three days.' There is no contradiction, but let [؟]. So for qisas of life, he is made to swear, and if he refuses the oath, qisas is not immediately binding, but the defendant is imprisoned until he either confesses to qisas or swears that there is no qisas against him. If he confesses, qisas becomes binding; if he swears denial, qisas is dropped. The Sahibayn say: Qisas, whether for a limb or for full life, is qisas, and qisas is dropped by shubha. When the defendant refuses to take the oath, it means he tacitly confesses that there is qisas against him. And in tacit confession there is shubha that he is confessing to qisas. Due to shubha, neither qisas for life nor for limb is established. However, since he tacitly confirmed the plaintiff's claim, diyah is due in lieu of life or limb. And because diyah is property, even if there is shubha in the confession, it becomes binding, since property can be established with shubha. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, a limb is in the category of property in some respects. According to the Sahibayn, a limb is not in the category of property. Thus al-Arsh: diyah of a limb, property in lieu of a limb.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the case where a defendant in a qisas (retaliation) claim refuses to take an oath after being asked to do so. The defendant's refusal is interpreted as a tacit confession (iqrar bi dalalat al-hal). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that since qisas is dropped by shubha (doubt), the tacit confession carries shubha (because it is not an explicit declaration), therefore qisas cannot be established. Instead, the defendant is liable for diyah (blood money) because diyah is property, and property can be established even with shubha. Imam Abu Hanifa holds a different view: he considers a limb to be in the category of property in some contexts, so for limb injuries, the ruling may differ. The text clarifies that according to the Sahibayn, 'arsh (compensation for a limb) is property, so it is due. The passage then transitions to the next ruling about witnesses.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for murder or bodily harm
شبہ (shubha): A legal doubt or ambiguity that prevents application of severe penalties like qisas and hadd
دیت (diyah): Blood money paid in compensation for homicide or bodily injury instead of qisas
ارش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a limb or body part

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the Sahibayn say that the defendant's refusal to take the oath in a qisas case results in diyah and not qisas?
A) Because the defendant explicitly confessed to qisas
B) Because qisas is dropped by shubha and property can be established with shubha
C) Because the plaintiff withdrew the claim
D) Because the defendant swore later

Key Takeaway
The defendant's refusal to swear in a qisas case results in diyah, not qisas, because shubha prevents qisas but allows property claims.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Surety for Defendant When Plaintiff Claims Witnesses
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on demanding surety for a defendant pending witness attendance, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the duration, and apply the ruling to resident and traveler cases.

Core Idea
A defendant must provide a surety for three days (resident) or only during the court session (traveler) to ensure he does not flee while the plaintiff brings witnesses.

In litigation, a plaintiff may claim to have witnesses but need time to bring them. How does the court ensure the defendant does not abscond? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on demanding a kafeel bil-nafs (surety for the person). We will explore the default rule of three days given by Imam Abu Hanifa, the more restrictive view of the Sahibayn, and the proofs from the Quran and Sunnah that support these positions. Understanding this ruling equips you to handle a common procedural issue in Islamic courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the basic obligation
When the plaintiff says 'I have witnesses present (in the city)', the judge tells the defendant: 'Give the plaintiff a surety for your own person for three days.' If he provides one, fine; if not, the plaintiff is ordered to keep the defendant under surveillance (mulazamah). If the defendant is a traveler, the period is only the duration of the court session.
This is a wajib obligation to safeguard the plaintiff's right to present evidence.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on duration
Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between residents (3 days) and travelers (court session). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that both residents and travelers are only bound until the end of the court session. This difference stems from different weights given to the athar and general principles of avoiding hardship.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: a resident may be given more time to bring witnesses from elsewhere in the city.
3
Examine the proofs (dalil) for the ruling
The author cites three proofs: (1) The hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable) from Abu Dawud, showing that a surety is answerable for whatever he guarantees, including the person. (2) The athar from Bukhari where Hamza took sureties from a man until he came to 'Umar, indicating surety for an extended period. (3) The athar from Bayhaqi where Shuraih detained a person overnight for surety, showing that the period can exceed the court session.
These proofs support Abu Hanifa's position that three days is a reasonable maximum.
4
Apply the ruling to different scenarios
Resident defendant: three days to provide surety. If no surety found, plaintiff keeps him under surveillance for three days. Traveler defendant: only for the duration of the court session (about 5-6 hours). This distinction avoids hardship for the traveler.
The surveillance is done by the plaintiff or his trustworthy agent.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is the time allowed for bringing witnesses. Abu Hanifa's view allows more flexibility for the plaintiff, while the Sahibayn's view protects the defendant from prolonged inconvenience. The text suggests that Abu Hanifa's position is stronger due to the explicit athar, but later practice may follow the Sahibayn for expediency.
In modern courts, the actual duration may be set by the judge based on circumstances.

And if the plaintiff says, 'I have witnesses present,' it is said to his opponent, 'Give him a surety for yourself for three days.' If he does so, fine; otherwise, he is ordered to keep him under surveillance, unless he is a traveler on the road, in which case he keeps him company for the duration of the court session. The basic principle is that if the plaintiff has no witnesses, the defendant is made to swear, and if he swears, judgment is given in his favor. But here there are witnesses, and they are in the city, so bringing them will take time. So merely based on the claim, the defendant is compelled to provide a surety for his person so that while bringing the witnesses, the defendant does not flee. So he gives a surety for three days, so that if the defendant goes somewhere, the surety can find him and bring him to court. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, three days because one day to go bring witnesses, second day to return, third day to present them. Nowadays people are released on surety for many years. If no one among those present volunteers to be a surety for the defendant, the plaintiff is told to keep the defendant under surveillance himself or through his trusted person, and wherever the defendant goes, he follows. And if the defendant is a traveler, then only for the duration of the court session (about 5-6 hours) does he take a surety; if no surety is found, the plaintiff keeps surveillance for that time. Because if he waits longer, it would harm the traveler. So the plaintiff is given only until the end of the court session. According to the Sahibayn, whether the defendant is a traveler or resident, the plaintiff is given only until the end of the court session to bring witnesses. The proof for surety of person has passed in the Book of Suretyship: I heard Abu Umamah say: I heard the Messenger of Allah... then he said: 'The borrowed item is to be returned, the gift is to be restored, the debt is to be repaid, and the guarantor is liable.' (Abu Dawud) In this hadith, 'al-za'im gharim' means the surety is responsible, whether he is a surety for presenting a person or for paying money. (2) In Sahih al-Bukhari, there is an athar about surety of person: from Muhammad ibn Hamza ibn 'Amr al-Aslami from his father that 'Umar sent him as a collector of sadaqah; a man had intercourse with his wife's slave-girl; so Hamza took sureties from the man until he came to 'Umar, and 'Umar had flogged him a hundred lashes, but he confirmed them and excused them due to ignorance. Jarir and al-Ash'ath said to 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud regarding the apostates: 'Ask them to repent and take sureties from them.' So they repented and their families stood surety for them. Hammad said: 'If he gives surety for a person and that person dies, nothing is due from him.' (Bukhari) These athar mention taking surety of person, showing that a surety for presenting a person can be taken. And the proof for giving surety within three days is this athar: I heard Habib, who used to bring litigants to Shuraih, say: A man disputed with a son of Shuraih before Shuraih; he had stood surety for a man who owed a debt, so Shuraih imprisoned him. When night came, he said: 'Go to 'Abdullah with bedding and food.' His son was named 'Abdullah. (Bayhaqi) This athar shows that he was detained for the night for surety. It indicates that a surety can be taken after the court session, and the period according to Imam Abu Hanifa is three days. (2) Also in the Bukhari athar above is the phrase: 'Hamza took sureties from the man until he came to 'Umar.' This shows that he took surety for that long, indicating that the surety is not limited to the court session but can be for a longer period if needed.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario in litigation where the plaintiff claims to have witnesses present in the city. Since witnesses are not immediately available, the defendant must provide a surety (kafeel bil-nafs) to ensure he does not abscond while the witnesses are being brought. The default ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa is that the surety is for three days: one day to go, one to return, and one to present witnesses. If no one volunteers to be surety, the plaintiff himself or his trustee must keep the defendant under surveillance. However, if the defendant is a traveler, the duration is only the length of the court session (about five to six hours) to avoid hardship to the traveler. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold a more restricted view: whether the defendant is a traveler or resident, the plaintiff is only given until the end of the court session to bring witnesses. The text then provides proofs: the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable) from Abu Dawud, which includes suretyship for person; athar from Bukhari where Hamza took sureties until he reached 'Umar; and athar from Bayhaqi where Shuraih detained a person overnight for surety. These show that taking surety for person is valid and that the duration can extend beyond a single court session if necessary. The commentary concludes that according to Abu Hanifa, three days is the maximum, while the Sahibayn limit it to the court session. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) today may vary, but the text provides the classical Hanafi view.

Key Terms
كفيل بالنفس (kafeel bil-nafs): A person who guarantees to produce the defendant (or another person) before the court when required
ملازمته (mulazamah): Ordering the plaintiff to keep the defendant under surveillance or accompaniment to ensure his presence
مجلس القاضي (majlis al-qadi): The session of the judge; the time during which the court is in session

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the maximum duration for which a defendant (who is a resident) can be required to provide a surety when the plaintiff claims to have witnesses?
A) Only the duration of the court session
B) Three days
C) Until the witnesses arrive, no limit
D) One week

Key Takeaway
When a plaintiff claims to have witnesses, the Hanafi school requires the defendant to provide a surety (kafeel bil-nafs) for a specific period—three days for residents (Abu Hanifa) or only the court session (Sahibayn)—to ensure his presence, or else face surveillance by the plaintiff.
